

# **MUNICIPAL GOVERNMENT HISTORY BOULDER, COLORADO 1980 - 1984**

**COMPILED BY  
VIRGINIA BRADDOCK  
FOR  
THE BOULDER PUBLIC LIBRARY FOUNDATION  
INC.  
JULY 1989**



# PREFACE

This pamphlet was made possible through a grant from the Boulder Public Library Foundation. Any errors or mis-statements are the responsibility of the compiler. Sources are primarily the newspaper clippings files of the Municipal Government Reference Center. In most cases, the items were found in the Boulder Daily Camera. The few were taken from the Denver Post, the Longmont Times-Call, or the Colorado Daily. In all direct quotations, the reporter has been cited within the text.

The format of this history is that under each chapter title, the years 1980 through 1984 are in sequence. Within each year are the sub-titles listed in the Table of Contents. If any heading is not found, the subject was not a substantial news item during that year. This format was chosen so the history may be expanded at some future time.

Some departments of the city may not be headliners, but provide invaluable support to all other governmental functions. More information on any department or any subject heading may be found in the files of the Reference Department in the Boulder Public Library.

Printed on

# CHAPTER I

## GOVERNMENT AND ELECTIONS

1980

### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

Ruth Correll was unanimously elected by her fellow council members to a second term as Boulder's mayor. (Camera, January 2, Daryl Gibson)

In January, Gunbarrel annexation proponents collected 176 signatures on a petition calling for an election. In March, the Board of Directors of the Boulder Country Club withdrew their support of the election. The board discovered that its articles of incorporation required a poll of its 700 members and the results were six to one against annexation. Less than half the club members live in Gunbarrel.

Without the Country Club's greens, Gunbarrel does not have the land link to the city needed for annexation. (Camera, March 28, Daryl Gibson)

In the Camera dated May 7, Daryl Gibson reported that the council voted to inform the Country Club's Board of Directors that it cannot legally oppose the annexation drive because in 1966 it accepted city water and sewer services. In the May 24 Camera, she reported that the Country Club's attorney said they had fulfilled their contract with the city by agreeing to an annexation election in 1978. That election had failed.

In July, City Attorney Joe de Raismes told the city council he does not want to take legal action against the Country Club until the Colorado Supreme Court decides a similar controversy involving the Broadmoor Hotel in Colorado Springs. (Camera, July 23, Daryl Gibson) The September 2nd 1980 Denver Post reported that the Supreme Court did not answer the

question of whether the Broadmoor had consented to annexation. They did uphold a lower court ruling that invalidated an annexation by Colorado Springs because it lacked the legal two-thirds of a common border. (In November, the voters approved an amendment to the State Constitution that required majority approval by residents in an area before it could be annexed regardless of how much boundary the area and a city share.)

Citizens Opposed to BURA submitted a petition calling for another election on the Crossroads Mall. The city council sided with the recommendations of the city attorney and refused to schedule the September election. (Camera, August 20, Daryl Gibson)

The mall opponents drafted another petition in October which was rejected by the city attorney on the grounds that it challenged administrative duties of the council rather than the legislative act of forming BURA.

In December, Boulder District Judge William Neighbors ordered the city council to schedule another election on the Crossroads Mall expansion. The Citizens Opposed to BURA had successfully argued that their petition was an initiative to repeal action by the council and not a referendum which has a deadline for filing. Judge Neighbors ordered the council to determine whether the petition has enough signatures and to either repeal the law themselves or send it to the voters.

City Attorney de Raismes told the council that he would probably recommend an appeal of the decision, but that the council would have to consider the length of time an appeal would take versus how long it would take to hold another election on Crossroads. The consensus of the attorney and the city manager was that, barring any instructions from the council to the contrary, BURA would continue to operate whichever decision was made.

(Camera, Dec. 10, Mike Stone)

Jane Cracraft reported in the Denver Post on December 27 that:

The Boulder City Council Friday voted to buy the 475-acre site 5 miles south of Boulder along Colorado 93 that SDC had hoped to use for a new corporate headquarters and 4,000-employee computer plant.

When SDC first came to town, its representatives talked to Boulder officials, who insisted that such a major land-use change would have to be approved by Boulder voters. The company didn't want an election and turned to Boulder County, asking for an amendment to the comprehensive plan. County officials said they don't approve rezonings unless they are near cities where urban services are available.

Two weeks ago SDC approached Superior, Louisville and Broomfield to see if any of them would annex across the county to reach the site south of Boulder.

... Monday, the county commissioners voted 2-1 to ask Boulder, which has money for open space, to buy the land and hold it for an election.

City Manager Bob Westdyke told the City Council that he warned SDC's project manager, Bob Williams, "not to attempt to play one city off against another" and not to try to get around the county's comprehensive plan. Boulder County and Boulder have an intergovernmental agreement to work together to uphold the plan.

On a separate motion calling for a public vote on the future of the land, Councilmen Lefferdink, Meckley and Boland voted "no". Boland said he thinks the City Council should make the decision to keep the land in open space, and an election isn't necessary. He pointed out that Boulder voters decided 15 years ago not to extend water and sewer lines to the Rudd property near Eldorado Springs, about halfway between the present city limits and the SDC site, because they didn't want the city to grow farther south.

Danish said one of the unusual things about Boulder is that the voters have determined many of the major land-use questions, including growth management and the open space program.

In the Denver Post dated January 28, Jane Cracraft reported that Planning Director Frank Gray suggested that the council appoint a broad-based citizen's commission ... to draft a new growth-control ordinance. The "Danish Plan", which expires in two years, became a part of the charter after the 1976 election and could be amended only by another vote of the people. Councilman Paul Danish said he agrees "with Frank that it would be better if the city council adopted a specific (growth) ordinance so it could be amended as needed." He said the council has the power to amend

the merit system portion of the current growth-control plan and may put even more emphasis on lower cost housing and on energy-saving construction techniques. (In May, the council revised the Danish Plan to encourage more energy efficiency in homes.) Gray estimated the cost of staff members to work with the commission might run to \$80,000 over two years. (This item, cut from the budget by City Manager Westdyke, was reinstated by the council in June.)

A 27-member citizens' task force was selected. Based on its recommendations, the council will have to decide whether to let the Danish Plan expire in March 1982, or to make modifications to its basic format, or to retain it in present form. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 16, Don Olsen)

On February 4, Boulder city officials told Colorado Daily staff writer Bob French that the city cannot remove its two percent sales tax on food without upsetting the budget. According to Cappie Fine, Boulder's finance director, eliminating the city sales tax on food would cause a \$1.8 million decrease in city revenues for 1980. City Councilman Greg Lefferdink believes the food tax to be regressive despite the present rebate by the city to residents who qualify. The state eliminated its tax on food beginning this year. Fine said the state could afford the loss of revenue funds. The city is lacking such a surplus, and would have to increase its tax on non-food items to replace any loss from its sales tax on food.

The recession curbed consumer spending in 1980 and sales taxes collected in Boulder were up only 5.9 percent over 1979. (The increase of 1979 sales taxes over 1978 was 15.1 percent). With the contribution to BURA deducted, the increase was only 3.2 percent over 1979. (Camera, February 12, Daryl Gibson)

Jane Cracraft reported in the Denver Post of May 14 that City Manager Bob Westdyke recommended to the council at a study session that a 2.5 percent across-the-board cut in the city's operating budget was necessary. The city instituted a "selective hiring freeze" to help with spending cutbacks. The council agreed to adopt a policy which could eventually build up a five percent reserve fund to cover future recession-lean years. The policy could not bind future councils, but could establish a tradition of having an adequate reserve. (Camera, May 14, Mike Stone)

Daryl Gibson reported in the May 21 Camera that:

The Crossroads Mall expansion was blamed ... for causing budget problems which have forced a 2.5 percent ... cut in City of Boulder programs.

(Councilman George Boland) ... objected to the mall project getting sales taxes from the new Target and LaBelle's stores, which are in the Crossroads area but were built before the mall expansion plan became a reality.

Boulder Urban Renewal Authority Chairman Janet Roberts reminded Boland that ... the council (had) unanimously approved the funding plan for the mall, including the sales tax provisions, last year.

In the Camera of January 19, Daryl Gibson had reported that U. S. Representative Tim Wirth warned the Boulder City Council about a cut in revenue sharing. He said, "We all want the goal of clean air, clean water, safe working conditions, but how much risk-free life are we willing to pay for?" The council reminded Wirth that Boulder had raised sewer rates to help meet federal clean water standards and were still waiting for federal funds to help expand its sewage treatment plant.

The city council approved a \$61 million budget for 1981, authorized a slight property tax increase and boosted nearly every city service fee. The increase of .25 mills was assessed to pay for city forest management programs, including a continuing fight against pine beetles. In addition, the city will have an energy coordinator, a review of the outdated city

code, more library books, and will keep open the George Reynolds branch of the library. The only major project left out of the supplemental appropriations list is the \$700,000 the transportation department wants for a pedestrian underpass beneath Broadway at College Avenue. (Camera, June 5, Daryl Gibson)

The proposed budget was up 7.8 percent from 1980. The dollar figure was distorted by the addition of some one-time projects like the \$23 million sewer plant improvement funded by a federal grant.

One of Boulder's largest government committees, which routinely operates with 40 or more members, will begin organizational steps ... as a prelude to deciding how \$1.1 million in federal grants will be spent.

The committee, which gets new members each year, has the task of advising the Boulder City Council on the use of federal Community Development grants. The 1981 committee will study distribution of grants for 1982.

Membership on the committee is open to as many people as want to join and are willing to stick with it through the year. But predictably, the members generally are representatives of agencies looking for a share of the money.

Committee rules dictate that each private agency can contribute only one member to the committee and that the member cannot vote when that agency's funding application comes up ... . Members who miss more than two meetings in a row are expelled.

The emphasis of the federal program is on housing, upgrading the living conditions of low- and moderate-income people and eradicating blight ... . About 73 percent of the 1981 grants will go to housing projects in Boulder.

The total Community Development allotment to the city has risen from \$245,000 in 1975 to \$1,078,000 already portioned for 1981. Projects funded include the Downtown Mall, the Senior Citizens Center, improvement to the San Juan del Centro low-income housing development, a day-care training center, restoration of the Chautauqua Auditorium, rehabilitation of the Carnegie Library, ECHO House, playgrounds and parks. (Camera, Nov. 18, Daryl Gibson)

Among several resolutions passed during the year supporting the sale of industrial revenue bonds were those requested by Cray Research Inc. and the Ball Corp.

In April, the council approved a resolution which calls for an end to the manufacturing of nuclear warhead components at the Rocky Flats plant located nine miles south of Boulder.

In June, a red-light system to stop long-winded speakers had its trial run before the city council. The green (four and a half minutes), amber (30 seconds), and red light system was proposed by Deputy City Manager Jim Piper in hopes it would trim lengthy meetings.

In a unanimous decision, the city council agreed to repeal the city sales tax on food as an incentive to pass the bond issue for a performing arts center. A proposed half cent overall increase in the city sales taxes would pay off the arts bond, fund operation of the arts center and make up for the lost food taxes. The 7.9 million bond issue would go for purchase and renovation of the old Boulder Theater and construction of a new complex of smaller theaters and rehearsal halls at 13th Street and Canyon Boulevard. (Camera, May 7, Daryl Gibson)

Denver Post reporter Jane Cracraft wrote on May 21 that the council had decided to delay a public vote on a bond issue for parks and recreation. A \$7.9 million bond issue to build a performing arts center will stand alone on the September ballot.

In February, the council renewed a policy first adopted in 1978, that of refusing to spend public money for employees to attend conventions in states which have not ratified the Equal Rights Amendment. Although the policy allows exceptions, none were granted to Mayor Correll so she might attend a National League of Cities convention in New Orleans. She had been invited to accept an award from the Department of Housing and Urban Development for urban environmental design of the downtown mall.

In March, the council reversed a planning board decision by rejecting plans by two developers to construct 55-foot high buildings. The city charter prohibits buildings higher than 55 feet. An ordinance requires special review by the planning board of any building over 35 feet. The board has final authority unless the council takes an interest and reviews the issue.

Daryl Gibson reported in the May 7 Camera that:

In a unanimous vote, the council approved construction of ... the once-controversial ... building proposed for 1025 Pearl St. (The Arnett Partnership), led by restaurateur Frank Day, will build a five-story office and store complex on land where former City Councilman Bob White envisioned ...remodeling of the historic Arnett Hotel. White's plans collapsed with the hotel shell in a spring snowstorm in 1978.

The Boulder City Council was looking for a new image in 1978 when it remodeled its meeting chambers, but the result makes the "wrong statement" about city government according to some council members.

From 1951 to 1978 council members sat like magistrates on an elevated platform in a tiny room which doubled as a courtroom.

The solution was a center stage where the council now sits in a line facing city staff instead of the public.

The public must choose between a partial view of the staff, a back view of speakers addressing the council and a side view of charts and graphs shown to the council.

"The public are spectators. They just witness the process," said architect Tom Nixon, who designed the remodeled chambers ... . "The room makes a clear statement that the government is not run by the public, but by the elected officials, who execute public orders."

"The primary emphasis was to clarify the process of what happens between the City Council and the city staff ... ."

(Councilman Paul Danish) admitted that the original room presented some tough limitations. "In the remodeling, we felt we could get more public involvement with the council sitting in the center," he said.

As Danish sees it, the main problem is that the platform where the council sits is still elevated. "It should have been down."

Danish cited the theater-in-the-round idea the Boston City Council uses as a good way to involve the public. (Camera, March 17, Klasina Vanderwerf)

In November the council gave an 11 percent raise to City Manager Bob Westdyke, a 12 percent raise to City Attorney Joe de Raismes and a four percent raise to Municipal Judge Richard Hansen.

#### CITY MANAGER

On February 20, Bob French reported in the Colorado Daily:

Due to unexpected city-wide business revenue decreases, and the unexpected growth of business in the Crossroads redevelopment area, Boulder may end up short \$445,000 in its general fund for 1980.

Included in the city's financial picture is an agreement the city has with the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority (BURA). The agreement provides for the city to allocate funds to BURA in accordance to the amount of sales tax raised in the proposed redevelopment area ... . The funds will be used to pay for the capital construction costs and operational costs of the ... project.

According to (City Manager) Westdyke, the city had estimated last fall that the redevelopment area would have a 17 percent sales tax revenue increase, but the area's sales tax revenues grew by 22 percent allowing BURA a greater share of the city's tax revenue. ... or \$235,000 more than the city anticipated ... . (Camera, Aug. 16)

In the Camera of May 21, Daryl Gibson reported that City Manager Westdyke had overturned a decision by Assistant Fire Chief Cliff Harvey, who refused to let International Business Machines fill an empty storage tank with diesel fuel because it would violate city codes. (The city building department had issued a building permit for the tank and inspected the construction last year, but the fire department had learned of its existence only after it was completed.) Westdyke agreed that it was prohibited, but concluded that it "does not constitute a serious fire hazard." IBM has other fuel storage tanks at its plant, but they were built before the area was annexed into the city. The new tank will be used for emergency fuel storage to feed generators in the event of a power outage.

On July 28, the national magazine Newsweek published an article entitled "Where the Hip Meet to Trip" that described Boulder as a laid-back city. Mayor Ruth Correll and City Manager Westdyke, in a letter to the editor, blasted the "factual inaccuracies and misleading innuendoes" and

refuted "a few examples of the sloppy and superficial reporting... . If you cannot get the facts straight on so simple a story, how can your readers trust the integrity of your reports on truly important national and international issues?"

In a guest opinion in the Camera of September 27, City Manager Westdyke explained the "design-build" process first used for the parking deck-RTD terminal at 14th and Walnut (a contract which council, on a 5-4 vote awarded to the highest bidder).

Unlike the traditional procedure, where the owner hires an architect at about 10 percent of the project cost to develop a design, followed by a bidding process whereby normally the low bid is accepted, the design-build concept asks each bidder to submit a complete package, including design, at a final price.

Selection is made on the basis of greatest overall value for the money spent ... . several factors are evaluated in addition to the price, including experience, committed completion date, and the merit of the design ... .

An article by Daryl Gibson in the December 3 Camera reported that:

In an unannounced presentation during a council meeting Tuesday night (City Manager) Westdyke said the recent public interest in the SDC (System Development Corporation) plan caused him to rethink the city's neutral status.

He outlined a list of options for the council to take a stand on the issue and showed a strong leaning toward a plan which would redraw the boundaries of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan to take in the SDC land, put the SDC question to a public vote and annex the land to the city if the voters approve the plan.

The surprise presentation came on the heels of news that SDC is courting Louisville, Broomfield and Superior for possible annexation into any of those cities of the plant site at the intersection of Colorado highways 93 and 128 south of Boulder.

Because the land is outside the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, the council has no jurisdiction over how it is developed and cannot stop other cities from annexing it.

#### CITY ATTORNEY

In the Camera of March 26, Daryl Gibson reported that the council had

authorized City Attorney de Raismes to appeal a federal District Court decision which ordered the city to lift the ban on expanding cable service in the city. The city attorney said he believed the decision is wrong and that the judge is "forging new law in antitrust and forging new law in cable communications".

The Appeals Court overturned the district judge's ruling in October. However, the same judge ruled unconstitutional the city's plan to limit TeleCommunications Inc. to serving one-third of the city. Daryl Gibson reported in the June 17 Camera that a Denver law firm has billed Boulder for \$59,000 to defend the city against cable television lawsuits.

In June, the council approved a plan by City Attorney Joe de Raismes to rewrite the city code. (The last revision was made 15 years ago and contains outdated laws and some too vague to be enforced).

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of June 18 that because of the code project and the high cost of defending the city in cable television lawsuits, de Raismes was exempted from the 2.5 percent budget cut imposed on other city departments. The council also agreed to add \$15,000 to de Raismes' fund for hiring private attorneys for the cable cases and other special projects.

In July, District Judge Murray Richtel ruled that the Boulder City Council was within its rights when it formed the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority. The decision settled at least part of a suit filed against the council by Don Tracy and two other mall-area businessmen.

Deputy City Attorney Jane Greenfield said she hoped Richtel's ruling could be used to squelch a second suit which echoed Tracy's claim that Crossroads is not blighted and, therefore, the city's plans to revive it are illegal. Greenfield said she did not ask for a ruling on Tracy's claim

that BURA violated freedom of information laws by refusing to show him part of a report on mall financing. (Camera, July 11, Daryl Gibson)

City Attorney Joe de Raismes has urged the city council not to schedule an election on the basis of a petition submitted by opponents of the Crossroads Mall expansion. He said the petition is "invalid" because it is worded as a referendum, and as such, should have been submitted within 30 days of the time the council approved the project last year. De Raismes also said the petition seeks to "impair a contract" since BURA, meeting the day before the petitions were submitted, had finalized \$10.5 million in loans to help finance the Crossroads project. (Camera, Aug. 16)

#### MUNICIPAL COURT

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of June 24 that the city council:

...voted 7 to 2 to make Municipal Court a "court of record".

That means a taped or stenographic record will be made of all court proceedings and kept in a word-for-word transcript.

Judge Hansen (said) it was an unnecessary expense which would stifle the informal workings of his court.

The recording issue arose after the city lost a case appealed from Municipal Court. In that case, Feyline Productions successfully fought a Municipal Court conviction on noise violations stemming from a Folsom Stadium concert.

The appeal had to be retried for three days because Municipal Court did not keep a complete record.

Hansen said that was the only such appeal during his time on the bench.

### ELECTIONS

The arts center proposal, placed on a ballot in the primary elections held September 9, was defeated by a percentage of 53.3 with votes against 8,488 and votes for 7,428. Thirty-seven percent of registered voters went to the polls. (Denver Post, Sept. 10, Jane Cracraft)

In the November election, voters in the six-county metropolitan area

voted for an elected, rather than appointed, Regional Transportation District Board of Directors.

An RTD light rail proposal was defeated by a 54.2 percent majority district-wide, and by a 51.3 percent majority in Boulder County. (Times-Call, Nov. 8-9, Barry Noreen)

1981

### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

On January 12, officials for the System Development Corporation (SDC) submitted an application for rezoning to Boulder County for the 475 acres of land near the Jefferson County line where they want to build an industrial complex.

They had previously applied for an amendment to the comprehensive plan as a first step to rezoning. The council is in the process of condemning the land so the city may purchase it as open space (to hold until a vote can be taken). (Times-Call, Jan. 13, Barry Noreen)

SDC, on the Friday before the court hearing on condemnation, outlined to the city attorney seven conditions under which they would agree to locate on the site. Among these were that the city withdraw its suit, that the city would not require an election on use of the land but abide by an opinion poll taken in the county, that there would be no annexation to Boulder until the county planning process was completed, etc. They gave the city five hours to respond. The council declined to call a special meeting. SDC then decided not to locate in Boulder County despite suggestions for alternative sites. (Camera, Feb. 15, Sue Deans)

On January 20, the council voted to redraw the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan map to add 2,515 new acres, including the SDC site, to the city's planning area. (Camera, Jan. 21, Daryl Gibson) The Boulder Planning Board had already approved the change. It must now be approved by the county planning commission and the county commissioners.

The county planners voted in favor of the addition. The Boulder County Commissioners turned down the request on a 2-1 vote. Commissioners Jack Murphy and Bob Jenkins (elected in November) were opposed with Commissioner Markey dissenting. (Camera, Feb. 24, Sharon Gillen) During the meeting Jenkins compared Boulder's attempt to control the land to Germany's expansion programs of the 1930's. His remarks were called "arrogant and condescending" by Councilman Paul Danish and "callous and obscene" by Planning Director Frank Gray. (Camera, Feb. 25, Sharon Gillen)

The District Court awarded the city possession of the SDC site and the city deposited \$770,000 with the court for the land purchase, with the final price to be arbitrated. (Camera, Feb. 17, Daryl Gibson and Sharon Gillen)

Councilmembers received much criticism for "chasing away a multimillion-dollar corporation". SDC claimed that city officials had refused a last minute offer to negotiate. County Commissioner Bob Jenkins said the "opportunity ... was intentionally ruined by a relatively small number of city officials ... and pressure groups." (Camera, Feb. 18, Daryl Gibson and Sharon Gillen)

The Boulder Chamber of Commerce sharply criticized the city's handling of the System Development Corporation affair as evidence of its "continued negative attitude toward business development in the Boulder area." (Camera, Feb. 19, Sarah Hoover)

On February 26, the County Commissioners voted to invite SDC back to

Boulder County with the promise that "any processing of applications within the county structure could be expedited." (Camera, Feb. 27, Daryl Gibson)

As a result of SDC's withdrawal from the county, the Chamber of Commerce, the Board of Realtors, and the council met to discuss setting up an economic commission to deal with businesses seeking to locate here. (Camera, April 17, Daryl Gibson) The city council approved the formation of an Economic Futures Panel of eleven members in June.

In February the city council complied with the decision of Boulder District Court and set another election on the Crossroads Mall redevelopment for April 7.

The sentiment of both the council and the community was probably expressed best by Boulder Chamber of Commerce President Richard Hadley when he reported the emotional and financial uncertainties being inflicted on many Crossroads' business men and women because of the delays. "We have to take the more expeditious route." (Colorado Daily, Feb. 18, Chip Hinds)

An alternative to an election would have been to await the results of pending court cases and possibly more appeals to those decisions.

The council passed a resolution guaranteeing that some plan will be put together to pay back the BURA debts of \$9 million if voters abolish BURA at the April 7 election. (Camera, April 2, Daryl Gibson)

A Boulder citizens' group requested the council to put a city charter amendment on the ballot that would divide the city into election districts. The council refused so the Citizens for Districting set out to get signatures on a petition demanding a September 1 special election. Three districting methods were to be proposed. The one chosen would then be decided at a second election. (Camera, May 28, Daryl Gibson)

The petition submitted by the group was declared invalid by City Clerk Dave Manzanares because it lacked the required number of valid signatures.

The city attorney stated that the wording of the petition was not adequate to meet the requirements stated in the city charter. (Camera, July 7, Daryl Gibson)

In August the citizens' group submitted a new petition with 2,000 more signatures than required to get the issue on the November ballot. (Fewer signatures are required for a regular election than for a special election). (Camera, Aug. 25, Daryl Gibson) The petition was certified in September.

Another citizens' petition was circulated to put a recreation bond issue to a vote. A Parks Department proposal for a separate bond issue (originally scheduled to coincide with the Boulder Valley School District board election on May 5) was accepted by the council. (The School Board had declined to share the election.) (Camera, Jan. 28, Daryl Gibson)

In the March 11 Camera, Daryl Gibson reported that the city council will assemble financial data on a \$16.9 million parks bond issue, but will not urge Boulder voters to support it. The council had favored a proposal in the \$7.2 million range. The parks staff favored one of \$13.3 million. A group of local ice skaters successfully petitioned for an election which included everything the staff had suggested, plus a city-owned ice arena. Among the new facilities would be a new east Boulder park, an east Boulder recreation center with the ice-rink and a branch library, an expansion of Olmsted Park, more athletic fields and improvements to Boulder Reservoir. The bonds would be paid off through a one-third of a cent increase in the city sales taxes over the next ten years.

In March the council approved a 20-year lease on Chautauqua Park. The lease would turn over ownership of four public structures to the Chautauqua

Association, charge \$2,500 per year (the fee had been \$1 per year since 1898), and allow council to appoint two representatives to the Chautauqua board. (Camera, March 11, Daryl Gibson)

For the last ten years, a food tax refund has been available to all applicants who resided in the city limits for 12 months and met the income criteria. In 1981, individuals are eligible for \$5.89, except for those 65 years old or older or those who are blind who are eligible to receive a double refund. However, fewer and fewer people have been applying for the return. Any unused money goes back into the general fund. (Denver Post, April 15, Linda Cornett)

Beginning in January 1982, the city will collect sales taxes on computer software, demonstrator cars, and equipment stored outside the city which is used for business inside the city. (Camera, July 8, Daryl Gibson)

Among council endorsements for development revenue bonds was the Arrow Office Supply which is being displaced by the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority.

In July, \$900,600 in federal Community Development block grants for 1982 were allocated. The city's Housing Authority received \$501,000. Other recipients were the privately owned Alvarado Village and San Juan del Centro housing projects, Boulder Attention Homes, People's Clinic, the Senior Center (to convert the shuffleboard courts to a room for a senior day-care program), the Carnegie Library, a project to remove architectural barriers in private homes, and \$100,000 to administer the Community Development office. (Camera, July 24)

Councilman George Boland believes that a controversial medical

questionnaire given new city employees requiring answers to such topics as birth control, abortion and pregnancy, and marijuana smoking should be eliminated. (Times-Call, Oct. 21, Sue Diehl)

In November, the council established a five-member Boulder Arts Commission to administer the city arts grants program.

The council gave City Manager Westdyke an 11 percent raise increasing his salary from \$54,389 to \$60,372. The city attorney was also given an 11 percent increase to \$48,484. Judge Richard Hansen was allowed a six percent raise to \$40,460. (Camera, Oct. 8)

#### CITY MANAGER

In January, Deputy City Manager Dave Knapp said that the "cushion" of 18.5 percent in the general fund planned for 1981 was nearly depleted. The freeze on hiring and purchases put into effect last year had not shown the expected benefits. (Camera, Jan. 7, Daryl Gibson)

With city revenues falling behind inflation, City Manager Bob Westdyke proposed a 1982 budget that calls for cutbacks in library, police, and fire departments. The major cut is in library services with the George Reynolds branch reducing its hours from 70 hours a week to 35 and the main library being closed each Friday. The police department would cut one juvenile and two drug enforcement officers and the fire department would lose one firefighter. (Denver Post, August 18, Jane Cracraft)

#### CITY ATTORNEY

In September, the council added a new series of laws to the city code making it illegal to sniff glue, camp on public or private property without

permission, use someone else's dumpster, set off fireworks, etc. The juvenile curfew was retained in the code.

#### MUNICIPAL COURT

The Denver Boot, a notorious contraption which immobilizes vehicles, debuted in Boulder on February 12. It will be affixed to the cars of habitual parking offenders who have not paid off their tickets. Municipal Court Administrator Terry Aragon said there were 206 people on that list. The first increase in parking fines since 1969 was implemented on February 6.

Fines on the list are worth \$15,300 to the city. In 1980, Boulder collected \$119,000, but only 52 percent of the tickets given out were accounted for either through payment or dismissal. (Camera, Feb. 24, Paul England)

### ELECTIONS

After the council decision to resubmit to the voters the question of redeveloping the Crossroads area, groups were formed to approve (a "yes" vote would abolish BURA) or disapprove (a "no" vote would retain).

Members of LOGIC (Local Orderly Government Involving Citizens) headed by Don Tracy and Dave Donaldson were joined by people representing the Socialist, Libertarian and Citizens' political parties, along with business people who opposed the concept of urban renewal.

Mr. Tracy said the goals of LOGIC were to "return the government to the people of the city of Boulder and release them from the hostage situation that's been created by the city administration". (Camera, March 12, Daryl Gibson)

George Doty, the man who launched the campaign against the Boulder

Urban Renewal Authority in 1979, formed his own campaign group, Citizens Against the Recent Charter Amendment (CARCA). Not allied with LOGIC, Doty is fundamentally opposed to the treatment displaced Crossroads merchants will get, while LOGIC members base their campaign on objections to big government and urban renewal as well as the fear that the project will drain other city programs. Doty is seeking funds and advising potential contributors that they may avoid publicity by giving smaller contributions (\$25 and under). (Camera, March 14)

Carolyn Holmberg heads the reborn Coalition for Boulder's Future which was formed to promote the first vote on Crossroads in 1979. She feels the Coalition's task is to reeducate the public to the benefits of the public-private partnership in the redevelopment.

Speakers, office volunteers and contributors are offered by Coalition member organizations - the League of Women Voters, PLAN Boulder County, Sierra and Audubon Clubs, Historic Boulder, Boulder County Women's Political Caucus and People United to Reclaim the Environment (PURE).

Business organizations have formed a cooperative Business Alliance. These include the Chamber of Commerce, Board of Realtors, Downtown Boulder Association and Boulder Economic Council.

BURA members and staff are keeping a relatively low profile in the campaign, but the redevelopment firm of MaceRich Co. has contributed with advertisements and with money to the Coalition. (Denver Post, April 1, Linda Cornett)

Eugene Wilson, who headed the Coalition for Boulder's Future in 1979, said he will vote against the project. In a letter to the Camera on April 5, he explained that the project originally was sold as a \$12.5 million package; then the city council gave BURA the power to issue \$20 million in

bonds and also committed itself to extra expenditures for flood control and transportation around the mall. BURA member Janet Roberts said Wilson's criticisms were misguided and that the Crossroads package had not changed in "any major way" since the 1979 election. (Camera, April 5, Daryl Gibson)

As the April 7 election drew closer, both sides of the issue traded insults. Councilman Paul Danish demanded an investigation by the district attorney of the anti-BURA campaign literature. Mr. Danish said that LOGIC may have violated state campaign laws by sending out unsigned postcards to registered voters. The law requires the name of the organization and a responsible person to be listed on all mailings.

Don Tracy protested that the pro-BURA group had listed the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) among several businesses and agencies which had "staked either their money or their reputation on its (the BURA project) success". The SEC saw a copy of an offering statement that went out to potential bond buyers, but did not pass judgement on it. That copy was mailed to the SEC by George Doty as part of a complaint that BURA was deceiving bond buyers. The SEC never acted on that complaint. (Camera, April 7, Daryl Gibson)

On April 7 voters defeated by 55.1 percent the ballot issue which would have abolished BURA. The final tally gave BURA a 2,000-vote margin, with 10,878 votes against and 8,831 for. (In the June 1979 election, BURA won by 688 votes when 12,196 voters turned out.) Nearly 20,000 votes were cast representing almost 47 percent of the registered voters in the city. (Camera, April 8, Daryl Gibson)

LOGIC founder Don Tracy and member Dave Donaldson say their group will probably play a role in a possible effort to impose a district system for electing city council members. Donaldson said further possible litigation against BURA could involve what he believed was the county residents' right

to vote on the issue because the project would draw tax revenues from outside the city. (Colorado Daily, April 24, J. Maxwell)

The parks bond election was held April 28 and was rejected by the 22.2 percent of those registered who voted. The final count was 6,289 against and 3,157 in favor. The special election cost the city \$17,500.

On November 3, five councilmembers were elected from a slate of 19 candidates. Thirty-five percent of Boulder's registered voters (16,348) went to the polls.

Ballot measures which won were: The nuclear arms moratorium with 66.3 percent; the \$7.4 million parks bond with 54.7 percent; the removal of gender references from the charter with 59.5 percent; and the Blue Line amendment with nearly 73 percent.

The vote on requesting the representatives from Boulder to introduce a resolution requesting the President to propose to the Soviet Union a mutual nuclear arms moratorium passed 9,965 to 5,059.

The actual vote on the \$7.4 million park and recreation bond issue was 8,693 for and 7,193 against. It will be paid for by a sales tax increase of 0.15 percent for the next 10 years. The money will be used for improvements at Boulder Reservoir, expansion of softball fields at Stazio Recreation Complex, addition of multi-purpose athletic fields and for purchase of land for community parks in north and east Boulder.

The Blue Line amendment extends city services to three properties on west Arapahoe Avenue: Nuzum's Nursery, the Silver Saddle Motel, and the Flagstaff Apartments. That vote was 10,963 for and 4,104 against.

The issues which lost were: Redistricting by a majority of nearly two to one; the \$12.7 parks bond issue by a margin of two to one; and the

question of allowing the council to reword ballot titles for citizen-initiated referenda almost two to one.

Ballot question number 2, attempting to divide the city into election districts (the third such vote in Boulder) failed with 63.8 percent or 9,354 opposed and 5,287 in favor. The proposal suggested four districts, with each electing two councilmembers. The mayor would have been elected at large.

There were five "housekeeping" amendments to the city charter on the ballot. Question number 5 which changed the day on which new councilmembers take office from New Years Day to the third Tuesday in November passed 11,875 to 3,146. It will take effect in 1987. Question number 6 which will require people who sign petitions to be registered voters was approved 11,903 to 3,616. Question number 7 which changes the formula that determines how many petition signatures are needed to put an item on the ballot passed 9,614 to 5,450. The current charter bases the requirement on the number of voters in the last gubernatorial election. The amendment uses the number of registered voters in the city as a base. Question number 8 would have taken away from petitioners the responsibility of preparing ballot titles for their referenda. It was defeated 9,344 to 5,224. Question number 9, to change to neuter terms all gender references in the charter was approved 8,931 to 6,066. (Camera, Nov. 4, Daryl Gibson)

### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

Ruth Correll was reelected mayor of Boulder by a unanimous vote of the city council. She was sworn in for her third term on New Years Day. (Camera, Jan. 2, Daryl Gibson)

In February, the council adopted a new public hearing procedure which moves hearings from the first council vote on an issue to the second vote. Two separate affirmative votes are required by the city charter for the council to pass any new ordinance.

Under the new procedure, the first vote will be to introduce and publish the proposed ordinance. Two weeks later at the second meeting, the council will hold public hearings and vote whether to approve or reject the ordinance. The object is to give the public and the council two weeks to consider a proposal before the final vote. (Camera, Feb. 4)

Annexations during the year included 52 acres on Arapahoe Road at Cherryvale, the Elks Lodge on North 28th Street, 100 Arapahoe Avenue adjacent to Eben Fine Park, and the Celestial Seasonings 51-acre site east of the Longmont Diagonal.

The Danish Plan expired in April and was replaced by a new "growth limitation ordinance" with fewer restrictions and more building permits available. However, the down-turn in the economy has "managed" growth this year and many building permits were still up for grabs in September. (Camera, Sept. 16, Daryl Gibson)

The council exercised its veto power over a planning board decision

and voted unanimously to disapprove a plan for a 250-room hotel proposed for the intersection of Valmont Road and 47th Street. The original plan, submitted in 1977 included a 100-room hotel and was approved by the planning board. In 1980 the request was changed to 150 rooms which was also approved by the board, but vetoed by the council. (Camera, Nov. 17, Daryl Gibson)

In the Camera of November 11, Daryl Gibson reported that mandatory street lights were removed from Boulder's subdivision law by the city council. The old code was not strictly enforced. Last year the Public Service Company, for the first time, began charging developers to install the lights, an expense passed on to the home buyer. The city will continue to pay the maintenance costs and the utility bills for any installed lights.

The council approved a \$6.59 refund on sales taxes paid for food. Last year, refunds totalling \$138,503 were paid out. (Camera, Jan. 6, Daryl Gibson) The refund program is composed of two distinct elements - the food tax refund and the low-income based refund. An applicant may be eligible for both. The food tax part offers a fixed refund for each exemption allowed on the application; an exemption may be claimed for each household member who resided in the city for the entire 12 months of the calendar year. The low-income based portion requires at least 6 months residency and an annual income for the entire household of no more than \$13,249 (for 1981).

Boulder ended 1981 with a 15.5 percent increase in sales tax revenues. That represents \$14.3 million, of which the city kept \$13.3 million and turned over \$982,000 to the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority to fund the

Crossroads Mall expansion project. (Camera, Feb. 18)

In October, the council voted to increase the property tax 1.7 mills. The increase raises the mill levy to the legal maximum of 13.4. The increase will fund reopening the library on Fridays in 1983. (Times-Call, Oct. 20, Barry Bortnick)

The council revised the conflict-of-interest laws to govern all city employees. The old law restricted only council members, advisory board members and judges. The new law says the conflict must be declared as soon as is reasonably possible; it sets no limit on the number of conflicts a person can declare; and states that a contract may be voided at the discretion of the council. The old law required disclosure of conflict no later than 72 hours before any action was taken, permitted three conflicts every two years, and declared contracts automatically void if a council member had benefited from it.

Amendments to the city election law were adopted that will require candidates to put a sponsor's name on all campaign literature and advertising. The name could be an individual or committee. Candidates also will have to disclose the number of contributions they receive of less than \$25 each. Under the old law, only the total amount of those contributions had to be reported. (Camera, July 21, Daryl Gibson)

The Community Development Block Grant program dates from 1974 and gives federal tax money to local governments to spend as they want within certain guidelines. Boulder has used the bulk of its grant money for low- and moderate-income housing. The city has received more than \$6.7 million from the program since 1975. Community Development Director Susan Purdy estimates a 15 percent reduction from the 1982 grant of \$887,000.

A 37-member citizens' committee has recommended to council the agencies to be funded in 1983. (Camera, Aug. 3, Daryl Gibson) Among the recipients of funds were the Boulder Housing Authority for rehabilitation grants, the Center for People with Disabilities to remove architectural barriers to the handicapped, and Safehouse to expand living space at its facility for battered women and children.

In May, the council adopted a resolution criticizing the "aura of fear and uncertainty" caused by a recent roundup of illegal workers. (Camera, May 26)

In August, a pamphlet created by the Nuclear War Education Committee to inform Boulder citizens of the horrors of nuclear war was given council approval. The pamphlet is the result of an action taken by the Boulder County Commissioners last March when they unanimously rejected a federally sponsored crisis relocation plan and suggested publication of an educational pamphlet. Councilwoman Linda Jourgensen chaired the Committee.

On November 17, Councilman George Boland died after a heart attack. The Boulder City Council had thirty days to fill the vacancy, and appointed Spenser Havlick on December 14 to fill out the remainder of Mr. Boland's term. Mr Havlick will have to run for reelection in 1983 if he wants to retain the seat. (Camera, Dec. 15, Daryl Gibson)

In September, the council approved a 10.2 percent pay raise for the city manager (to \$66,529), a 9.5 percent raise for the city attorney (to \$53,090), and a 7.7 percent increase for the municipal judge (to \$44,397).

#### CITY MANAGER

Two opinion polls were conducted in 1981 and the partial results were

reported to council by City Manager Westdyke. He made his presentation from a chart summarizing "Poll A" and "Poll B". One example said that 46 percent of the citizens approved of growth control, and 78 percent rated Boulder "a livable city". Mr. Westdyke said that only 10 or 11 percent felt the city was not doing a good job but that these people are quite disgruntled.

The sponsors of one poll, the Boulder Business Political Committee said city officials were not given the entire poll. The other poll, done by Paul Talmey, was not available to the public. (Denver Post, Jan. 13, Jane Cracraft)

The city manager briefed the council in March on the investigative hearings into the deaths of two Boulder firemen during a January 26 training fire. He said that the investigation results will be secret until it is decided whether any disciplinary action will be taken. Assistant City Manager Jim Piper conducted the hearings. (Camera, March 24, Daryl Gibson)

In April, City Manager Westdyke proposed to the council an election that would add 0.35 percent to the city sales taxes. In May, however, two unexpected sources of income gave the city enough money to avoid going into the red. These sources were the statewide lottery and the city's parks and recreation bond which sold for considerably less interest than expected. This means that all the proceeds from the 0.15 percent sales tax increase to pay back those bonds will not have to be used for that purpose, but can go to the city's general fund. The council told the city manager to prepare a city budget for 1983 that would include an increase in property taxes. (Camera, May 12, Daryl Gibson)

In a straw poll taken by the Daily Camera, readers who responded were united on one issue: "If the City Council raises taxes in 1983, it had better spend the money ... to restore lost library operating hours ... ." (Camera, Sept. 12, Daryl Gibson)

City Manager Bob Westdyke proposed a \$42.9 million budget for 1983. He recommended a property tax increase for other projects, but advised the council that restoring full operating hours at the library would be a financial mistake. He is convinced that if the council reopens the library on Fridays in 1983, they will have to close it again in 1984.

Deputy City Manager David Knapp announced a hiring freeze and spending restrictions have been imposed for the remainder of the year on those departments whose money comes from the city's general fund. This decision was the result of a shortfall in revenue in 1982, primarily from a lower amount than expected from the sale of gas and electricity through the Public Service Company. If predictions for a deficit for 1982 are accurate, money will be taken from the city's reserve fund which is maintained in anticipation of revenue fluctuations. (Camera, Dec. 4, Peter Nielsen)

In August, David Grimm, news director for KBOL Radio was hired by the city manager as the city's media relations expert. He replaces Stephen Foehr who resigned last spring.

In November, Steven Fisher was selected as Boulder's new budget director. The position has been vacant since August when Geoffrey Holt resigned. While Holt was director, the position changed from merely preparing and keeping track of the budget to include making economic forecasts of city income and expenditures. (Times-Call, Nov. 18, Peter Nielsen)

#### CITY ATTORNEY

A new gun law was passed in March. City officials stressed that it was merely a rewording of the old law to make it conform to state statutes. It makes carrying a concealed weapon or a loaded gun in the city illegal unless the owner has a permit issued by the city. A person applying for a permit can expect to be investigated and to have his photograph and fingerprints put on file with the police department. A first-time permit will cost \$100 and an annual renewal, \$50. Gun dealers must keep a record of the name of every person who buys a gun from them, as well as a description, occupation, address, and the gun specifications.

City Attorney Joe de Raismes and Police Chief Jay Propst wanted to include a section giving police the right to confiscate a gun for 72 hours if the owner posed a danger to himself or others but the council dropped that idea. De Raismes said there is no legal precedent to defend such a provision if the city were sued. (Camera, March 15, Daryl Gibson)

On April 23, a dog-at-large ordinance went into effect doubling previous fines and making it harder for violators to escape a fine without a good excuse for an unleashed dog. The higher fee should discourage repeat offenders. In enforcing the ordinance, the city contracts three full-time employees of the Boulder Humane Society. The staff is adequate to handle the number of complaints phoned in, but does not allow for street patrol.

City Attorney de Raismes and Municipal Judge Hansen proposed changes to the city code that would have upgraded some offenses to allow strip searches. Sheriff Brad Leach will not jail anyone he cannot subject to a

strip search.

Some of the petty offenses that would be upgraded to misdemeanors are nuisance occurrences on the Downtown Mall. The offenses chosen to be upgraded are only the ones already classed as misdemeanors under state laws. Police Chief Propst prefers to charge offenders with municipal violations rather than state violations so the cases can go through Municipal Court.

The council tabled the issue. (Camera, June 16, Daryl Gibson)

#### MUNICIPAL COURT

The council has had the idea of making Boulder Municipal Court a court of record on the back burner for several years. As currently operated, it is an informal place where a verbatim record is not kept.

Municipal Judge Richard Hansen said he would go along with a \$10,000 plan to experiment with tape recorders to make his court a partial "court of record" which will not involve traffic violations. (Camera, May 26)

### ELECTIONS

There were no city elections in 1982.

1983

### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

Councilman Homer Ball died on June 2. He was elected to the council in 1981. He had served previously as a councilmember after being appointed to fill out the three months of the unexpired term of Robert Knecht in 1971. On June 28, the council appointed Robert Greenlee to fill the

vacancy. (Camera, June 4 and 29, Daryl Gibson)

In April, Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera that the city agreed in an out-of-court settlement to pay \$1,543,750 for 475 acres of land once chosen by System Development Corporation as a site for a computer plant. The city had condemned the land in 1981 to preserve it as open space. It has taken two years for the price to be settled.

In February, the council passed a resolution asking Congress to adopt legislation exempting cities from antitrust laws. A lawsuit filed against Boulder by TeleCommunications Inc. resulted in a precedent-setting ruling by the United States Supreme Court last year that makes cities subject to antitrust laws unless Congress or state legislatures grant them an exemption. (Camera, Feb. 2, Daryl Gibson)

Jane Cracraft reported in the Denver Post on Feb. 23 that the city council had voted 5-4 to ban nudity at Coot Lake. Council members said they don't want to decide any issue, including nudity, on moral grounds. "We shouldn't be legislating lifestyle," said Councilmember Linda Jourgensen. Mayor Correll said it didn't seem right to her that "after 10 years of standing up for the point that people have the right to be themselves within the law -- that we would be more restrictive than the rest of Colorado."

In May, the Boulder County Commissioners approved a major change to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. The "Performance Industrial Concept" opens up to industrial speculation land outside Boulder that previously had been ineligible for annexation until 1997. Mayor Ruth Correll said it is "growth management of industry". (Camera, May 6, Sharon

Gillen)

Because the county made some changes, their version was sent back to the city for another vote. The most significant of the changes approved was a demand that any industrial proposal would have "no major negative fiscal, service, environmental or related land-use impacts on the city, the county, or other communities in the county." NBI Inc., a Boulder computer company, sparked the change over a year ago with the claim that there was not enough land in large parcels for an industry to build within the city limits. (Camera, June 22, Daryl Gibson)

A five-story office building was approved for the northeast quarter of Boulder's "superblock", which is bounded by Ninth, 11th, Canyon, and Walnut. (Camera, March 17, Sharon Gillen) The council agreed that they want more information about conceptual planning in the downtown area so they could better handle decisions about individual projects as they arise. (Camera, April 6, Sharon Gillen)

The council agreed to rezone property north of the Boulderado Hotel so that an addition could be built. The original hotel, a historic landmark, would be connected to the annex by a bridge across an alley. (Camera, July 6, Daryl Gibson)

The new growth management ordinance, which replaced the Danish plan, had used more than half its allocated residential building permits by September. In 1982 only 280 permits were issued, thus adding 200 unused permits to the 1983 allocation of 849. Developments are scored based on a merit system which gives points to residences that, for instance, are close to municipal services and have energy-saving features or unique designs. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 6, Kathleen Smith)

The council unanimously endorsed the application of the Golden Buff Motor Hotel for \$1.65 million in development revenue bonds. The Boulder County Commissioners have the final say in issuing the bonds. (Camera, May 5, Sharon Gillen) The city council approved the issuance of \$10 million in municipally backed development revenue bonds for the construction of an office and retail complex to replace the abandoned Regional Transportation District bus depot at 9th Street and Canyon Boulevard. This allows the developers to purchase bonds at a discount because of the tax-exempt status of city-sponsored projects. (Denver Post, July 27, Dave Price) Also endorsed were \$14.9 million in bonds for improvements at Boulder Memorial Hospital. (Camera, Dec. 21, Daryl Gibson)

In the April 10 Camera, Daryl Gibson wrote that interviews with council members prompted her to write the headline "Council alters liberal course".

If there is a trend, council members call it "caution" and they attribute it to the beating the city took in the System Development Corp. and the TeleCommunications Inc. issues.

The council took its knocks for that (SDC) and immediately set out to be more hospitable to future industrial development applications.

(In the TCI issue) the U. S. Supreme Court ... essentially told the council it overstepped its bounds when it tried to regulate the cable TV company. It cost the council more than a quarter of a million dollars in legal fees to learn that lesson. ... as former Councilman Paul Danish observed, (the number of risks taken by council may have dwindled) because the radical decisions were all made in the 1970s and the council now is in "a period of consolidation." ... he sees a "trend toward social conservatism," and points to the closing of the Coot Lake nude beach as an example.

Coot Lake is not the council's only recent action that smacks of a new political outlook:

The city recently drafted a "performance industrial" plan to allow industrial plants in rural areas in advance of the development time set for those areas in the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

The council is considering a "Superblock" development downtown. Council permission to allow buildings to go to the city's 55-foot height limit, especially downtown, is becoming routine.

City affordable housing plans are geared to loosening restrictions ... .

Councilwoman Annette Anderson thinks the key to current city politics is found in the economy. "A lot of us have become increasingly aware of a sound economic base for our community," she said.

Correll noted that for the first time, the council listed a strong economy as one of its primary goals this year.

In 1983, despite expectations of lower Community Development block grants, Boulder actually received a 17 percent increase over 1982. (Colorado Daily, June 20-21, Tim Lange) Susan Purdy, administrator of the program said the extra funding was primarily because of an increase in the city's population living below the poverty level (one of the criteria for allocation of grant money). A family of four earning less than \$24,480 is in this category. Based on recommendations from the CD Committee, the council divided the money among low-income residential sites, the purchase of a larger facility for a sheltered workshop for the mentally ill, the acquisition of a house to be shared by eight mentally ill adults, restoration of the historical Chautauqua dining hall, making more areas accessible to the handicapped, and other equally important projects. (Denver Post, July 27, Dave Price)

The council voted to prohibit non-motorized bikes on trails, fire access roads and other unimproved roads in city parks and open space. It did give the city manager authority to reopen specific areas to mountain bicyclists at his discretion.

Among other annexations was the unanimous approval for including the city-owned airport facility within the city limits. (Camera, Jan. 19, Sharon Gillen) Others were the Hungry Farmer restaurant and the Boulder Dinner Theater. (Camera, Oct. 19, Daryl Gibson)

A total of \$57,000 in grants was awarded to 31 Boulder literary,

visual and performing artists and groups by the council. (Camera, March 19)

Boulder landlords renting property for more than 30 days will be required to provide written leases to all tenants under a new ordinance. The law had been recommended by the Human Relations Commission and derived from proposals by the Boulder Tenants Union. (Times-Call, Oct. 5, Juliet Bourne)

In a 5-4 vote, the council approved an ordinance requiring that landlords should give tenants reasonable notice before entering their property on a non-emergency basis. (Times-Call, Oct. 19, Juliet Bourne) Two weeks later, by a 5-4 vote, the tenant privacy law was tossed out. The Tenants Union had originally requested that landlords give tenants 24-hour notice before entering the rental. This was watered down to "reasonable" notice, but "reasonable" was not defined. The city attorney brought back the ordinance because it had no mechanism to prosecute landlords for failing to give "reasonable" notice. In fact the tenants' rights law only provided for prosecution of tenants if they failed to let their landlords enter. Councilman Greenlee, who had voted for the ordinance initially, said he could not accept the new language. (Camera, Nov. 2, Daryl Gibson)

In December 1982, the council changed the city code governing advisory boards and commissions to require that no board would be composed of all members of the same sex. They forgot to take the new law into account when they advertised for applicants to fill a vacancy on the planning board, a vacancy created by the retirement of the only woman on that board. Sixteen men and five women applied to fill the vacancy, and the men had to be notified that they have been eliminated from the running. A new deadline was set in hopes that more women would apply. Other boards are similarly affected. The council's affirmative action attempts are being scrutinized

by the Department of the Interior because the federal agency is not happy with the lack of ethnic minorities on the city's Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. That is one problem the city cannot solve, said Mayor Correll, because the council cannot ask for ethnic data on application forms for the advisory boards. (Camera, Jan. 6, Daryl Gibson)

Three years ago, the city council refused to let Mayor Correll go to a convention in New Orleans because Louisiana had not passed the Equal Rights Amendment. She will go this year, accompanied by seven other council members. The policy prohibiting the expenditure of tax money in states which have not ratified the ERA has been dropped. Time expired on the ratification of ERA in June 1982. (Camera, Nov. 17, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder anticipates \$322,604 from the Colorado Lottery. Cities and the state share 35 percent of lottery proceeds not used for prizes or administration. That money is to be spent on parks projects or capital construction but there are no specific guidelines for spending. (Camera, July 8, Daryl Gibson)

The council approved a \$47.8 million 1984 budget based on a 12.177-mill levy, a drop from the 1983 levy of 13.4 mills. Specifics of the budget include four new police officers and restoration of full-time operating hours at the George Reynolds branch library. (Camera, Oct. 19, Daryl Gibson)

In December, the council was in the unusual position of getting extra money from two sources -- \$441,000 from the Colorado Lottery and \$1.2 million in unplanned revenues, mostly from interest on investments. The lottery money was committed to Boulder Creek Park, a gymnastics center at North Boulder Recreation Center, a tree nursery, and energy improvement at

the recreation centers. From its 1983 contingency fund, the council agreed to expend \$2,500 to coordinate efforts of volunteer groups and churches to feed and shelter the needy. (Camera, Dec. 21, Daryl Gibson)

City Manager Westdyke was given a 6 percent salary increase to \$70,522. City Attorney de Raismes' salary was increased by 5.5 percent and that of Municipal Judge Hansen by 5 percent.

#### CITY MANAGER

Deputy City Manager Dave Knapp has predicted a 13 percent increase in 1983 sales tax revenue over that of 1982. Sales taxes provide more than a third of the city government income.

Knapp says past tax history is the only data he has on which to base estimates for future revenue. Unfortunately, sales tax is variable. "It defies econometric projection," he maintains.

To even out the ups and downs, the city is building a reserve fund which can be used to maintain city services without mid-year cuts when revenues are low.

The number of businesses which provide sales tax revenue is fairly constant. There have been about 3500 active sales tax accounts (retail and wholesale) since 1979 with an equal number closing and opening each year. (Camera, Feb. 13, Sue Diehl)

#### CITY ATTORNEY

City governments have braced for a rush of antitrust lawsuits ever since the U. S. Supreme Court made its landmark decision against Boulder in a cable television dispute on Jan. 13, 1982. The court ruled that a state's immunity to antitrust laws does not automatically extend to its

cities. States must pass laws to do that. A bill introduced in this session of the Colorado legislature would exempt local governments only from antitrust suits over land-use regulations. The threat of antitrust suits disturbs City Attorney de Raismes. "The greatest danger is not liability being found, but the expense of litigation and the political expense." He fears that legal threats alone will have a chilling effect on councils and boards making regulatory decisions. (Camera, Feb. 15, Thad Keyes)

#### MUNICIPAL COURT

People who neglected to pay more than two Boulder parking tickets last year will receive a notice in the mail informing them they are on the city's "scoff", or pick-up list. That means that in addition to the \$5 per ticket late charge, they are liable for an additional \$20 charge.

If these scoffers of municipal law do not pay up within 10 days of receiving the notices, their cars are liable to be booted, which means another \$25 to get the boot taken off.

The city collected nearly \$400,000 from parking tickets last year, comprising about 65 percent of the money obtained through Municipal Court. (Colorado Daily, March 11-12, Marty Durlin)

Nearly one-fourth of the 1,180 people who had neglected to pay at least six parking tickets responded to the notices according to Terry Aragon, administrator of the Boulder Municipal Court. The list has been given top priority by the parking control force. Seven patrol officers are checking license numbers against the list. The goal is to catch about half the offenders. (Camera, April 5, Laurie Anderson)

### ELECTIONS

Instead of the usual 35 city voting precincts, this fall there will be only 21. The consolidation of the precincts is the product of discussions and work sessions between City Clerk Dave Manzanares, the county and the school district. Manzanares initiated the discussions following Boulder's 1981 elections, so that voting locations would be identical in every election and citizens could find their polling places more easily. The reduction in the number of places means a savings to the city in the pay for election judges and the cost of voting supplies. (Boulder County Courant, Oct. 19)

Because of the deaths of two councilmembers in office, six council seats were vacant at the November 8 elections. The fifth- and sixth-place winners will get two-year terms. Voters will choose the six from 16 candidates and decide on six ballot questions. ( The seventh question on negotiations between the city and its firefighters was withdrawn.)

By a vote of 6,215 against and 4,044 for (60.5% to 39.4%), the proposal to pay each councilmember \$400 a month was defeated.

With a vote of 5,206 to 3,658, (58.7% to 41.2%) the voters said "no" to a proposition to repeal a section of the charter that makes it illegal for people to spend money on election campaigns. The provision is not enforced and is unconstitutional according to the city attorney.

The amendment to eliminate a charter restriction on the size of city advisory boards was defeated 6,233 to 3,618. (63.2% to 36.7%)

An amendment which would have deleted references to specific city departments in the charter was defeated 6,023 to 3,349. (64.2% to 35.7%)

Voters agreed to the charter amendment that clarified provisions

relating to publication requirements, 5,598 to 4,181. (57.2% to 42.7%)  
(Camera, Nov. 9, Daryl Gibson)

The Central American initiative (placed on the ballot by petition) calling for an end to all U. S. military aid to Central America received a 57.7 percent approval. (Colorado Daily, Nov. 9, Kurt Chandler)

The election drew out only 27.6 percent, or 10,787, of Boulder's 39,001 voters.

1984

### GOVERNMENT

#### CITY COUNCIL

Ruth Correll was re-elected mayor of Boulder for a fourth term. An expected challenge to her long-held seat failed to materialize when Councilmembers Spencer Havlick and Phil Stern did not show up for the biennial ceremony to swear in new members and elect one to serve as mayor.

Stern and Havlick had been Correll's most vocal critics. Stern wanted someone else to take the mayoral seat because he thinks Correll has taken the job beyond its largely ceremonial status. Havlick said he would support Correll's re-election only if she increased the efficiency of council meetings. (Camera, Jan. 3, Daryl Gibson)

Council meetings will be changed from 7 p.m. to 6 p.m. The agenda will be restructured so routine business will be taken care of during the first hour, leaving the time for citizen participation at 7 p.m. Rejected by council was a suggestion placing a three-minute limit on each citizen's participation. The present limit is five minutes.

The council petitioned President Reagan to get the U. S. military out of Central America (in accordance with the resolution passed by voters in the November election), and asked the Department of Defense to hold local hearings on the proposed placement of the MX missiles at Warren Air Force Base near Cheyenne. (Camera, Jan. 4, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder's Assistant City Manager Jim Piper was appointed acting city manager by the city council. He will replace City Manager Bob Westdyke who is resigning on Jan. 31 to take a job with NBI Inc. Piper started with the city as a management intern in 1970. (Camera, Jan. 18)

The council chose the consulting firm of Paul A. Reaume Associates to search for a new manager. (Camera, Jan. 25, Daryl Gibson)

In May, Mr. Piper was chosen by the council as the city manager from among four finalists. A nationwide search produced 14 candidates. The decision represented a compromise choice among Piper, David Knapp, Boulder's deputy manager, and Neal G. Berlin, city manager in Iowa City, Iowa. The fourth finalist was Judith A. Weiss, assistant to the manager in Pasadena, Calif. (Camera, May 7, Brighid Kelly)

Mayor Correll sent a letter to the embassy in Nicaragua asking for a "friendship city" in that country. The council agreed unanimously to participate in the exchange program developed by the Friendship City Project Inc. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 2, Leslie Jorgensen) Jalapa, a town in northern Nicaragua on the border with Honduras, was selected. (Camera, March 11, Doris Webster Havice)

A resolution passed on June 5 recognized the city of Dushanbe, U.S.S.R. as a sister city of Boulder.

The most recent of the perennial complaints about Boulder's non-

annexation of the residential area of Gunbarrel has come from the county Planning Commission.

Gunbarrel is home to more than 6500 people. The city receives an average of \$200,724 a month from Gunbarrel area businesses and industries, including IBM. The commercial and industrial land was annexed to Boulder after Gunbarrel residents discussed incorporation as a separate city in 1975.

In an informal poll in August 1975, residents voted against annexation 915-137. In an official vote in November 1978, they rejected annexation 890-788. Less than a year later, annexation advocates hoped to hold another election, but that became impossible when the Boulder Country Club in 1980 refused to sign a petition approving an election.

The country club is a key link to annexation. Without it, Gunbarrel does not have the land required to link it to the city. State law requires that owners of property worth \$200,000 or more in an area to be annexed must sign a petition approving an election.

County Commissioner Buz Smith, whose district includes Gunbarrel, has criticized the city for annexing areas that would benefit its tax base and leaving the rest for maintenance by the county. (Camera, Feb. 5, Sally McGrath)

A key element to the annexation issue is how Boulder's growth limitation ordinance would affect projects under way in the Gunbarrel area. As long as Gunbarrel remains unincorporated, building permits are acquired fairly readily in the county. But if and when Gunbarrel comes into the city, developers must compete on a point system for a reduced number of permits. (Times-Call, March 31-Apr. 1, Jean Hanley)

Mayor Correll, in a guest editorial to the Camera on November 7,

stated that council policy does not endorse forced annexation. She includes the incentives promised by the city in the Nov. 1, 1978 annexation election as well as those in the aborted 1980 campaign. Since that time the city has taken over the direct provision of water and sewer services following the dissolution of the Boulder Water and Sanitation District, so that present utility rates paid by Gunbarrel residents are the same as those paid by in-city residents.

The city council approved changes to the city's growth management ordinance that will make it easier for builders of single-family detached homes to obtain building permits. The decision concludes nearly five months of bitter fighting between the city and builders after the 1983 quota of permits ran out. Only 20 builders of the 39 competing for 1984's supply received permits. Two of the losers were so angry that they decided to sue the city. Those lawsuits are now pending in District Court. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 2, Kathleen Smith)

The council changed growth management rules to exempt from building permit competition the first 30 units in a landmark building remodeled as residences each year. The change is designed to retain residential use of landmarks. (Camera, Feb. 22, Brighid Kelly)

Mount St. Gertrude Academy was given landmark status by the council. Under changed growth management rules, developers planning to turn the main building into 17 condominiums will not have to compete for building permits. (Camera, April 4, Brighid Kelly)

Plans to build 116 apartments on a 14-acre site at Folsom Street and Valmont Road (the old trout farm) were rejected by the city council. (Camera, March 21, Brighid Kelly)

The Crystal Square hotel and retail complex on the southwest corner of 15th and Spruce Streets was approved despite the concerns of residents for the nearby Whittier neighborhood. The opponents said the five-story structure is too big and will add to parking and traffic problems in the downtown area. Proponents argued that the project would provide an activity center for the east end of the mall, would preserve the Boulder Theater and include a parking plan that would ease the crowded situation. (Camera, Oct. 3, Linda Cornett)

The council endorsed \$6 million in development revenue bonds for the expansion of Synergen Inc. They may be the last company to receive easy approval. In 1985, projects will be required to compete with one another for a limited number of bonds as stipulated by recent federal legislation. (Times-Call, Nov. 14, Juliet Bourne)
