The city council approved a request from city officials to file suit against the county in an attempt to overturn the county's approval to expand a subdivision one and one-half miles above Boulder Reservoir. City Manager Jim Piper said he fears the subdivision, because it does not have an adequate sewage treatment plant, might pollute the city's water supply. (Camera, Dec. 19, Linda Cornett)

Building a \$3.6 million hydroelectric plant and spending \$4.1 million to improve Barker Dam gained approval from a unanimous city council. City officials have negotiated a complex plan with Public Service Company of Colorado that would increase the amount of water Boulder could store in the reservoir and would produce electric power from that same water as it comes into the Betasso Water Treatment Plant. (Camera, March 14, Brighid Kelly)

The council set May 15 for an election on financing the hydroelectric

plant. Although property taxes will back the general obligation bonds, the city would pay the debt by selling the electricity the small power plant produces.

The council unanimously approved selling 15-year revenue bonds to finance \$4.1 million which will help repair Barker Dam. City water and wastewater income will be used to pay off the bonds. (Camera, April 4, Brigid Kelly)

Cultural arts in Boulder gained support when the city council approved a \$375,000 donation to the University of Colorado Macky Auditorium renovation fund and a \$100,000 cultural arts center study. In exchange, the city, within limits, can use the auditorium and other campus buildings. The university also deeded the Kids' Fishing Pond, near Sixth Street and Canyon Boulevard, to the city. The council also earmarked \$20,000 for improvements to the Boulder Center for Visual Arts. (Denver Post, March 28, Alicia Leppert)

A proposed \$14 million Boulder cultural complex in the 1700 block of 13th Street was approved, but the council delayed a decision on how to pay for it. (Camera, Sept. 19, Linda Cornett)

In the April 5 Camera, Brigid Kelly reported that the council had approved awarding \$61,000 for 36 arts grants.

The council passed a resolution honoring local architect and former Deputy Mayor Charles Haertling for his dedicated service and work toward saving and enhancing the natural beauty of Boulder. (Camera, March 14, Brigid Kelly)

The council banned the use of animal traps without the permission of the landowner. The action amends the 1982 ban on leg-hold traps and

effectively prevents trapping on city land. (Denver Post, April 11, Alicia Leppert)

The council passed a \$58.3 million budget for 1985 despite dissenting votes by two councilmen who opposed the city's investment in the proposed Bureau for Conference Services and Cultural Affairs. The Bureau, proposed by the Chamber of Commerce and backed by arts groups, would operate from the chamber office. (Camera, Oct. 17, Linda Cornett)

The layoff of nearly 2,000 high technology workers from Boulder Valley firms will cost the city about \$400,000 in sales tax revenue in 1985, according to unofficial estimates. City Budget Director Steve Fisher said he is confident the loss can be covered by the \$1.4 million reserve in the city's general fund budget. (Camera, Nov. 11, Linda Cornett)

A food tax refund program initiated by Boulder voters 17 years ago was eliminated by the city council.

The decision means city residents will not get \$7.56 each this year to compensate them for the sales tax which is imposed by the city on food purchases. Instead, the council voted to put the refund money into a program which refunds taxes only to low-income people.

The food tax refund was a part of the 1967 election when voters added an extra cent to the city sales tax to be earmarked for greenbelt and transportation expenses.

A second refund program was created in 1973 to give a portion of property taxes back to low-income people.

The food tax refunds would have cost the city \$160,000 this year and the property tax refunds, \$100,000. Under the new program, all \$260,000 will go to low-income people.

Families must make less than \$15,212 and individuals less than \$10,140 to qualify for refunds. (Camera, Feb. 8, Daryl Gibson)

#### CITY MANAGER

After seven and one-half years as city manager of Boulder, Bob Westdyke resigned to take a job in private industry. He was appointed after the council fired City Manager Twitchell in 1976.

Westdyke's management style earned him a reputation for quickly carrying out the will of the council and only rarely interjecting his own opinions. (Camera, Jan. 10, Daryl Gibson)

The council chose Assistant City Manager Jim Piper to replace Mr. Westdyke. Mr Piper eliminated the position of deputy city manager and promoted David Knapp to assistant city manager. In his new position, Mr. Knapp will supervise the administrative services, public works/utilities, and planning departments. (Camera, May 30)

Peggy Wrenn was named as a second assistant city manager to be responsible for the departments of police, fire, parks and recreation, human services, real estate and open space, and the library. (Camera, Oct. 20, Linda Cornett)

Although the city has had a central mini-computer since 1975, only now are departments preparing to reduce the "paper blizzard" with micro-computers on many desks. The data processing staff decided in 1982 that the micros were "mature" enough for professional office use according to Director Marty Grisham.

Through a bid process, four vendors were chosen and then put to a test by the staff who would be using the computers. The winner was Convergent Technology, marketed by M/A-COM Alanthus. In early 1985 there will be 50

terminals in city offices. (Camera, Oct. 17, Linda Cornett)

### ELECTIONS

On May 15, voters approved a \$3.6 million bond issue to construct a three-megawatt hydroelectric power plant at the Betasso Water Treatment Plant. The bonds, although backed by property taxes, will be paid for during their 20-year term through the sale of electricity generated by the turbine.

Ninety percent of 2,324 people who voted (2,091 to 226) approved the measure. Only 5.5 percent of registered voters turned out for the election, the lowest count city officials could remember. (Camera, May 16, Brighid Kelly)

At the state level two ballot issues affected the local scene. One required that all petitions be signed by "registered" rather than "qualified" electors, and that five percent of registered electors are needed to initiate local government issues. An amendment to the state statutes allowed voters to register by using the same application used to obtain a Colorado Driver's License and changed the deadline for registration from 32 to 25 days before an election.

# CHAPTER II

## HEALTH AND SANITATION

1980

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

A new contract, increasing the price of the Windy Gap water project by \$9.5 million, calls for the five cities and the Platte River Power Authority (who purchased the share originally held by Fort Collins) to pay for the Azure Reservoir and hydroelectric power plant. The added expense would be repaid through the sale of power from the plant. (Camera, May 28, Daryl Gibson)

The agreement, averting a lengthy court battle, was reached between the Northern Colorado Water Conservancy District and western slope interests. (Times-Call, May 21, Rebecca Cantwell)

The average residential sewer bill in Boulder will go up 90 cents, or 20 percent, to complete a two-part rate increase implemented by the city council last week. Last May, sewer bills for the average residential user went up about 100 percent or \$2.15 per month.

The rate increase pays for improvements to the 75th Street wastewater treatment plant and balances out the utility burden traditionally carried by commercial and industrial sewer users. Federal guidelines require each sewer user to pay a fair share of the bills.

Improvements to the treatment plant are nearly complete. They include odor control and sludge handling facilities which cost about \$6 million. (Camera, Jan. 3, Daryl Gibson)

City Utilities Director Roger Hartman recommended \$17.5 million in

improvements at the wastewater treatment plant. He wants council to endorse a revenue bond for advanced treatment to meet 1983 federal Environmental Protection Agency clean water standards.

Traditionally cities must come up with 20 percent of the price, but recent Congressional efforts to balance the federal budget have left Hartman and others in doubt about the future of wastewater grants.

The \$17.5 million would pay for not only the plant improvements but also for a sludge injection program, possibly on city greenbelt property.

City Manager Bob Westdyke explained the idea could kick off the long-discussed notion that crops for gasahol production could be grown on the greenbelt.

Boulder turns out 60,000 gallons of sludge each day. It is trucked to the Marshall Landfill or used as fertilizer in city parks. (Camera, May 28, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Utilities Department, which has operated on a pay-as-you-go basis for most major construction projects, now needs a \$5 million loan to pay for wastewater treatment plant improvements ordered by the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA).

In 1972 and 1977, the EPA issued water-quality standards for discharging treated sewage into streams such as Boulder Creek. The city put together what it thought would be a \$14 million plan to meet the requirements.

By 1978, it became apparent that the cost would be closer to \$17 million. A utility-rate study commissioned by the city that year showed the work could be paid for without loans if utility rates went up 60 percent by January 1980.

But delays by the EPA in approving the plan and deciding which regulations affected Boulder dragged out nearly 26 months and pushed the construction cost up to \$20.3 million.

So, the city has a mid-1981 deadline to come up with \$5 million and Hartman will suggest approval of a 20-year bond issue as the source.

(Camera, November 16, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder may not need a \$5 million bond issue ... after all, according to city Utilities Director Roger Hartman.

(He) said ... that the state Water Quality Control Commission is on the verge of rewriting ammonia discharge standards for Boulder Creek which could cut the cost of \$20.3 million in needed sewer plant improvements to \$6 million or \$8 million.

The money from the EPA is funneled to Boulder through grants to the state. The Water Quality Control Commission sets the pollutant discharge standard for streams in the state and then allocates EPA money to various cities and counties to improve their sewage treatment plants to meet the standards. (Camera, December 12, Daryl Gibson)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

Last August, the city had promised Eco-Cycle \$35,000 in Community Development funds. The Department of Housing and Urban Development ruled that the recycling group did not qualify for the CD grant. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 4)

The council tentatively approved support of Eco-Cycle in August, but asked for a consultant to study the feasibility of continuing the recycling operation.

The consultant's report did not come back to the council until last month. In the meantime, Eco-Cycle all but shut down its operations for lack of money.

The council approved a \$175,000 donation (the \$140,000 tentatively approved last year plus \$35,000 to make up for the loss of CD funds) if Pete Grogan could come up with money from other sources to pay off outstanding debts within six months. They agreed to let him begin construction of a recycling plant immediately. (Camera, Feb. 6, Daryl Gibson)

Eco-Cycle failed to meet the deadline for raising the money and the city was faced with taking over its debts and the operation. City Manager Westdyke suggested council give Grogan more time. (Camera, Sept. 3, Daryl Gibson)

Eco-Cycle held a grand opening ceremony at its new \$300,000 processing facility at 5030 Pearl Street. (Camera, Oct. 10, Todd Malmsbury)

The Boulder County Commissioners increased rates at the Marshall Landfill. "Part of the increase will go to Browning-Ferris, the landfill operator, to cover increased costs of operation. And part will go into a fund to cover leachate and any other hazardous waste problems the landfill may create." (Camera, May 21, Sharon Massingill)

In December, the council cut the \$5,000 tree pickup service from its

budget. (Camera, Dec. 31, Daryl Gibson)

### AIR POLLUTION

In March, the decision of the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) to withhold \$301 million in federal money from Colorado was a result of the state legislature's failure to meet a March 1 deadline for approval of an automobile emission controls program. Colorado had received two previous extensions on the original 1975 deadline for compliance.

The EPA sanctions could halt the upgrading of the Boulder sewage treatment plant, a project using \$8.6 million in federal funds. City officials fear the completion of the Foothills Parkway may also be in jeopardy. (Colorado Daily, March 3 and March 10, Patrick Summers)

In May, the Colorado legislature and the EPA finally reached agreement on a plan to reduce air pollution along the Front Range. The EPA regional director said when the compromise legislation has been signed, his agency immediately will remove sanctions that could have cost the state millions in federal grants.

The bill will require annual emissions tests for all vehicles in a nine county area. Some legislators and health officials are concerned that simply adjusting polluting vehicles will not reduce auto emissions by the necessary 25 percent. (Camera, May 7, Todd Malmsbury)

1981

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

The Northern Colorado Water Conservancy District agreed to pay western slope interests \$10.2 million in exchange for the Windy Gap project.

The pact eliminates the obligation to build a storage reservoir and hydroelectric plant for use by western slope communities. Recent studies found there wasn't a market for the power the project would have produced.

Instead the cash will collect interest until officials decide what kind of water project to build. (Times-Call, March 26, Dave Downey)

Bonds amounting to \$84.25 million are sold, construction contracts awarded, and all systems are go on the Windy Gap project. Included in its current cost are a payment of \$550,000 to the U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service to help preserve two endangered fish species downstream, \$120,000 to make sure no archeological damage is done, \$450,000 for water and sewer improvements to Hot Sulphur Springs, about \$500,000 to improve rangelands and diversion facilities for downstream users, and \$25,000 to Grand County for a salinity study. (Times-Call, June 25, Rebecca Cantwell)

Sixty percent of the city's water demand is south of Baseline. In the summer it is necessary to move water quickly to that part of town. A trench for a pipeline from 10th and Cascade through Chautauqua Park to Chautauqua Reservoir is the first phase of construction which will eventually link Chautauqua and Kohler Reservoirs. (Camera, Feb. 15)

With mountain snowpack the lowest on record and runoff in mountain streams almost nonexistent, a backup water supply at Boulder Reservoir will provide the city with much of its water this summer.

In most years, the treatment plant at the reservoir northeast of Boulder doesn't even operate, explained Roger Hartman, city utilities director. Normally, the Betasso Plant west of the city provides all of Boulder's water. Last summer, the reservoir plant operated for only three weeks, and the previous summer not at all. This year the plant began operating April 1.

The plant, with a capacity of 9 million gallons a day, cannot produce enough water to serve the entire city. In the winter, 12 million gallons are used every day, and summer consumption can reach 40 million gallons.

The city is urging its residents to conserve water voluntarily this summer. (Camera, May 13, Todd Malmsbury) In the News From City Hall in July, it was reported that water use was 30 percent below that of last year.

The council approved the extension of a water main along east Arapahoe Avenue to serve the Boulder Valley School District service center, the Vo-Tech school and neighboring properties. (Times-Call, Oct. 21, Sue Diehl)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

City Utilities Director Roger Hartman reported in detail Eco-Cycle's financial problems. Both they and the city are threatened with liens from unpaid contractors.

The recycling agency used its \$175,000 donation from the city to set up a cardboard baling machine. It failed to meet a deadline to raise another \$90,000 as its share of the baler cost and the city gave the agency more time.

A neighborhood program for curbside pickup of household trash which is recyclable was begun last fall and has about 5.5 percent of the city's households participating. The low participation is a major disappointment because as many as 26 percent of city residents were participating in 1978. (Camera, Jan. 7, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder County Commissioners handed Eco-Cycle a reprieve by granting an extension of an already extended \$35,000 loan (for the

corporation's baler and other equipment) and then giving the recyclers a \$15,000 "grant" which will be repaid if the organization receives money from surplus solid waste funds available before the end of the year. (Camera, Oct. 9, Margie McAllister)

Eco-Cycle is the largest non-profit recycler in the United States, but the company has never operated in the black. Recently they had to cut back from twice a month to once monthly curbside pickup service because of low participation and high costs. Environmentally conscious Boulder has not rallied behind recycling. (Camera, Nov. 30, Daryl Gibson)

Rates charged at the Marshall Landfill are going up. The landfill operator, Browning-Ferris Industries, has asked the county to approve a 40 percent rate hike. A special surcharge to fund recycling and resource-recovery programs is scheduled to go into effect also. Money from this extra fee will be used to help such organizations as Eco-Cycle. (Camera, July 11, Sharon Gillen)

### AIR POLLUTION

For the second summer in a row, Boulder's air has stayed within the standards for ozone. Ozone forms primarily as a byproduct of the internal combustion engine. The cleaner air is due in part to the reduction in gasoline consumption and to cleaner-running cars that produce less emissions. But air pollution officials say most of the reduction is due to more favorable weather. (Camera, July 29, Todd Malmsbury)

Other pollutants, such as carbon monoxide, are on the increase. Also, the 1979 amendment to the Clean Air Act relaxed by 50 percent the permissible levels of ozone. (Colorado Daily, Aug. 7, S. K. Levin)

The Valmont Power Plant is the source of as much as 90 percent of

locally produced air pollution. The county is urging the Public Service Company to correct the problem.

The pollution control equipment at Valmont, installed in 1973, frequently breaks down. Costly new equipment will only reduce the visible pollutants which are not a health hazard at their present levels. (Camera, Dec. 7, Todd Malmsbury)

1982

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

The remains of ancient Indian huts were unearthed during the construction of the Windy Gap water diversion project. Archeologists have been working since February to uncover the remains of mud and stick buildings constructed 4,000 to 6,000 years ago. The site is important because it indicates that the ancient people built permanent structures and were not just nomadic hunters and gatherers.

By the time bulldozers return to continue their work, the scientists will be able to collect as much information as they had planned from the materials. The Northern Colorado Water Conservancy District has helped pay for archeological studies in the area. (Times-Call, July 3-4, Jim Towle)

Windy Gap is actually more like a diversion project than a reservoir. The dam itself is 27 feet high and 5,580 feet long. It will impound 320 acre-feet of water.

In addition to increasing water supplies, it will increase the energy capacity of the Colorado-Big Thompson Project by 95 million kilowatt hours annually. (Times-Call, Aug. 28-29, Kep Pettit)

An increase in operating costs for water treatment and proposed plant improvements over the next five years, combined with 10 years under the old water rate, are causing an impending deficit in city water utility funds. The council approved a measure to lower the minimum monthly service charge, but to increase the charge per 1,000 gallons from the current 46 cents to 66 cents on June 1 and to 77 cents on Jan. 1. (Camera, April 7, Mike Stone)

The city council accepted the arguments of environmentalists and voted not to run a water line along the cheapest route (through mountain park land) between Chautauqua and Kohler Reservoirs. Water customers will pay a surcharge of two cents for every 1,000 gallons of water as the price for protecting Enchanted Mesa from the scar of a buried city waterline. (Camera, July 8, Daryl Gibson)

The expected two cent increase to pay for the new water line will not be needed. The original cost was estimated at nearly \$1 million. Construction bids came in lower than expected and the line can be built for \$905,000. That savings, coupled with favorable bids on other utility construction projects, will save enough money to eliminate the need for the increase. (Camera, Dec. 9, Daryl Gibson)

A new industrial wastewater law approved by Boulder City Council is more stringent than federal Environmental Protection Agency standards. The law could affect 13 local companies including Arapahoe Chemicals and IBM. (Times-Call, April 21)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

Suspected carcinogens are leaching from both the current and inactive Marshall Landfill sites and entering the Louisville Community Ditch, a major source of Louisville's drinking water. The U.S. Environmental

Protection Agency and the state will request money from the EPA "superfund" to study ways to clean up the landfill. (Denver Post, Oct. 22, Kaye Bache-Snyder)

Marshall Landfill has been the state's top priority for cleanup for almost three years. It is now listed as one of 418 sites nationwide eligible for federal cleanup money. The state or Boulder County must contribute a share of the cost.

Boulder's garbage and sewage sludge were dumped at the old Marshall Landfill during the 1950's and 1960's. Health officials are uncertain exactly what else ended up in the old dump, although they suspect some hazardous wastes may be there.

A partial solution to the immediate problem could be a water line between Marshall Lake and Louisville that would bypass Community Ditch. (Camera, Dec. 21, Todd Malmsbury)

#### AIR POLLUTION

Measurements at a NOAA field site on Gunbarrel Hill show a steady decline in air quality from 1967 to 1980. The decline is attributed to the expanding Boulder Valley population and its energy and transportation needs. William Cobb of the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration said, "Perhaps the factor with the greatest impact has been the number of motor vehicles registered in Boulder County which ... has increased by about 10,000 vehicles annually." (Colorado Daily, Jan. 22/23)

Boulder police will begin enforcing a new state auto emissions law April 1. Officers will have to check the validation sticker on the rear license plate, then look on the windshield for the emissions sticker which indicates the car has passed an inspection test. (Camera, March 26, Betsy Howard)

With fuel bills in an upward spiral, homeowners are installing fireplaces and wood stoves to keep heating bills in check.

However, recent studies have shown wood is not an environmentally conscious choice. Included in the haze of wood smoke are potentially dangerous compounds that may affect human health.

A study conducted for the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency found that for the same energy output, a wood burning stove can produce as much as 1,000 time more carbon monoxide than heating with natural gas. (Camera, Jan. 22, Todd Malmsbury)

City Councilman Phil Stern is among an increasing number of public officials concerned about wood smoke pollution. Joe Palombo of the Colorado Air Quality Control Division feels that wood stoves and fireplaces will be as significant a problem in the future as autos used to be before pollution controls and emission inspections. (Camera, Nov. 18, Todd Malmsbury)

1983

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

Cities in the Windy Gap water project are trying to find ways of meeting the payments that will come due in 1985. Each city is to receive 8,000 acre-feet in an average yield year. (An acre-foot is the amount of water needed to flood one acre to a 1-foot depth.)

Originally six cities were involved in the project, but several years ago Fort Collins transferred all of its rights to the Platte River Power Authority. Two other cities, Estes Park and Loveland, each have sold half

their rights to Platte River. The price tag for each one-sixth share will be \$1.6 million per year for 30 years. In years when water is delivered to the Front Range, that amount will jump to \$2.4 million.

Boulder has factored the cost of the project into the city's capital improvements program, according to Roger Hartman, director of public utilities. He said Boulder probably won't need the water until 1990. Some will be used directly and some will be stored in Boulder Reservoir.

Boulder is considering selling the second use of the water (a step not allowed with water originating on the eastern slope.)

John Sayre, attorney for the Windy Gap Project, is looking into the possibility of refinancing the bonds. (Camera, Feb. 21, Rusty Pierce)

Gunbarrel residents, members of the Boulder Valley Water and Sanitation District, voted to turn their water and sewer system over to the city of Boulder.

The vote was 386 to 31 in favor of the transfer. About 2,500 property owners were eligible to vote in the special election.

The approval by the district members settles a 3-year-old lawsuit against the city by 25 of those members who objected to the city's practice of charging more for water and sewer services to customers outside the city limits than it does for city residents.

Turning the system over to the city and lowering Gunbarrel rates to the same as those charged in the city will mean the lawsuit will not be pursued by the district.

The election means the city now owns the water and sewer lines and the district is dissolved.

To compensate the city for the loss of revenues that will come from lowering the rates in Gunbarrel, the district will pay the city \$227,830

which was left in the district's treasury. (Camera, March 23, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder has the third lowest water rates in the metro area according to a survey released by the city of Westminster.

Roger Hartman said the \$188 average annual rate "sounds a little high, but not outrageous." He said if Boulder has one of the lowest rates, the reason is due to excellent long-term water planning, good fiscal management, and far-sighted land use planning.

Hartman reported that Boulder was one of the first cities in the state to begin charging a tap fee for developers in 1958. Tap fees are charged for hooking up to the system. Water system development is done on a pay-as-you-go basis with developers footing the bills for extended water service.

Boulder has enough water for anticipated growth up to the year 2020. Its system uses gravity in distributing water, eliminating high energy costs for pumping water to users. The city's high quality water also requires less treatment which helps keep costs down. (Denver Post, June 22, Frank Mullen)

Boulder Public Works Director Andy Hollar, the man who spearheaded construction on the Downtown Mall, has been moved to the job of director of utilities, a division within the Public Works Department. Former director Roger Hartman left last summer to take another job.

Until a new public works director is hired, Assistant City Manager Jim Piper will supervise the department. (Camera, Dec. 3, Daryl Gibson)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

In February, arsonists caused an estimated \$10,000 damage to Eco-Cycle's vehicles and their contents. Director Pete Grogan believes it was

an act of vandalism rather than one of animosity. (Colorado Daily, March 1, Kurt Chandler)

Last year, despite recycling more material than ever, depressed prices for recyclables still left Eco-Cycle \$30,000 short of the break-even point. Pete Grogan thinks participation may climb this year above the current 33 percent. And a county surcharge fund collected at the Marshall Landfill may help Eco-Cycle move towards self-sufficiency. (Camera, March 2, Todd Malmsbury)

During the first four months of 1983, Eco-Cycle's operating expenses totaled \$190,855. But, thanks primarily to rising prices paid for recyclables, the organization's income reached \$193,318.

Since 1977, Eco-Cycle has stayed alive with grants and loans. The city and the county have provided more than \$300,000 of that. Other factors have been new trucks donated by an area business and a bigger volunteer network to encourage recycling. The Apple Corporation has donated a \$6,000 business computer. (Camera, May 28, Todd Malmsbury)

While the Boulder County Commissioners attempt to solve pollution problems at the Marshall Landfill, its past and current owners are throwing charges and countercharges over who is to blame.

Jim Kean, operator of the site until 1975, blames septic dumpings for a serious leachate problem. He referred to 2 million gallons of sewage wastes dumped by Boulder into the landfill in the early 1970s.

A clay liner was supposed to be installed over a fault area and aquifer in the active section of the landfill, according to an engineering plan submitted to the county before excavation of the active site began in 1974.

A Browning-Ferris manager maintains his firm followed the engineering

plan, but the area where a liner was supposed to be installed already was covered with trash when they bought the business.

The county commissioners agreed to begin negotiations with present owners on ways to protect Louisville's water supply from leaching chemicals. So far, no pollutants have been detected in the ditch, which leads from Marshall Lake.

The site was an improvised dumping ground in the 1940s where abandoned cars, broken appliances and worn furniture were tossed. The site officially became a landfill in 1965. The county began to regulate it in the 1970s. (Denver Post, Feb. 16, Dave Price)

The county approved expansion of Marshall Landfill, thus prolonging the site's life by six and a half years. Browning-Ferris Industries predicts its drainage system will prevent the creation of the leachate formed when water seeps through accumulated wastes. (Denver Post, April 6, Dave Price)

#### AIR POLLUTION

The Denver metropolitan area, including Boulder County, was put on notice by the Environmental Protection Agency that federal grants may be withheld if air quality in the area does not improve.

A state air pollution official said he will recommend that Boulder be split off from the metro area and be included in a different air quality region, thus avoiding the loss of federal money.

The sanctions against the area may never happen because state officials feel they have a plan that will bring the area into compliance by the 1987 deadline. (Camera, Feb. 2, Todd Malmsbury)

For the first time in three years, Boulder's air failed to meet

federal ozone pollution standards. Three violations in July and August show that even if Boulder were not included in the Denver Air Quality Control Region, the city would still be considered a "non-attainment" area. That designation requires industries to meet more stringent pollution control requirements.

Standards for carbon monoxide haven't been violated for nearly two years in Boulder. (Camera, Sept. 25, Todd Malmsbury)

Valmont Power Plant is installing new pollution equipment to filter fly ash emitted by the coal-fired plant. The old electrostatic precipitator, a scrubber and a mechanical dust collector, installed in 1973, has repeatedly malfunctioned. The new process is going to cost \$17 million and is being financed with tax-exempt, 30-year pollution control bonds. It will pull emissions through hundreds of filter bags, which will be emptied periodically and disposed of in a pit on the plant site. (Camera, Dec. 7, Todd Malmsbury)

Councilman Phil Stern has read a recent state report on the pollution caused by woodburning. He has suggested some solutions for the city where approximately two-thirds of the residents have woodburning appliances.

He wants either the Boulder Energy Office or the County Health Department to gather data and then begin a program to educate users about more efficient and less polluting ways to use their appliances.

He also wants the city to enact some legislation either limiting the number of wood stoves per dwelling and/or only allowing new appliances in well-insulated homes. (Colorado Daily, April 20, Kathleen Smith)

Councilman Stern, in a second attempt to introduce city regulations to control pollution by wood burning devices, said if he had his way, installing a fireplace in an individual condominium may some day be illegal

in Boulder.

Even in new houses, builders would have to meet stringent energy efficiency standards and install only approved wood stoves.

A researcher at the National Center for Atmospheric Research found that if wood stove emissions were cut in half, the brown cloud could be 11 percent less by the year 2000.

A new Boulder County brochure "How Should You Burn Wood?" has met the educational goal suggested by Stern. (Camera, Nov. 18, Todd Malmsbury)

1984

### WATER SUPPLY AND SEWAGE DISPOSAL

Boulder Transportation Director David Baskett was named acting public works director to replace Jim Piper, who will become acting city manager. Baskett has been director of transportation since 1982. (Camera, Jan. 31)

The shakeup in Boulder's Public Works Department continued with the resignation of Ray Ingraham, director of development and inspection services .

A private consultant was hired to help with management of the department. (Camera, Feb. 15, Daryl Gibson)

In May, the first issue of the Public Works newsletter announced that Tina Christiansen had been hired to direct the Development and Inspection Services Division.

David Baskett resigned as acting public works director and accepted a job with the Regional Transportation District. (Camera, Nov. 30)

Richard Goecke, the man hired after a 12-month search for Boulder's public works director, backed out of the job just two weeks before he was

due to start it. He was chosen from 78 candidates around the country.  
(Camera, Dec. 21, Linda Cornett)

Half of Boulder's famous drinking water will not come from the mountains this year. The Public Works Department will switch on its Boulder Reservoir treatment plant to supplement the mountain water supply. February 1 is the earliest date the city has had to use its alternative plant. It is necessary this year because of the reduced capacity of two mountain reservoirs. The water in Barker Reservoir at Nederland and in Goose Lake in the city's watershed were lowered because their dams need repairs.

Only three areas of the city are not hooked into the system of pipes coming from Boulder Reservoir - Table Mesa, University Hill and Wonderland Hill. (Camera, Jan. 31, Daryl Gibson)

Water that has been sitting in Boulder Reservoir contains high amounts of dissolved minerals such as calcium and magnesium. "The water averages 1,200 parts per million of dissolved solids," said Rick Hay, an environmental supervisor for Boulder County. "Water from Betasso (where mountain water is treated) is near zero." Although it may be cloudy and may clog dishwashers, the "hard" water is not a health hazard. (Camera, Feb. 28, Todd Malmsbury)

The city guaranteed itself a steady flow of cash and a secure supply of water when it reached an agreement with the Public Service Company of Colorado.

The company agreed to pay Boulder more than \$600,000 annually for hydroelectric power the city plans to generate at the Betasso treatment plant.

In addition, Boulder will be guaranteed the long-term use of water it

stores in Barker Reservoir. The city now has only temporary rights to 8,000 acre-feet of water.

Boulder will pay \$3.3 million of the estimated \$4.9 million in repairs the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission has ordered for Barker Dam. Until the repairs are made, the reservoir is limited to two-thirds capacity. (Camera, Jan. 24, Todd Malmsbury)

#### REFUSE DISPOSAL

After four months of negotiations, Eco-Cycle and Western Disposal Service Inc. have reached an agreement. Western will sell Eco-Cycle all the recyclable goods that Western retrieves from its residential customers. Eco-Cycle will pay the trash hauler a percentage of what the non-profit organization receives for the goods. (Camera, Jan. 8, Lou Chapman)

After seven years of scraping by with leftover equipment and cash contributions from local governments, Eco-Cycle has finally turned a profit for an entire year.

During 1983, they recycled 12,000 tons of solid waste, more than any other community or municipal recycling program in the country. (Camera, March 1)

Eco-Cycle turned a profit of \$9,147 in 1983, and in the first six months of 1984 has a profit of \$31,370. Its outstanding debt is \$31,000.

Forty percent of the households in Boulder are regular contributors. In Longmont, where Eco-Cycle started a collection program six months ago, eight percent of the households are contributing.

City and county offices and several businesses are contributing by separating their waste paper. The high quality of office refuse brings a better price than recycled newspaper. (Camera, Nov. 18, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder County Commissioners approved a 13-percent increase in the cost of dumping trash at the Marshall Landfill, the first since December 1982.

Landfill Inc., a subsidiary of Browning-Ferris Industries, had requested a 31-percent increase.

Trash haulers, who account for 82 percent of the wastes dumped at Marshall, will pay a 20 cent increase per cubic yard of compacted trash. Haulers must also pay a 40 cent county surcharge to finance environmental monitoring and local recycling efforts. (Camera, March 21, Todd Malmsbury)

#### AIR POLLUTION

The city passed an ordinance that allows giving \$25 tickets to parked cars without emission inspection stickers. (Camera, March 14, Brigid Kelly)

Boulder violated federal ozone and carbon monoxide standards four times in 1983, sinking any chance that the Environmental Protection Agency will give its blessings to the city as an "attainment area".

Historically, Boulder has averaged one or two carbon monoxide violations per year and two or three ozone violations.

Boulder, along with Denver, must bring its air completely into compliance with federal standards by 1987 or face sanctions, including withholding of federal funds. (Camera, Sept. 19, Daryl Gibson)

The rest of Boulder County, except the city of Boulder and the Boulder-Denver Turnpike corridor, are attainment areas for carbon monoxide. Boulder County, except for the Rocky Mountain National Park, is a non-attainment area for ozone pollution.

Between November 15 and January 15, metropolitan area residents are

being asked not to drive one day a week. The push to clean up the brown cloud is part of an effort to bring the state into line with federal clean air standards. (Camera, Nov. 15)

A van-pool service between Boulder and Denver, organized three years ago, has announced it can no longer run because of a drop in riders. (Camera, Nov. 17, Todd Malmsbury)

The city, in an effort to help the Better Air campaign, will be offering incentives to participate in the program for motorists who use city parking lots. Those who have one passenger in their car will be able to park at half price. Those with two passengers or more will be able to park free. The incentives are good only from November 15 to January 15. (Camera, Nov. 18)

State health officials said that in the first two weeks of the no-drive program, there has been a two percent reduction in traffic. That is only one-fifth as large a drop as is necessary to meet federal air pollution standards. If the program fails and clean air regulations are violated after 1987, the state could lose up to \$200 million in federal money. (Camera, Nov. 29, Todd Malmsbury)

# CHAPTER III

## TRAFFIC ENGINEERING AND TRANSPORTATION

1980

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

In March, the city council formed a Goss-Grove street assessment district. Property owners will pay for 23 percent of the total, and the city transportation fund and community development block grants will pay for the rest.

The street design was selected after many meetings with citizens of the area. It includes diverters at intersections with 21st and 23rd streets to minimize through traffic between Canyon Boulevard and Arapahoe and to "preserve a compact neighborhood environment for Goss and Grove streets." (Camera, March 5, Daryl Gibson)

The cost of the Goss-Grove project is \$1.2 million. It includes the closure of parts of 19th and 20th streets to form mini-parks where streets used to be. Street widths will be adjusted to make them uniform and contractors will install sidewalks with curbs and gutters. (Camera, June 16, Daryl Gibson)

The project was completed in October. Because of the diverters and mini-parks, names of the streets were changed to Goss Circle West and East, and Grove Circle West and East. (Camera, Oct. 30, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder's Foothills Parkway suffered another setback in August.

The Colorado Highway Commission cut ... the parkway budget from \$16 million to \$13.5 million in the state's five year plan. It will not affect the construction schedule for the next three years, but the cutbacks will begin to show in the fourth and fifth years ... . The money was diverted to U.S. 287, the perilous route connecting Broomfield, Longmont and Fort Collins through eastern Boulder County. (The decision) ... will push the completion date of the parkway back ... to 1989. (Camera, Aug. 22, Daryl Gibson)

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of March 5 that figures collected by the public works staff show that 77 inches of snow fell between November and January.

The heavy moisture, the freeze-thaw cycle, sanding and salt have combined to destroy several Boulder intersections.

(Public Works Director) Hollar had budgeted ... to overlay ... and sealcoat ... miles (of streets), but some of that money may have to be diverted for major reconstruction of the intersections where overlay will not work.

One victim of the expenditures for the stormy winter was 19th Street. It was removed from the list of roads scheduled for major repairs when the budget took a beating. (Camera, June 20, Daryl Gibson)

The street lights of the city are owned by Public Service Co. The city pays to lease them, pays for installation of new lights and pays the utility bill. The light bill paid to PSC in 1980 will be \$487,000.

... the city light bill has increased 290 percent in the last five years ... . Only 25 percent ... actually pays for electricity. The other 75 percent goes for renting the system from PSC. (Camera, April 14, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder Transportation Director Bob Whitson wants the city to buy the street light system from Public Service Company and thinks the system could "be purchased for less than \$3 million. ... (He) thinks the reduced cost of lighting could quickly pay for the purchase price of the system." (Camera, July 14, Daryl Gibson)

In the Camera dated December 22, Daryl Gibson said:

The \$400,000 computer system (to synchronize Boulder traffic lights) was delivered to the city in September, two years after it was ordered and one year after it was promised.

The computer system gives the city the capability of automatically adjusting any single intersection to respond to traffic demands through sensors in the pavement and walk buttons which can be pushed by pedestrians.

Parts of four streets are synchronized by the computer - Broadway, Baseline, Arapahoe and 28th. Canyon Boulevard and the traffic loop around

the downtown mall still are regulated by an old electric system which has no computer.

The only major project left out of the supplemental budget this year is the \$700,000 the transportation department wanted for a pedestrian underpass beneath Broadway at College Avenue. (Camera, June 5, Daryl Gibson)

### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

"The Year of the Pedestrian" is the label given to 1980 by the transportation department. They are gearing up to provide necessities and amenities to make walking safer and more pleasurable. "The goal ... is to reduce the emphasis on cars and increase the emphasis on pedestrians until the two receive equal treatment." (Camera, March 16, Daryl Gibson)

In September, Barbara Doe, assistant to the transportation director, organized a pedestrian conference, the first of its kind in the country. Experts on pedestrian planning were invited to the one-day event at the University of Colorado. (Camera, Jan. 11, 1981, Daryl Gibson)

The Camera of June 26 published a bicycle map under the heading "To Bike or Not To Bike - Why?" Answers were given based on a survey done by Urban and Regional Planning Department students at the University of Colorado Denver Center.

The newest link in the bikeway system is the Wellman Canal route connecting east and northeast Boulder. It connects to the Centennial bikepath allowing bicyclists to ride from Cherryvale Road to the University of Colorado. (Camera, July 9, Todd Malmsbury)

#### PARKING

In May, the council approved the purchase of land for a downtown parking lot at 15th and Pearl streets.

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of August 20 and September 17:

Boulder city officials have turned down the low bidder on a new downtown parking terrace in favor of a company with a bid \$107,445 higher.

The contract is for a one-level parking terrace to be built above a new Regional Transportation District (RTD) bus station.

The Boulder City Council compared two construction bids ... and ... handed the contract to the highest bidder, NCE Construction Co. and Downing-Leach Associates architects.

The vote was a narrow 5-4 ... (with the dissentors wanting) ... to give the contract to Fagre Construction Company.

NCE was the choice of a bid review committee made up of city staffers and private citizens, but Fagre made an issue of the choice and accused the city of allowing irregularities in the bidding process.

Fagre had complained because NCE was not required to submit a bid bond as were the other bidders.

City Transportation Director Bob Whitson took the blame for the slipup and NCE has since submitted a bid bond.

RTD will pay the city \$1 million for the corner lot which is now the site of a ground-level city parking lot.

The council, meeting as the board of directors for the Central Area General Improvement District, approved its budget for 1981. The budget included a \$260,533 surplus which, according to Transportation Director Whitson, is needed to build up a reserve for parking improvements listed in a five-year capital improvements program.

Councilmen Dick Meckley and Greg Lefferdink refused to vote for the budget since they had not seen the five-year program nor authorized future projects.

City Attorney Joe de Raismes told the council that the law allows the district to carry over unused money from one year to the next as long as that reserve is going down and not up.

De Raismes said the council must have erred in the past to let the reserve build up to \$301,891 this year. (Camera, Oct. 22, Daryl Gibson)

More than three years of negotiating and countless legal setbacks

were ancient history ... when the Boulder City Council settled the purchase price of land for a University Hill parking lot.

... more than a year ago ... (the council decided) that the corner of 12th and Pleasant streets was the best spot.

Both property owners balked at the offer from the city.

The Fowler Trust, which owns part of the land, rejected an offer of \$183,000, and the city filed to condemn the land. While the case was pending in court, the city was given the right to pave the Fowler lot. ... the council agreed to pay the negotiated price of \$205,000.

To obtain the other half of the property, the city had to go to court to condemn the land and lost the case. District Court Judge William Neighbors ruled that the city cannot condemn land outside the University Hill Parking District for a parking lot.

Real Estate Director Jim Crain negotiated a price of \$125,000, which (the owners) the Boulder Townhouse Corp. accepted.

The half of the lot which was paved on the Fowler property has been operating at a loss ... because it requires a full-time attendant to take care of only 27 spaces. (Camera, Dec. 3, Daryl Gibson)

Able-bodied people who park their cars in spaces reserved for the handicapped will soon be getting messages placed on their windshields that will remind the driver that the space is reserved.

A new city ordinance gives the city the power to levy a fine for illegal parking in a handicapped space, but first the message approach will be used to inform motorists of the new law. (Camera, Nov. 19)

#### TRANSIT

Negotiations between the city and the Regional Transportation District (RTD) for construction of the new downtown bus terminal came to a standstill with the ouster early this year of the RTD executive director. The city owns the land at both the present terminal site at Ninth and Canyon and the proposed site at 14th and Walnut. (Camera, April 8, Daryl Gibson)

The RTD has agreed to pay the city \$1 million for the new terminal

site. The city has mailed out bid requests for a 100-car parking deck to be built on top of the terminal. The proposed site presently is occupied by a parking lot of the city's Central Area General Improvement District. (Camera, July 1, Thad Keyes)

RTD's board of directors has urged the Colorado General Assembly to authorize more money for the construction of light rail rapid transit. A proposed primary segment of 73 miles could, along with an expanded bus system, provide better transportation. (Times-Call, Feb. 29)

A question was put on the November 4 ballot which would authorize RTD to increase sales taxes by three-fourths of a cent to fund a light rail system. It offers to lift the RTD sales tax on food and utilities if the increase is granted. The increase would expire after 14 years. (Camera, June 11, Daryl Gibson) (This issue was defeated.)

Supporters of an elected rather than appointed RTD board of directors succeeded in their petition drive to place the item on the November ballot. (Camera, June 5) (The voters approved the change.)

Boulder County Commissioners strongly endorsed a rapid transit link between Boulder and Denver, citing the problems of air quality and the energy crisis as reasons for their approval. (Denver Post, Jan. 8, Jane Cracraft)

Boulder probably will not see a light rail track between the downtown mall and Crossroads very soon, but buses freed up by a light rail system in Denver could benefit Boulder. (Camera, June 11, Daryl Gibson)

City Transportation Director Bob Whitson said he is going ahead with a joint study with the Regional Transportation District to determine the need for light rail in Boulder. In a draft master plan, Pearl Street is named as the likely route for light rail; Canyon Boulevard was designated as the

best route for cars, and Walnut Street for pedestrians and bike traffic.  
(Camera, Nov. 10, Daryl Gibson)

Bus ridership has increased 45 percent during the last year, according to Charlotte Houston, chairman of the RTD's operations committee. The increases are occurring in peak hours between 6 and 8 a.m. and from 4 to 6 p.m. and have caused strains on the bus system's capacity. RTD has 127 buses on order, but a large number of the new coaches are needed to replace older buses. (Times-Call, Feb. 29)

A program providing curbside bus service to Boulder's senior citizens was started seven months ago. Senior Ride will pick up passengers at any senior center or private home if at least five passengers are available at each stop. Passengers pay 25 cents each way and trips must be within the city. (Camera, May 13, Patrick Carver)

A committee of 14 citizens was appointed by the city council to investigate the problem of noise from the Municipal Airport. (Camera, July 2)

1981

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

Transportation Director Bob Whitson announced his resignation to be effective December 11. Public Works Director Andy Hollar said he will appoint Traffic Engineer Dave Baskett as acting transportation director.  
(Camera, Nov. 18)

A member of the city's planning board has suggested that the city council form a citizens' advisory board to monitor the transportation

department. The planning board makes a cursory review of the department's five-year capital improvements program. Transportation is one of the biggest expenditures of money in the city, but unlike other departments, it has no citizen review board. (Camera, Nov. 19, Daryl Gibson)

State Senator Les Fowler introduced a resolution to rename the Boulder-Denver Turnpike the Roderick L. Downing Freeway. After Boulder Mayor Ruth Correll and the city council voiced their opposition, the resolution was withdrawn. Downing, a University of Colorado engineering professor, was a major force behind the construction of the turnpike. (Camera, May 8)

In December, a stone marker was placed at the site of the Boulder-Denver Turnpike tollhouse which honored Professor Downing and others who were instrumental in the development of the highway. (Camera, Dec. 23, Laurence T. Paddock)

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

Boulder will open its Second Annual Pedestrian Conference on October 11. Boulderites are encouraged to express their support of pedestrians and their rights. Another major theme of the festival is health and the individual benefits that can be gained through recreational and social walks. (Camera, Oct. 4)

Bicycle safety is the driving force behind programs in the city. There are 25 miles of facilities and plans for more on-street bike lanes "because they're safer", a new sign and striping program, and better cleanup of glass and nails in bike lanes.

About 50 of Boulder's busiest intersections have been fitted with

special bicycle detectors which sense the presence of a bicycle and change the light to green. (Camera, Aug. 2, Michael Ross)

Although bicycle registration is required, only about 1,500 bikes actually are registered. The council debated whether to eliminate it altogether or to charge a heftier fee that might prompt bicyclists to take the rules of the road more seriously. City Attorney de Raismes said a strong enforcement approach would not generate enough money to pay for police officers to patrol bicycling violations and might exacerbate some of the misunderstandings that already exist between bicyclists and law enforcers. (Camera, Aug. 12, Daryl Gibson)

#### PARKING

Boulder Traffic Engineer Dave Baskett says there are about 100 parking spaces reserved for handicapped people in the city and there is now in effect a \$15 fine for those cars which do not have or display a handicapped sticker or license plate and yet occupy those spaces. (Colorado Daily, March 10, Jack Pommer)

The new parking lot at 12th and Pleasant streets has a 50-car capacity, but the average number of cars parked there daily is only 15. Because of this, the University Hill General Improvement District suggested eliminating the parking attendant and installing meters.

The city paid \$330,000 for the land. It lowered the price of long-term parking permits from \$25 to \$12 per month and advertised extensively, but got very little response. (Camera, May 19, Daryl Gibson)

The new parking terrace at 14th and Walnut streets was opened to the public. It contains 100 spaces. The garage was built by NCE Construction Co. for \$809,403. (Camera, Aug. 17)

The Boulder City Council, acting as the board of directors for the downtown and University Hill improvement districts, approved an increase from 20 cents to 30 cents per hour at city meters. The quarterly permit rates for long-term parking at five city lots were also increased.

The 1982 revenue budget for the Central Area General Improvement District includes \$424,000 in parking fees and \$160,555 in property taxes. For the University Hill district, property tax income was projected at \$12,313 and parking revenue at \$93,000. (Camera, Oct. 7, Thad Keyes)

#### TRANSIT

The Colorado Society of Architects gave an honor award to the design of the RTD bus facility at 1707 Exposition Drive. The building houses administrative facilities and a garage and maintenance area for 96 buses. (Camera, Feb. 14)

The RTD Board of Directors approved an increase in fares that will double the cost of riding buses in Boulder and eliminate the "free" in the Boulder FreeB, the circulator route in downtown Boulder. (Camera, April 30)

The Boulder County Commissioners ratified the appointment of Roger Cracraft to the RTD Board of Directors. He will serve an 18-month term, replacing Jim Bowers who chose not to remain on the board. (Camera, June 19, Sharon Gillen)

The city of Boulder owns and operates Boulder Municipal Airport under a special-use permit granted by the county commissioners in 1975. The permit allows 210 tie-downs for airplanes based at the airport and an unlimited number for transient planes and those up for sale.

The city has allowed 321 tie-downs to be installed and the

commissioners gave the city six months to remove the extras. City Transportation Director Bob Whitson told the commissioners the city was forced to install the extra tie-downs by a court order. In 1976, one of the airport tenants sued the city when another tenant was brought into the airport and transportation officials attempted to reallocate the tie-downs. The issue was complicated by Federal Aviation Administration regulations which prohibit the city from limiting competition among the fixed-base operators which provide flight services at the airport.

A city task force has been grappling with the problem of airport nuisance complaints for the past year but has been unable to reach agreement. (Camera, April 21, Daryl Gibson)

According to a study paid for by the Boulder Chamber of Commerce, the Municipal Airport is inadequately managed, has antiquated equipment and offers insufficient space for the number of planes that could use it.

The airport saw 131,366 takeoffs and landings in 1980. The city collected \$27,000 in ownership and gasoline taxes and property rentals, and spent \$23,200 on operations. (Camera, May 10, Daryl Gibson)

1982

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

In April, the Boulder City Council voted to initiate condemnation proceedings to take 2,500 square feet of the property on the corner of Folsom Street and Canyon Boulevard.

The land is needed to widen the two streets to improve traffic flow resulting from the expansion of the Crossroads Mall.

City Real Estate Director Jim Crain began to negotiate for the property of eighty-year-old Annie Joratz nearly one year ago. She refused to accept certified letters, has not allowed city appraisers to come on her property and has not taken advantage of a city offer to pay for her own appraiser to put a value on the land. The city's appraisal is about \$27,000.

Councilmembers Boland and Stern voted against the condemnation. (Camera, April 7, Daryl Gibson)

Traffic Engineer Dave Baskett was appointed Boulder's transportation director. (Camera, June 17)

Boulder's Foothills Parkway has been nearly 30 years in planning and 10 years in construction. This December, when the parkway reaches past the half-way mark at Pearl Street, the finished product is still at best five years away.

Last year, for the first time, completion of the highway was knocked from priority one to priority six on the county commissioners' list of recommendations to the state Highway Commission. The competition for highway funds in the county almost always turns into a tug-of-war between Foothills Parkway and improvements to U.S. 287. South Boulder Road is also in need of funds. (Camera, Aug. 29, Daryl Gibson)

The county commissioners voted two to one to place the Parkway completion up to Valmont Road as their number one priority. Boulder officials had hoped the request to the state would be to complete it to the Longmont Diagonal. Second place priority was given to an environmental study of U.S. 287. (Camera, Nov. 16, Margie McAllister)

The city Transportation Department started widening Ninth Street from Portland Place to Maxwell Avenue. The project will straighten a dangerous curve and will provide for bicycle traffic and pedestrians. (Camera, Oct. 3)

The council voted to approve formation of an assessment district in the south Whittier neighborhood. Property owners will share the costs of upgrading streets and sidewalks. (Camera, Oct. 6)

Dead trees in the 28th Street median south of Arapahoe Avenue will be replaced and a new landscaping plan will be drawn. The trees died after the city spent \$90,000 in an experimental program to see if mature vegetation could be transplanted to give new landscaping a more finished look. The contractor on the project has agreed to replace at no cost the dead trees which amounted to 30 to 50 percent of those originally planted. (Camera, July 18)

Of the 100 trees planted to dress up the University Hill business district, 25 have died. The contractor will replace any casualties in the \$29,000 landscaping project. (Camera, Oct. 12, Daryl Gibson)

The new alignment for Pearl Street between 30th and 47th streets will have a concrete surface because of its durability as well as the increasing cost of asphalt. Mapleton Avenue is one of the few concrete streets in the city. (Camera, Aug. 11)

Boulder has been phasing in a \$400,000 computerized traffic light synchronization system for four years. The latest phase covers the downtown mall loop.

Dave Baskett and Traffic Engineer Steve Jepson said the system has improved traffic flow. Average daily traffic on 28th Street has increased

from 28,000 to 38,000 cars in the last three years, while traffic speed has remained constant.

The system monitors itself through cables beneath intersections which produce electric magnetic fields capable of detecting cars, bicycles, motorcycles and even "a pedestrian with a large belt buckle." (Camera, Nov. 27, Colin Reid)

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

Boulder's third annual pedestrian conference will be addressing linkages between commercial districts, neighborhoods and open space. (Colorado Daily, Oct. 6)

Traffic signals in the city have recently been fine-tuned to detect the presence of bicycles. Last year the Transportation Division prepared 38 signals and this year 51 were marked with a white "X" where detectors are most sensitive. By stopping on the mark, cyclists should be able to change the signal to green within two minutes. (Camera, May 30)

The transportation department has constructed a \$60,000 bike path between the Boulder Public Library and the Justice Center on the northern edge of Boulder Creek. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 24, Kathleen Smith)

#### PARKING

The city council approved higher rates for hourly parking and long-term parking permits for 1983. The budget for the downtown district was set at \$1.2 million and for the Hill district at \$149,402. (Camera, Oct. 6, Daryl Gibson)

#### TRANSIT

The Amalgamated Transit Union and the Regional Transportation District ratified a new three-year contract and ended a 26-day-old bus strike. (Camera, Aug. 8, Susie Volek)

The council adopted a new ordinance which outlaws the use of motorized hang gliders or ultralights at the airport. (Camera, Aug. 4, Daryl Gibson)

Pilots call Boulder Municipal Airport "the stepchild of the city". Boulder owns it, but spends no general funds on it and allocates no staff time to manage it. The city spends 13 times more on bikeways than on the airport. All of the airport's \$19,800 budget comes from airport revenues.

The Chamber of Commerce has recommended annexing the airport to the city, hiring an airport manager and developing a master plan.

The city has applied for an FAA grant to do a master plan. (Denver Post, Sept. 1, Jane Cracraft)

1983

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

Last year, the city paid Public Service Co. \$800,000 to keep the city's 3,600 street lights burning. Complete maintenance, right down to new light bulbs, cost the city \$250,000.

A Public Utilities Commission ruling requires PSC to turn over to willing cities the maintenance of street lights, and Dave Baskett has declared that Boulder is willing. He figures the actual maintenance will be about \$50,000 a year. PSC will continue to do the pole repairs, but will bill Boulder directly for each specific job instead of an annual estimate based on a state average that includes interstate highways.

The PUC also has ordered the PSC to replace all of the old mercury vapor bulbs with high-pressure sodium bulbs. (Camera, May 30, Daryl Gibson)

The rusted Silver Lake Ditch irrigation pipe was recently removed from its spot spanning North Broadway at Norwood Avenue and placed underground.

For about 40 years, the pipe supported a wooden sign welcoming people to Boulder. Different citizens have varying versions of what additional information was provided by the sign and why it was removed a few years ago. (Camera, April 11, Cindy L. Brovsky)

Boulder suspended the Flatiron Paving Co. from bidding on city street projects because of a suspension of Flatiron by the Colorado Highway Department. One of the firm's subsidiaries was indicted by a federal grand jury and pleaded guilty to charges of rigging bids on state highway contracts. The parent company of Boulder does from \$1 million to \$2 million in direct city work annually and earns additional dollars on sub-contractor work. The temporary suspension will not affect contracts Flatiron - Boulder has now with the city. (Camera, June 21, Daryl Gibson)

Transportation Director Dave Baskett has requested \$75,000 for a traffic study of Table Mesa Drive in hopes of finding a solution to the daily traffic jam of 22,000 cars a day between South Broadway and U.S. 36.

Ten years ago, under public pressure, the council eliminated from the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan a bypass curving south of Boulder to connect U.S. 36 and Colorado 93 at the southern city limits.

No one considered that cars would impact Table Mesa Drive and its intersection with Broadway.

Baskett's idea is for a two- or four-lane street branching off Broadway at about Greenbriar Drive, circling the south edge of the Majestic

Heights neighborhood and linking up with Table Mesa Drive near the turnpike. He noted that even though the council threw out the bypass notion, it stopped short of abandoning scattered pieces of right-of-way for that bypass. (Camera, Sept. 11, Daryl Gibson)

The county commissioners want to raise the levy five mills above the allowed seven-mill maximum for the next three years to raise \$5 million a year for special projects such as the widening of South Boulder Road.

The city council endorsed a county proposal to raise the road and bridge tax levy if the county will devote \$1 million from the increase to completion of the Foothills Parkway.

The council has agreed to pitch in another \$1 million of its own and take the \$2 million to the state as a local contribution in hopes of enticing another \$8 million in federal grants to finish the parkway to the Longmont Diagonal. (Camera, Oct. 6, Daryl Gibson)

The county commissioners, acting under the auspices of a new state law which permits counties to approve special mill levies for capital improvements projects, approved a three-year, five-mill increase in property taxes. The county agreed to the \$1 million requested by Boulder to assist in completing the Foothills Parkway, as well as additional requests from other communities. (Louisville Times, Oct. 12, Charlotte Minor)

The county commissioners approved 1984 requests for state Highway Commission funding for roads, and agreed with the County Long Range Planning Commission that the requests should no longer be prioritized. (Lafayette News, Nov. 2, Charlotte Minor)

City Manager Bob Westdyke suggested to the city council that Boulder designate two kinds of streets - major arteries eligible to be altered so they may carry even more traffic than they do now, and "neighborhood" streets eligible for various controls to discourage all but local traffic.

The Mapleton Hill Neighborhood Committee has asked that a temporary barricade across Eighth Street between Pearl and Spruce streets be made permanent. They have pledged to raise half the money for the project. The council voted to close the street but added a proviso that the action did not set a precedent for other neighborhoods. (Camera, Nov. 16, Daryl Gibson)

#### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

The Transportation Division will finish five bikeway and pedestrian projects in November. The Arapahoe Road path will stretch between 55th and 30th streets. A South Broadway path from Hanover to the Viele underpass will connect residents with the Table Mesa Shopping Center. The Wellman Canal project will rebuild and supplement the bike path from Foothills Parkway west to Monroe Avenue. A sidewalk has been added along 28th Street from Colorado to Taft Avenue. A one-mile stretch of bikepath was added to 63rd Street from Gunbarrel Avenue to the Longmont Diagonal. (Camera, Oct. 26)

The council moved to prohibit the use of mountain bicycles on all park trails unless specifically approved by the city manager. (Times-Call, Jan. 19, Darcy Bonfils)

#### PARKING

City policy states that a business or residential complex that

provides bicycle racks can cut its parking spaces by 10 percent, knocking off one automobile parking space for every three bike racks.

The rationale behind this is that the city wanted to get people out of their cars and onto a bicycle or a bus.

There are other policies that may keep Boulder from becoming a sea of asphalt. The planning board may eliminate as much as 70 percent of the required parking for government-owned senior citizen housing projects. The planning staff may trim 20 percent of the parking in certain developments where offices and apartments share the parking. Core area developers, whose land is expensive and limited, are more likely to ask for and receive a reduction in parking space requirements, because it is assumed their tenants will walk since they are close to the city's center. (Camera, March 6, Daryl Gibson)

On an average weekday, the Randolph Center parking garage stays about half full. City Parking Administrator Judy Underwood said that the center, which has operated in the red since 1978, was close to the break-even point.

The city might be able to lure more people to the garage, but officials have had a policy of not advertising for fear of promoting more automobile usage in the downtown area. (Camera, March 6, Sharon Gillen)

#### TRANSIT

Regional Transportation District officials announced a 12 percent decrease in fare-box receipts. In Boulder, bus ridership was down 9 percent in January - from an average of 9,000 daily weekday trips in 1982 to 8,100 this year.

Roger Cracraft, one of two Boulder County representatives on the RTD

board of directors, thinks that there are two basic reasons for the drop in ridership - poor economic conditions and last summer's bus strike. People who got out of the habit of riding the bus during the 27 days of the strike never went back. (Colorado Daily, April 6, Kurt Chandler)

The average total operating cost for a local bus is \$44 per hour (including drivers' salaries, fuel, maintenance, bus terminal overhead, etc.).

The RTD added three bus routes accessible to wheelchair-bound riders in June. (Camera, June 4)

The RTD board has set aside \$1 million in their 1984 capital improvements budget for a buses-only traffic lane that would start at the south end of the Boulder-Denver Turnpike. The exclusive bus lane would help ease passenger complaints of slow bus service between Denver and Boulder and might also help the state meet U.S. Environmental Protection Agency air pollution guidelines. (Camera, July 13, Sally McGrath)

History does repeat itself. Fifty-two years ago, the solution to Boulder transportation problems was to replace the city's rail street cars with buses. Today, the solution being discussed is replacing buses with streetcars.

Some newly elected council members want light rail transit with the possible first step linking the downtown and Crossroads Mall.

The RTD has made Boulder a low priority on its light rail funding list. Councilwoman Bev Sears suggests that Boulder stop relying on RTD and start taking some local initiatives for mass transit.

Councilman Phil Stern wants the city to make transit more inconvenient

for the motorist.

Councilman Spenser Havlick said he will ask major employers in the city to stagger their work hours so employees are not all hitting the streets during rush hour. And he will ask RTD to begin phasing out large buses in favor of mini-buses in Boulder. He wants to explore "gentle incentives" to encourage people to use alternative transportation. He calls for disincentives such as higher parking fees but says penalties are "the last thing we should do."

Councilman Greenlee would support a light rail system in Boulder, "as long as it was not operated by RTD." (Camera, Nov. 13, Daryl Gibson)

There are opposing views of whether Boulder County gets its money's worth out of the RTD and statistics to go along with both views.

Roger Cracraft said that "money's worth" is not the criterion by which a bus service should be judged. If that was applied to building highways, "there wouldn't be any ... in western Colorado."

Boulder County contributes 9.5 percent of RTD's income based on collection of the 0.6 percent sales tax.

Of the district's 20 major Park-n-Rides, five are in Boulder County.

A ridership study showed that for every 10 people leaving Boulder on a bus during the morning rush hour, one person came into Boulder on a bus. This percentage may alleviate Denver's traffic and air pollution but seems to add to Boulder's. (Camera, Nov. 20, Sally McGrath)

Boulder Horse & Buggy Inc., doing business as Boulder Trolley Systems, won permission from the Colorado Public Utilities Commission to operate a trackless, horseless trolley in Boulder. (Denver Post, June 29)

The Union Pacific Railroad Co. announced plans to abandon a spur track

running east and west through part of Boulder so the Colorado Department of Highways can save \$1.25 million on the construction of the Foothills Parkway. (Camera, Aug. 9, Daryl Gibson)

Gerry Bandy was hired to prepare a master plan for the airport. He will apply for a waiver of FAA rules that require a 700-foot separation between runways. Boulder Airport runways are separated by 100 and 180 feet now. He also reported that noise from the airport operations appear insignificant when matched to federal standards. (Camera, June 10, Daryl Gibson)

1984

### HIGHWAYS AND BYWAYS

When ground was broken in 1972, completion of the entire Foothills Parkway between Table Mesa Drive and the Diagonal Highway was expected to take five years and cost \$7 million.

Twelve years later, 2.75 miles have been finished at a cost of \$10 million and the state Highway Department projects the remaining 2.3 miles will cost another \$21 million. Foothills is getting 20 to 25 percent of the \$8 million available for a six-county district. Costs of highway construction inflate between 7 and 12 percent each year.

Transportation Director Dave Baskett, while recognizing the state's funding limitations, does not believe Boulder is getting back its fair share of highway money. The one good road is the Boulder-Denver Turnpike and that was built with toll money. His best guess is that the city has already invested \$5 million in the Parkway project, including design and road work and land purchases. (Camera, Sept. 9, Linda Cornett)

The transportation department has earmarked \$65,000 for a study of South Boulder traffic. The area has been impacted for several years and with the steadily increasing traffic north from Golden and with completion of the improvements to South Boulder Road, the load will be ever heavier. (Camera, Aug. 18, Linda Cornett)

An Ad Hoc Citizens' Committee was formed to consider solutions to the problems posed by traffic in South Boulder. It is comprised of neighborhood, business and agency representatives and will oversee the study and provide community ideas. (South Boulder Traffic Study Newsletter, Dec. 1984)

Members of the Boulder County Consortium of Cities are considering forming a C-470 task force to represent the county's position on a proposed extension of the highway through the county. (Denver Post, March 14, Alicia Leppert)

The highway, now designated W-470, was further discussed at a Consortium meeting in May. Boulder City Council has not yet adopted an official position on joining a task force but, when it does, it will be based on "the most effective way to oppose this highway coming into Boulder County". Transportation Director Dave Baskett called the task force "a renegade planning process that is a deliberate attempt to circumvent the regional planning process that's already in place through DRCOG." (Boulder County Courant, May 9, Charlotte Minor)

The Colorado Department of Highways will soon open the new four-lane overpass at the Superior interchange. The \$2.2 million cost is being paid for by Louisville Associates, developers of the once-planned regional shopping mall. The group now plans to build a massive residential-

commercial project next to the interchange. (Camera, April 17, Sharon Gillen)

Although the Boulder City Council opposes adding yet another interchange on the Boulder-Denver Turnpike south of Superior, it will not fight its construction.

Mayor Ruth Correll said, "People were willing to make it a toll road (when U.S. 36 was built) to get the efficient route to Denver, but each time an on-and-off ramp is built, it reduces the ability to retain this as an efficient link. ... we have to live with all our neighboring cities and towns, and we're not trying to tell them what to do. We just want them and the state Highway Commission to know our position." (Denver Post, Aug. 15, Jeannine Hartman)

Street closures are no longer an option to solve the woes of residents upset by the number and speed of cars passing through their neighborhood. This policy was voiced by the council at a study session and will be officially confirmed later. Of eight requests under consideration, only three remain. These are Kewanee, which is already closed and will not be re-opened, Eighth Street which is approved and waiting for funding, and West Pearl which is still being considered. (Camera, June 13, Linda Cornett)

The council gave the West Pearl improvement district its support and the plan will be to keep the street open but to put landscaped traffic circles in the street at its intersection with Fourth Street.
