There will also be curbs, gutters, sidewalks and street paving where none exists, and curb neckdowns on Pearl at Eighthth Street to narrow the distance pedestrians must cross.

Property owners and the city will each pay half of the \$640,000 cost

of the project. (Camera, Aug. 8, Linda Cornett)

For a second year, the Boulder County Commissioners approved a special property tax for roads. It is expected to generate \$5.4 million in 1985. The road tax is exempt from the state law limiting county increases to 7 percent. (Camera, Oct. 12, Sally McGrath)

### PEDESTRIANS AND BIKEWAYS

The city council established as a goal reducing traffic congestion and pollution by encouraging people to bike, bus or walk to work. (Denver Post, June 13, Pamela Kruger)

Boulder will give pedestrians a small break when they adjust "walk" lights at all intersections to give people one more second to cross. (Camera, Feb. 25, A. R. Alper)

The 5th annual Pedestrian Conference brought speakers from around the world. They discussed pedestrian malls, suburban pedestrian access and restricted parking to enhance environmental quality. (Camera, Sept. 21, Linda Cornett)

In May, the city started using a bicycle rider pulling a 50-pound custom-made trailer to patrol and keep bike paths free of debris. (Camera, Sept. 27, Linda Cornett)

Bicycle accidents in Boulder have increased about 70 percent since 1978. Figures from the city's Bicycle Education Program show that nearly 60 percent of the accidents are the motorists' fault and 75 percent occur at intersections. (Colorado Daily, Oct. 2, Dick Kenworthy)

#### PARKING

The city has 1,770 parking spaces downtown - 935 metered spaces and 835 spaces in lots.

There are 617 permits issued in downtown lots to long-term parkers, all the spaces the city can afford to devote to permit parking.

The quickest solution to the parking problem would come from completing the parking deck above the RTD terminal on Walnut Street. It was designed for three levels and is now at one and a quarter levels. Completion would add 150 spaces at a cost of \$6,000 each.

Most controversial of the options the Central Area General Improvement District has been considering is a three-story parking structure at the corner of Broadway and Spruce Street. The \$1.8 million structure would provide 186 spaces where a surface lot now provides 58. A neighborhood ad hoc committee reported that the spaces would cost \$9,800 each. (Camera, July 22, Linda Cornett)

A design committee authorized by the Boulder City Council approved a scaled-down version of a proposed parking garage on the northwest corner of Spruce Street and Broadway.

The committee requested research on underground parking, but an engineering report indicated that groundwater is just 13 feet below the surface. The original three and a half story design would have cost \$2.05 million - about \$10,500 a space for 196 cars. Excavating for two full levels below ground would cost \$12,200 a space or \$2.7 million. (Camera, Sept. 28, Linda Cornett)

A group of businesses paid for a parking survey of downtown employees. Eighty percent of people working in the core area pay nothing for parking and 70 percent park within two blocks of their place of employment. (Camera, Sept. 13, Linda Cornett)

The city created the equivalent of an extra parking lot downtown by restriping parking spaces into smaller areas. The 59 new places cost \$7,700 in paint and a consultant's time. They are worth between \$400,000 and \$600,000 based on what it would have cost to build a new lot. (Camera, Dec. 5, Linda Cornett)

The city council agreed to make downtown meters free on Saturdays during December to lure more shoppers downtown. The Central Area General Improvement District will lose about \$6,000 in revenues and the city will lose an estimated \$8,000 from parking tickets. (Camera, Dec. 5, Linda Cornett)

#### TRANSIT

The RTD board of directors approved a plan to make two bus routes in Boulder free beginning January 1, 1985. They agreed to split the \$178,000 cost of the Free Ride with the city. For nine months an attempt will be made to increase the number of riders and decrease cars on crowded Broadway. (Camera, Nov. 28, Linda Cornett)

The owner of Boulder's red trolley is giving up because he could not get the support of either downtown merchants or the people of the community. (Camera, July 28, Margie McAllister)

The study of Boulder Municipal Airport has recommended expenditures of \$2.5 million in improvements over the next 15 years. They include hiring an airport manager, improving the current runway, and replacing the runway and taxiway lights which were installed in 1956.

The first flight from the airport was in 1928. It was not until 1959 that the city acquired the airport and in 1982 it was annexed to the city.

(Camera, Dec. 3, Linda Cornett)

The city council approved all the basic recommendations of the master plan for the airport. City Attorney de Raismes will attempt to re-negotiate agreements with the four aircraft service and rental firms holding 20-year leases at the airport. Assistant City Manager Dave Knapp estimated revenues from the airport should be ten times what they are at present. (Camera, Dec. 12, Linda Cornett)

# CHAPTER IV

## LAND USE

1980

### PLANNING AND ZONING

The 1979 Pow Wow rodeo was held at the Boulder County Fairgrounds in Longmont and did not fare well economically. Pow Wow is still looking for a home after it sold its land in the city to La Belle's and could not meet the conditions imposed by the county for land at 55th Street and the Diagonal.

City Manager Bob Westdyke offered the Pow Wow 50 acres of city-owned land west of Boulder Reservoir on 51st Street. The county must decide on the feasibility of the new site because access to the property would be through county land and would pass by the homes of county residents.

At issue is whether the site can be served with utilities, whether the city will annex the land, and whether the Pow Wow board can buy or lease the property. (Camera, January 10, Sharon Massingill)

The Boulder County Health Department expressed reservations about an engineered septic system because of soil conditions and the proximity to the reservoir. Water could be provided by Left Hand Water Supply Co. Traffic circulation and fire protection are posing problems.

The Pow Wow cancelled its rodeo for 1980 breaking a tradition of more than 40 years. Dale Marcotte, president of the Pow Wow, said they are looking at another site in Louisville. (Camera, April 9, Sharon Massingill)

Louisville became the final resting place for the Boulder Pow Wow.

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera on February 8:

Boulder's skyline is in for some changes.  
The city Planning Board approved a five-story high-rise on Folsom

between Walnut and Pearl streets ... .

Also approved were a five-story hotel, a handful of apartments and a 46-foot-high computer plant in a string along 47th Street between Valmont Road and the Longmont Diagonal.

City building codes permit buildings of more than 35 feet in height only after special review by the Planning Board. The maximum allowable height even with a review is 55 feet.

The planning board dealt with permits under the Danish plan. In April, in the core area of the city, 81 units of the 88 available were approved. A total of 203 permits were available in the outer area, but 343 permits were requested. The board selected the projects with the highest number of points under the merit system. (Camera, April 2)

In October, 150 of the 271 permits approved went to Canyon Pointe and Winding Trail Village. Both are owned in part by Downing-Leach Associates. The board borrowed 50 extra permits from next year's Danish Plan allocation. These went to small projects which earned fewer points for their designs. (Camera, October 1, Daryl Gibson)

The city council changed land use codes so that a professional office building is classed as a "related" function of a hospital and should be allowed in areas zoned for hospitals after special review. This change will allow Memorial Hospital to reapply for a building permit for a professional office annex.

Todd Malmsbury wrote in the Camera dated September 12:

For eight years, Boulder officials have been concerned that 30 acres of undeveloped federal land near the National Bureau of Standards might be sold for commercial development.

The parcel, just south of the ... Bureau ... is part of the land donated by the city to the federal government in 1947 as the site of scientific laboratories. Because the 30 acres never were developed, the GSA (General Services Administration) - citing a federal regulation designed to sell off large tracts of land - began action to have the land sold.

(Rep. Tim) Wirth began working to stop the sale ... . He called the effort to sell the land a "breach of faith with the city."

... the efforts ... to "excess" (dispose of) the land have

officially ended. General Services officials in Washington have "rescinded" the order ... .

The dominant planning story in 1980 stemmed from the announcement that the System Development Corporation of Santa Monica, Calif. "intends to file a formal request to amend the Boulder County Comprehensive Plan to allow the company to build a new facility south of Boulder."

The company has a binding option on a 475-acre site just west of Colorado 93, 2 1/2 miles north of the Jefferson County line.

Bob Williams, SDC planning director, said the facility would be located on 100 acres of the ... site. The remaining land would be used as open trails and preserves ... .

He said the company's maximum size would be about 4,000 people, most of them recruited locally over the next 10 to 15 years.

Plans are for the company to build its own roads and sewage treatment system and to use its own water rights ... .

The County Comprehensive Plan presently designates the land as an open space buffer. County Planning Director Ed Tepe said a major industrial complex should be in or near an urban area. Allowing one in an unincorporated area is contrary to the Comprehensive Plan. (Camera, August 29, Sarah Hoover)

In the Camera of October 12, Daryl Gibson wrote:

... SDC officials broached the idea of redrawing the boundaries of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan to take in the proposed plant site ... .

SDC spokesman Bob Williams said the company assumed redrawing of the line would be the first step to the city's actually annexing the plant site into the city limits ... .

(Frank) Gray said he would not recommend (redrawing the lines) ... to the City Council without a public vote.

SDC ... called ... (such an election) ... "unreasonable, unnecessarily time consuming, costly and void of final resolution."

"Frankly," Williams told (City Manager) Westdyke in a formal response to the city's terms, "given the fact that SDC does not require traditional city services, the concept of annexation into the City of Boulder does not merit such a protracted and non-committing exercise."

The negotiations with SDC are not in "active consideration", since that response, Westdyke said.

The Boulder County Parks and Open Space Advisory Committee placed an urgent priority on the designated south county line open space buffer to provide separation between Boulder County and the industrially zoned areas

in northern Jefferson County. (Camera, Nov. 21, Sharon Gillen)

System Development Corporation has asked several nearby cities to consider annexing their 475-acre site. (Camera, Nov. 26, Betsy Bates and Sharon Gillen)

The proposal by System Development Corp. to build a \$100 million-plus research facility ... has emerged as the first major test of the 2-year-old Boulder County Comprehensive Plan.

SDC's application to amend the plan will be decided by the five-member county Planning Commission after a public hearing Jan. 28.

Under state law, county planning commissions have final say over amendments to comprehensive plans. The rezoning question, however, will be decided by the County Commissioners. Some observers note that if the comprehensive plan amendment is denied, the commissioners could override that by approving the rezoning. However, whether zoning in violation of the comprehensive plan is viable is a question that only the courts would decide. (Camera, Nov. 30, Sharon Gillen)

City Manager Westdyke asked the Open Space Board of Trustees to start the process of redrawing the boundaries of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan (BVCP) to take in the SDC land. The board agreed but also voted to recommend that the land remain as open space. (Camera, Dec. 11, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Planning Board also voted to amend the BVCP so the site is within the control of the city. (Camera, Dec. 12, Daryl Gibson)

Superior, population 250, voted to put the SDC site within its planning boundaries. It is only three miles from the town to the site. (Camera, Dec. 13, Daryl Gibson)

The Denver Regional Council of Governments reviewed the SDC request for an amendment to the County Comprehensive Plan and recommended denial, primarily because of the policy against development in "non-urban" areas. (Colorado Daily, Dec. 15, Karen Kos)

Sarah Hoover reported in the Camera of December 16 that the proposal by the System Development Corp. to build a computer software plant in Boulder County has been called a "unique proposal and a unique opportunity"

by the directors of the Boulder Chamber of Commerce.

Bob Williams, System Development Corp. planning director, said his company does not want its site annexed by the city of Boulder. (Camera, Dec. 18, Sharon Gillen)

The Boulder County Parks and Open Space Committee, in a unanimous vote, recommended that SDC's request to amend the county comprehensive plan be denied. (Camera, Dec. 19, Sharon Gillen)

The Boulder County Commissioners voted 2-1 to ask the city of Boulder to buy the SDC site. The motion meant that the city purchase the land and put it on hold until a city election can be held to determine what the people want done with it. (Camera, Dec. 22, Sharon Gillen)

Commissioner Wally Toevs, who campaigned on a platform of keeping the SDC land as open space but lost the election, entered (the) motion to ask Boulder to buy the land.

... His successor, ... Bob Jenkins, has taken the stand that the SDC application should be given a full hearing before county boards.

There is some question whether Boulder can use its open space fund to purchase land just to hold for a few months while a development decision is being made. (Denver Post, Dec. 25, Jane Cracraft)

The council announced Wednesday that (a) Friday morning meeting would be held. The action followed a Monday request by the County Commissioners that the city purchase the (SDC) site.

Friday was chosen, ... because the commissioners asked for a "prompt" response, because the entire council was available to meet that day, and because potential annexation of the site by the town of Superior before the regularly scheduled Jan. 6 City Council meeting would eliminate Boulder's power to act. (Camera, Dec. 27, Thad Keyes)

When System Development Corp. refused to play ball with Boulder, Boulder decided to buy the ball and end the game.

The Boulder City Council Friday voted to buy the 475-acre site 5 miles south of Boulder ... .

The City Council is planning to ask voters whether the land should remain in open space or whether SDC should be allowed to build on it. If so, the land would be sold to SDC.

SDC might not wait around for the election. (Denver Post, Dec. 27, Jane Cracraft)

Bob Jenkins, newly elected ... county commissioner, ... blasted the Boulder City Council for its decision ... .

He called the vote by the council ... another example of "midnight maneuvering" ... .

County Commissioner Jack Murphy of Longmont voted against the SDC land-buy measure . . . . And future SDC votes by the commissioners, if any, likely will favor the company because Jenkins has displayed qualified support for the development. (Times-Call, Dec. 30, Barry Noreen)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

Mayor Ruth Correll wrote to federal officials asking them to refuse federal funding, if it were requested, to help provide any modifications to U.S. Highway 36 and any expansion of the Louisville sewer system needed to accommodate the Centennial Valley Mall. (Camera, Jan. 8)

A state decision on rebuilding the Superior interchange on the turnpike to handle traffic for the proposed mall is awaiting a study from the Denver Regional Council of Governments. DRCOG is awaiting federal funds so they may begin the study. (Camera, Jan. 19, Gloria Bucco)

A \$50,000 air pollution study of the environmental impacts of both the Centennial Valley Mall and the Crossroads Shopping Center expansion revealed that either development could lead to an improvement in the county's air quality and a reduction of daily fuel consumption by area shoppers. A simple summary of the data would be that more people would have less miles to travel to shop, with either, or both, developments. (Colorado Daily April 9, Patrick Summers) The study was requested by Governor Richard Lamm.

The Boulder County Long Range Planning Commission will not support any study of U.S. 36 which includes traffic projections for the proposed mall. Their position maintains that the mall is a flagrant violation of the county's comprehensive plan. (Camera, May 29, Sharon Massingill)

Douglas L. Houston has been hired to head the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority and manage the Crossroads shopping center redevelopment. Frank

Gray, who has been filling this role, will return full time to his position as city planning director. (Camera, Jan. 21, Daryl Gibson)

George Doty, the retired real estate agent who has spearheaded opposition to the Crossroads renewal project, has asked the governor to inquire into condemnation procedures being used to displace established businesses. (Camera, Feb. 22)

Doty is bothered by the method the city is pursuing to accomplish the expansion of the shopping center, the condemnation of 60 properties and the forced displacement of over 50 businesses. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 6, Bob French)

In response to the complaints to Gov. Lamm, the Colorado Division of Commerce and Development has asked the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority to provide more staff and money for the relocation process. (Camera, March 11, Daryl Gibson)

The land once owned by George Wilson, and proposed for addition to the Crossroads area is now owned by The Regional Partnership. Stan Connolly, one of the partners, has applied for and received a building permit for a \$750,000, 35,000 square-foot shopping center to be located at 2909-2949 Walnut Street. The area is shown as a parking lot in plans for the Crossroads expansion. (Camera, March 5. Sue Deans)

The city could not deny Connolly a building permit because BURA did not have the money to buy the land. However, Connolly has been turned down for loans by Boulder banks because of the pending condemnation by the authority. He has now sued BURA for more than \$3 million and has demanded that his land be exempted from the urban renewal area. (Camera, Aug. 26, Daryl Gibson)

The MaceRich Company will spend more than \$50 million remodeling and expanding, and will pay BURA \$3.5 million for expansion land. The authority will have to spend \$12 million to purchase the land from current owners. (Camera, May 5, Daryl Gibson)

A suit to overturn the results of the June 12, 1979 election was dismissed by a federal court in November 1979. Don Tracy has filed an appeal. (Camera, Jan. 10, Sue Deans)

The U.S. Court of Appeals on Jan. 25 denied a motion by the city to affirm a lower court decision which dismissed a challenge to the election. The challenge was based on the claim that voters were denied the right to vote because the city had published an inaccurate election district map. (Camera, Feb. 5, Gloria Bucco)

Don Tracy and three Crossroads Mall businessmen filed a lawsuit in District Court asking that the Urban Renewal Authority be abolished, that work on the expansion be stopped and that the authority turn over to Tracy documents labeled "confidential". The suit also claims the Crossroads area is not "blighted" and therefore does not qualify as an urban renewal area. The authority instructed City Attorney de Raismes to explore legal options for stopping Tracy.

The Crossroads opponents formed committees to circulate petitions calling for the demise of BURA. They need more than 5,000 signatures to put the issue on the September 9 ballot. (Camera, May 30, Daryl Gibson and Mike Stone)

District Judge Murray Richtel ruled that the city council was within its rights when it formed the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority.

A second lawsuit, similar to Tracy's, has been filed by Crossroads Optical owner Walter Ebner.

Money to finance the mall was to have been borrowed nearly a month ago, but the lawsuits have forced money lenders to be more cautious.

Still left to be settled in court is Tracy's claim that BURA violated freedom of information laws by refusing to show him part of a report on the mall financing. Deputy City Attorney Jane Greenfield said she did not ask for a ruling on the freedom of information claims, wanting to get rid of the blocks to financing first. (Camera, July 11, Daryl Gibson)

On July 29, the opponents of the Crossroads expansion submitted a petition signed by 6,700 people requesting another election. City Attorney de Raismes urged the council not to schedule the election because the petition is "invalid", was "not timely filed", was not a valid subject for a referendum, deals with "administrative" rather than "legislative" measures, and seeks to "impair a contract". The petition group claims the petition is an initiative and thus not subject to time constraints. (Camera, Aug. 16)

The Boulder Urban Renewal Authority has signed an agreement with the MaceRich Company committing \$20 million for land acquisition and public works improvements. The construction schedule rests on the ability of the authority to sell bonds to finance the project. The city must buy all the necessary property within one year of getting its financing. (Camera, April 3, Daryl Gibson)

The planning board gave unanimous and final approval to architectural plans for an expanded Crossroads Mall.

During the hearings, opponent Jerry Van Sickle called the project, "morally and politically and economically wrong."

The board's approval included a waiver of the city's standard 35-foot height limit to allow a 51-foot high building.

Board member David Leserman abstained from the vote because the city plans to take part of his Cordry Court backyard to widen Boulder Creek as a flood control measure to protect the mall. (Camera, July 11, Daryl Gibson)

Merrill Lynch and Company began marketing \$10 million in short-term notes at 8 percent interest to finance the expansion of Crossroads Mall.

Frank Gray, city planning director said the rate could have been better if bond buyers were not faced with unsettled lawsuits over the expansion project. Potential lenders must be informed of the suits and weigh the outcome before deciding on the investment. (Camera, July 26, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Urban Renewal Authority got its first \$10.5 million in a pre-midnight maneuver to foil Crossroads Mall opponents. The authority dismissed Merrill Lynch as an underwriter and at 11:49 p.m. hired Kirchner Moore and Company to take over. The new underwriter delivered a check to the authority at 11:30 p.m.

City Attorney Joe de Raismes said the action by the authority makes the funding law for Crossroads "irrevocable".

The transaction gives the authority \$1 million to use immediately and another \$9.5 million in escrow for 90 days while Kirchner Moore looks for financial backers. (Camera, July 29, Daryl Gibson)

Opponents of the Crossroads expansion filed yet another suit asking the District Court to order an election on the mall project. The suit challenges an August 19 vote by the council dismissing their petition.

The opponents were encouraged by a Colorado Supreme Court decision this week that gave Louisville citizens the right to vote on the Centennial Mall. The Louisville petitions were filed the day following the Louisville Council's vote to annex the land for that mall. (Camera, Sept. 11, Daryl

Gibson)

While the case was pending in court, a second petition asking for an election has been ruled invalid by City Attorney de Raismes. He had agreed to review the wording of the petition before it was circulated for signatures. He declined to furnish the wording of a "valid" petition. (Camera, Oct. 8, Daryl Gibson)

The five suits pending against BURA may have added \$200,000 a year to the cost of the Crossroads project. It also may have cost the project an opportunity to sell its bonds at two percent less than the current rates. (Denver Post, Oct. 20, Linda Cornett)

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera on November 11:

Boulder City Council members clashed openly over the floundering Crossroads urban renewal project . . . .

Councilman George Boland capped the debate with a vow to seek another public vote on the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority.

The lively debate . . . followed a discouraging week in which the 10.5 million in BURA notes fell flat on a faltering municipal bond market.

The city had other bad news to discuss. Merrill Lynch and Company had filed a claim against BURA because the firm was fired as an underwriter last July.

And the improvements on Boulder Creek planned to protect the mall from flooding which had originally been estimated to cost \$3 million were now quoted at \$5 million. One engineering firm had been fired because it suggested the higher figure earlier this year.

The city hired an engineering company to study the flood plain map because of the \$5 million price tag on protection. The study showed the flood plain may stop just north of Arapahoe Avenue.

In light of the new findings, Don Tracy requested a new trial "to prove his property north of Crossroads is not blighted because it is no

longer in the flood plain." In his motion, Tracy claims BURA and the city redesigned the flood plain map specifically to reduce the cost of the Crossroads project and are "simply manipulating the facts ... to justify the conclusions which are sought." (Camera, Dec. 3, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder District Judge William Neighbors ordered the Boulder City Council to schedule another election on the Crossroads Mall expansion project.

The ruling was in response to a suit filed in August asking the judge to decide whether the petitioners for another election could be denied by the council.

Citizens Opposing BURA argued that the petition was an initiative to repeal council action. The city believed it was a referendum which by law had to have been filed within 30 days after council action. Judge Neighbors did not rule on the city's argument that the original election could not be reversed because the project had already acquired debts which must be paid. (Camera, Dec. 9, Daryl Gibson)

City Attorney Joe de Raismes will appeal the District Court decision if Judge Neighbors does not reverse his ruling. (Camera, Dec. 10, Mike Stone)

Failure to sell \$10.5 million in bonds and five lawsuits filed against the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority may mean the end of plans to expand the Crossroads Shopping Center.

City Manager Westdyke said the city council would have to re-evaluate the shopping center revitalization if the bonds are not sold by Nov. 20. (Times-Call, Nov. 12)

Bond underwriters Kirchner Moore and Co. have found a lender for \$8.5 million in spite of the most sluggish bond market in recent history. BURA needs \$10.5 million in short-term notes to begin buying land and to pay for

street and flood control improvements.

The land will be purchased by the city for about \$12 per square foot and resold to the MaceRich Co., the mall owner, for less than \$4 per square foot as an incentive for MaceRich to expand the mall into expensive urban land rather than build a suburban mall on cheaper rural land. (Camera, Nov. 25, Daryl Gibson)

The University Hill Merchants Association is currently almost completely ineffective. They say that since 1972, the city has made promises for beautification and for more parking, but instead has placed its emphasis on the Pearl Street Mall and now on the Crossroads Shopping Center. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 10, Don Olsen)

The city set in motion a bond issue of \$400,000 to pay for upgrading the Hill shopping area. It will pay for trees, plants, signs, bike racks and other highly visible improvements. However, the city needs the support of at least 50 percent of the landowners before it can assess the district and begin the improvements; and there is not unanimity among the merchants. (Camera Focus, Oct. 26, Daryl Gibson)

#### DOWNTOWN

The Boulder Planning Board approved a five-story building on the site of the old Arnett Hotel at 1025 Pearl Street. It included an exception to the city's building height ordinance. The new Arnett Center will have two lower floors of shops and three upper floors of offices. (Camera, March 7, Daryl Gibson)

In March, the planning board approved the Canyon Pointe housing project, another five-story building. Phase I of the project was built last year by Downing and Leach to house the elderly and handicapped.

Phase II is planned around 75 units that will be sold as condominiums and retail space. The Department of Housing and Urban Development wants a small grocery store included, but the planning board told the builders that as long as the market on Pearl Street was in the neighborhood, it would suffice. The city also approved a reduction in parking spaces. Downing and Leach is pledged to pay the 1977 land price which was between \$5 and \$6 a square foot. Until RTD moves, according to the purchase contract, the price of the land cannot escalate with the market.

City Manager Bob Westdyke announced last week he got RTD to make up the deficit in the land value from January of this year until the bus terminal is moved.

Downing and Leach still gets a "windfall" deal on the land ... but the city will not bear the full loss, ... estimated at \$4,000 a month. (Camera, June 2, Daryl Gibson)

Despite chidings by opponents that the city had made too many "trade-offs" with the developers, the council voted to approve the second phase of Canyon Pointe. (Camera, June 4, Daryl Gibson)

A partnership of about five investors is purchasing an entire city block in downtown Boulder. The Mountains Downtown group has bought all the property on the block bounded by Walnut, Pearl, Eighth and Ninth streets with the exception of the building at 825 Walnut, the site of Nancy's Restaurant. Tentative plans are to build luxury condominiums, shops and offices. (Camera, Oct. 23, Mike Stone)

During 1980, several planned developments on the mall failed to materialize. Owners said that lack of cheap construction money, an unstable money market, and the reluctance of tenants to commit themselves were factors leaving vacant lots downtown. (Camera, Nov. 3, Daryl Gibson)

The lawn of the County Courthouse will no longer be a sanctuary for people dodging the regulations of the Downtown Mall. Both city and county

officials approved the extension of law enforcement onto County land.  
(Camera, Dec. 3)

### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

The century-old Allen-Faus house at 15th and Spruce will be restored for use as an office building.

Members of the First United Methodist Church decided it was not feasible economically to sell and move the house. A member of the congregation offered seed money for the renovation. (Camera, Jan. 30, Sue Deans)

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera dated Feb. 13 that:

An amiable deal between Historic Boulder Inc. and the City Council took an unfriendly turn ... when the historic group changed its offer to sell the Boulder Theater to the city.

To bridge the gap (between the expiration of its option on March 1 and the performing arts bond election) until November, Historic Boulder last week offered to exercise its option to buy the building, refinance the loan ... and lease the whole theater to the city until November.

Under that original offer, the city would get back any money it spent for the lease if the voters refused to approve the bond issue.

(Historic Boulder President Karl) Anuta told the council Tuesday night that Historic Boulder can't find a bank willing to transfer the loan ... .

Specifically ... the city would have to pay about \$90,000 ... .

Historic Boulder assumed the responsibility for saving the Boulder Theater for a year with the assumption the city would eventually buy out their interest. The theater has been selected as one component of a downtown theater complex.

The \$2.5 million cost of buying and remodeling the building is thought to be about half the cost of constructing a new theater of comparable size.

The council voted ... to declare the old theater a landmark. The action will preserve the art deco architecture in the marquee and mosaic facade. But it does not guarantee preservation of frescoes on inside walls. (Camera, Feb. 20, Daryl Gibson)

The bond issue failed in September and Boulder Theater was placed on

the market. (Denver Post, Jan. 1, 1981)

The 1898 Chautauqua Auditorium facade was restored to the way it looked in 1906 as the second phase of a three-year project to revitalize the old building with structural improvements and landscaping. The cost of the project is \$248,000. It is funded by city Community Development grants and the Colorado Historical Society. (Camera, Nov. 18)

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of December 14 that Highland School has been converted into a plush office building. The renovator, Sina Simantob, and the building received the first award for design excellence issued by the city planning department.

The city's downtown commercial area has been designated a historic district by the National Register of Historic Places.

Historic Boulder applied for the designation two years ago. The district generally is bounded by Ninth and 16th streets and Walnut and Spruce streets. It includes 143 buildings of which 78 are considered historic buildings.

For property owners, there is the possibility of tax breaks and federal matching grants for rehabilitation work on historic buildings. (Denver Post Jan. 26, 1981, Linda Cornett)

Manuel Weiss is developing a program to survey as many historical sites in rural Boulder County as can be identified. A \$32,000 federal grant, matched by local funding, will finance the venture. It is sponsored by the Boulder County Historical Society, Boulder County Parks and Open Space Department and the Boulder Public Library. (Colorado Daily, May 14, Jennifer Heath)

### PLANNING AND ZONING

The Boulder Planning Board voted unanimously to redraw the lines of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan to take in 2,515 more acres, including the SDC site. The council had voted in December 1980 to buy the 475-acre site as open space to keep SDC from developing it until an election can be held. (Camera, Jan. 16, Sharon Gillen)

The planning board voted to recommend to the council three extensions of city water service west of the Blue Line. The council must decide whether the extensions will be placed on a ballot. (Camera, Feb. 6)

The three parcels, Nuzum's Nursery, the Silver Saddle Motel, and the Flagstaff Apartments, were developed before the Blue Line was drawn and already get city water and sewer service. City planners suspect the Blue Line was drawn in error.

The council refused to act on a request from its own Housing Authority to include a low-income housing unit at 101 Pearl Street in the Blue Line boundaries. It also rejected a request by an entire subdivision of 40 homes on Knollwood Ave. (Camera, Aug. 22, Daryl Gibson)

A streamlined process of reviewing development plans was unveiled by Planning Director Frank Gray.

The system promises to cut development reviews to a fast-tracked four-to six-week procedure. In the past, builders have been faced with a four-to six-month ordeal. The new policy is to give daily construction projects priority over city long-range planning. (Camera, April 1, Daryl Gibson)

The planning board approved construction of an industrial complex for

NBI Inc. with two buildings that exceed the city's 35-foot height standard. Because the site is close to Boulder Municipal Airport, provisions were included that release the city of liability for safety and environmental problems because of the airport. (Camera, April 3, Daryl Gibson)

City Planning Director Frank Gray will resign effective January 1 to go to work for the Carl A. Worthington Partnership, an architectural firm. (Camera, Dec. 5, Daryl Gibson)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

Yet another lawsuit was filed in Boulder District Court asking that all further land deals be stopped until the court settles the issue of whether Boulder should hold another election on the Crossroads expansion.

An attorney for Crossroads Optical contends that the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority got money for the land purchases as a result of an illegal meeting.

BURA and the city came under fire when it was announced that Governor Richard Lamm had made phone calls to encourage investors to buy notes to help finance the bonds. Mayor Ruth Correll had requested his help. (Camera, Jan. 8, Daryl Gibson)

BURA Director Doug Houston reported that legal fees, higher interest rates, escalating land prices and the effect of inflation on transportation improvements have cost the public more than \$1.2 million. He assumes the bond sale would have been on schedule last June if Don Tracy had not filed a suit to stop the project. (Camera, Jan. 14, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder District Judge William Neighbors denied a city appeal of his ruling last month ordering a new election on the Crossroads Mall expansion. (Camera, Jan. 20, Daryl Gibson)

Opponents of the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority have changed their group's name from Citizens Opposing BURA to Local, Orderly Government Involving Citizens (LOGIC). (Camera, Feb. 12, Daryl Gibson)

The city council made the reluctant decision to hold a second election on the fate of BURA. The District Court had ordered the city to honor the petition demanding an election. The council has appealed to the Colorado Supreme Court to reverse the ruling of the lower court. (Camera, Feb. 18, Daryl Gibson)

The Colorado Supreme Court turned down the appeal. The election will take place in April. However, City Attorney de Raismes will continue to pursue the issue of the power of petitioners to call for an election which would wipe out the results of a previous election. (Camera, March 13, Daryl Gibson)

BURA reached a settlement of \$33,971 with Merrill Lynch and Co. for debts incurred before the company was replaced as BURA's bond underwriter. (Camera, March 5, Daryl Gibson)

On April 7, 10,878 voters reaffirmed their support of BURA and 8,831 voted against. (Denver Post, April 15, Linda Cornett)

Opponents of BURA formed a new committee, Taxpayers Awareness Group (TAG) and took the county commissioners to task for allowing BURA to divert county property taxes under a financing scheme that was approved only by voters within the limits of the city of Boulder.

Don Tracy wanted the commissioners to join in a lawsuit against BURA to test the state's urban renewal law. When they refused, he said he could sue them for failing to protect the tax money of county residents.

The commissioners had to vote last December to raise the county property tax mill levy to compensate for the money being taken out of their budget by BURA. (Camera, June 2, Daryl Gibson)

The Colorado Court of Appeals ruled against Don Tracy by upholding a Boulder District Court decision that the Boulder City Council had enough evidence to declare the Crossroads Mall area blighted. (Camera, June 5, Daryl Gibson)

Don Tracy has appealed his lawsuit against the city council to the Colorado Supreme Court. (Camera, Aug. 12)

The Colorado Supreme Court chose not to hear the appeal. (Camera, Nov. 3)

The Colorado Court of Appeals dismissed the lawsuit filed against BURA by the owner of Crossroads Optical Center. (Camera, Sept. 24, Jan McCoy)

The 10th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals rejected Don Tracy's challenge of an election on June 12, 1979. The suit was dismissed because the plaintiff could prove no "legally cognizable injury". (Camera, Sept. 29)

Boulder County Taxpayers Association filed a lawsuit in Boulder District Court. This one claims that county residents should have had a voice in the initial creation of BURA because a portion of the property taxes in BURA's district goes to BURA instead of to the county and the school district. (Times-Call, Oct. 7)

BURA has purchased five parcels of the 32 which they are buying for the MaceRich Co. Wrecking crews have knocked down the first of many "blighted buildings. (Camera, July 1, Daryl Gibson)

BURA chairwoman Janet Roberts, wielding the same shovel that was used at the ground-breaking for the original Crossroads Mall in 1962, turned the earth for the first expansion of Crossroads. (Camera, Aug. 20, Daryl Gibson)

BURA is now filing lawsuits to condemn property for the Crossroads expansion. (Camera, Oct. 28)

The Boulder County Commissioners issued the May Co. \$10 million in industrial revenue bonds so they may take advantage of exemptions from federal and state taxes in building a two-level department store in Crossroads. (Camera, Dec. 18, Margie McAllister)

In July, the Boulder County Commissioners reversed an earlier decision and voted to ask the State highway commission to accept the \$4.6 million in construction money offered by the developers of the Centennial Valley Mall to build the Superior interchange. The deciding vote was cast by the newest commissioner, Bob Jenkins. (Denver Post, Oct. 13, Joe Watt)

A \$400,000 facelift for University Hill was approved by the city council. The revenue will come from an assessment district approved by 55 percent of the property owners. The city will spend another \$300,000 in street improvements. (Camera, June 17, Daryl Gibson)

#### DOWNTOWN

The board of directors of the Downtown Boulder Association voted to support the redevelopment of Crossroads in the special election on April 7. Those who favored the project commented that without a viable Crossroads Mall the downtown would suffer anyway, with the entire sales tax base shifting to out-of-town areas. The board also stressed the need for the city to be concerned with the vitality of the downtown area and hoped that they would follow through on promises to tie in the downtown with Crossroads through transportation systems. (Camera, March 11)

Entertainment on the mall ranges from a rope walker who juggles flaming torches to a string quartet playing chamber music. The city has adopted numerous regulations such as limiting the number of pushcarts,

banning skateboards, bicycles, roller skates, and dogs, no sleeping or loitering, etc. The mall is patrolled regularly by police on foot, and its trees and flowers are maintained by parks crews. (Denver Post, Sept. 7, Jane Cracraft)

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

After five hours of discussion the city council agreed to the terms of a new lease for the Chautauqua Park complex. The lease gives the city-owned auditorium, dining hall and administration building to the Chautauqua Association. The council agreed to pay for street improvements, water and sewer lines, and drainage work as well as maintaining the park grounds.

The Chautauqua Association will have to pay \$5,000 to lease the park lands and will pay a fee in lieu of property taxes. The lease is a complex agreement, taking in owners of the private cottages and the association which owns some of the old cottages and rents them as student housing. Under the old lease, the association paid no rent on the city land. It managed the use of the buildings while the city retained ownership. (Camera, Feb. 25, Daryl Gibson)

The Chautauqua Association unanimously rejected the new lease terms offered by the city council. A counter proposal was offered consisting of a 20 year lease (rather than 10 years), a payment of between \$1 and \$10 a year, and retention of the present board structure with only one member appointed by the council (rather than five). (Camera, March 3, Mike Stone)

The Chautauqua Association voted to accept a 20-year lease and to pay \$2,500 a year. It will accept ownership of the buildings and will take on the financial responsibility for repairing them. (Camera, March 18, Daryl Gibson)

The survey of county historical landmarks and sites has been completed. It was the first countywide historical survey to be done in Colorado. It will be available to state and federal officials for use in making land management and planning decisions. (Camera, June 21, Sharon Gillen)

Mountain Productions Inc. is proceeding with the renovation of the Boulder Theater after arranging to lease it from Boulder Theater Associates with an option to buy. (Camera, Aug. 13, Sarah Hoover)

1982

### PLANNING AND ZONING

Ed Gawf has served as acting director of planning since the resignation of Frank Gray. City Manager Bob Westdyke announced his appointment as permanent planning director. (Camera, May 4)

The Boulder County Commissioners approved a special-use permit for Flatiron Sand and Gravel Co. to operate open mining at the Deepe Farm. The 152-acre site is bordered by South Boulder Creek and U.S. 36. The company accepted the commissioners' seven-year limit for two phases of the proposed five-stage operation. After that, the company must reapply for another permit. (Camera, Jan. 12, Margie McAllister)

The council approved the addition of a medical office building annex to Boulder Memorial Hospital. (Camera, Jan. 20, Daryl Gibson)

A major, five-story addition to the Golden Buff Motel on 28th Street at Canyon Boulevard was approved by the Boulder Planning Board. (Camera,

Feb. 5, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Planning Board approved a solar access ordinance to be passed on to the city council for a final vote. The law would guarantee access to the sun for solar collectors by regulating the size of neighboring buildings. (Camera, April 2, Daryl Gibson)

The planning board unanimously approved recommending to the council an ordinance which would allow accessory or "in-law" apartments in owner-occupied homes. There were several restrictions included in the proposed law addressing the concerns expressed by residents about increased density, noise, trash and parking problems.

The dwelling must be owner-occupied, must be created only through internal conversion, and the owner must provide an off-street parking space for the tenant.

Planning Director Ed Gawf said the measure will alleviate housing needs for students, single parents and small families. (Colorado Daily, June 7-8, Kathleen Smith)

After a postponement in July and a lengthy debate on the issue, the "accessory dwelling units" ordinance was passed by a 5-4 vote of the council. The law will allow homeowners the option of renting a portion of their homes provided the rented portion has a separate kitchen and bathroom. Homeowners will be required to purchase a \$100 permit prior to adding the units on the property.

The law also limits to 10 percent the number of accessory units in a neighborhood, limits the number of persons occupying the unit to two, and limits the number of unrelated people in the house to three. (Times-Call, Nov. 10, Barry Bortnick)

In the first review by the planning board of the five-year update to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, several controversial developments were eliminated. Among these was the proposed Eldorado Country Club, a golf course with several hundred homes below Shanahan Ridge. The area is designated as greenbelt, but has not yet been purchased by the city. (Camera, June 18, Daryl Gibson)

The city's research and evaluation division moved from the Department of Human Services to the Planning Department. The division head, Tom Miller, has the "responsibility for designing and conducting research and evaluative studies about human resources and the quality of life to help decision makers with program planning and policy analysis." (Denver Post, June 23, Barbralu C. Manning)

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera dated Nov. 28:

Boulder has a reputation for turning a cold shoulder to new industry, a reputation earned by reams of restrictions.

There are laws against industry in the cornfields, laws against annexing land before the city is ready, laws against buildings which are too tall, laws against infringing on the greenbelt and an unwritten aversion to anything that looks bad or pollutes.

Some of the city's major employers - IBM, the National Center for Atmospheric Research, the National Bureau of Standards - would have gotten a thumbs down if the current laws had been in effect when those businesses were built.

City officials now are trying to patch up that ill will between business and government and stop a feared exodus of native companies from the Boulder Valley.

The crux of the plan is to allow medium- and large-size industrial plants to be built on land previously tagged as untouchable in the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan.

Area 3 (of the plan) cannot be developed until at least 1992 and only then if the possibilities in Area 2 are exhausted.

(The new) proposal (the Performance Industrial Plan) is to amend the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan to permit early development in Area 3 if a company meets strict criteria.

The idea has the endorsement of the city staff, but must still be approved by the Planning Board, City Council, county Long Range Planning Commission and the Board of County Commissioners. (Camera, Nov. 28, Daryl Gibson)

The planning board voted to approve a new 250-room hotel on the proposed route of the Foothills Parkway. The site is in the Center Green industrial park being developed by Joe Sanchez.

The board also approved an application from the Celestial Seasonings tea company to build a manufacturing plant on 52 acres east of the Longmont Diagonal Highway. It approved annexation of the property to the city under industrial zoning. The application must be voted on by the city council. (Camera, Sept. 3, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder City Council approved the last of two annexations making the Celestial Seasonings site part of the city. (Camera, Dec. 22, Daryl Gibson)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

Boulder District Court Judge Richard Dana granted BURA the right to condemn Don Tracy's property for expansion of Crossroads Mall. Under the terms of the decision Tracy will be able to withdraw a \$330,000 deposit made with the court by BURA. That is the amount of the last purchase offer to Tracy before the dispute was submitted to the court. (Camera, Jan. 23, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder District Judge Richard McLean ruled that BURA did not violate the Colorado Constitution and is not illegally depriving the Boulder Valley School District and Boulder County of tax money. Since BURA's debts technically are not an obligation of the county, school district or city, a vote by county taxpayers was not required. (Camera, May 20, Daryl Gibson)

BURA finally found buyers for \$20 million in municipal bonds to finance the Crossroads project. The money will be used to pay about \$10

million in short-term loans borrowed by BURA to begin the project, to buy the rest of the land and do street and landscaping work around the mall.

To get an "AAA" bond rating, an insurance policy was purchased for \$550,000 that guarantees lenders will get their money even if the sales and property taxes generated by the new mall are not enough to pay off the debts. Although the insurance was expensive, it reduced the interest rate BURA will have to pay to the lenders. That interest varies, but averages about 13.5 percent. (Camera, March 27, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder District Judge Richard Dana has decided that BURA and Stan Connolly of the Regional Partnership must jointly establish a commission to set a price on the property in the 2900 block of Walnut Street. Connolly filed a suit requesting \$3 million from BURA. (Camera, April 20, Mike Stone)

Originally, the \$3.5 million that MaceRich agreed to pay for the land at Crossroads would have been used to reduce the \$20 million debt or for improvements around the mall. It now will go for land purchase only. When BURA first appraised the 26 parcels of land, its value was put at \$10.3 million. By the time BURA was able to buy half the land and put down deposits on condemnation suits on the other half, the price had gone up to 13.6 million.

The Regional Partnership bought a vacant lot on Walnut Street south of the Target Store in late 1979 for \$400,000. BURA condemned the land this year for a deposit of \$1.3 million. (Camera, July 4, Daryl Gibson)

The Homart Development Co. and Jacobs-Kahan Co., known as Louisville Associates, announced their decision not to proceed with their \$55 million plans to build the Centennial Valley Mall. (Colorado Daily, July 30-31, Karen Kos)

BURA originally had more than \$2 million to spend on a transit mall. That budget has been eroded to about \$200,000 by escalating property costs and delays. The \$200,000 will provide a concrete bus route, bus shelters and some landscaping.

The BURA board approved the basic design along with the design for \$586,000 in bike paths, sidewalks and trees to ring the mall. (Camera, Aug 26, Daryl Gibson)

Janet Roberts stepped down as chairwoman of the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority, but will remain on the board. Sally Martin was elected to replace her. (Camera, Nov. 17)

A "grand opening" will take place to celebrate the completion of the University Hill renovation project. More than 100 trees, 9,000 square feet of red bricks, 20 benches and a 16-foot cast iron clock have given the Hill a facelift. (Colorado Daily, Aug. 25-26, Kurt Chandler)

#### DOWNTOWN

Tightrope walker Evan "From Heaven" Ravitz has entertained on the mall for four summers. On May 12, the Mall Commission voted to ban his act, citing the possibility of damage to public property and danger to spectators. Ravitz attempted to resume his act, but was escorted from the mall by police officers. (Camera, May 28)

License fees for mall portrait artists jumped from \$35 per month in 1981 to \$100 per month this year. Portrait artists are allowed to sell their works on the mall, and they must have city permits like the food vendors. Mall entertainers are not similarly regulated. (Camera, Oct. 4, Colin Reid)

Police arrested 25 men and one woman in the aftermath of the Pearl Street Mall's annual Halloween celebration. The crowd became "rowdy" and "unruly" and refused to clear the intersection at Broadway and Pearl Street. Police statistics revealed that 40 percent of those arrested were from out of town and 13 of 23 arrestees had prior records. (Colorado Daily, Nov. 2, Rosey Harty)

The Halloween celebration cost the city about \$7,500 for overtime police officers, damage to mall property and clean-up of the mall. (Camera, Nov. 13, Daryl Gibson)

#### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

The Boulder Historical Society sold its site on the corner of Arapahoe and Broadway and moved into the historic Squires-Tourtellot House at 1019 Spruce Street. (Camera, Aug. 29, 1983, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder City Council designated the Mapleton Hill neighborhood as a historic landmark district. As a result 454 properties will be protected from any development that is not compatible with their historic nature. (Camera, Sept. 8, Daryl Gibson)

1983

### PLANNING AND ZONING

The performance industrial concept, passed by the city council and planning board and the county planning commission, now must receive the approval of the county commissioners.

The proposal was prompted by threats that NBI Inc. would leave the area if it did not get zoning approval for a 160-acre site north of the

city.

Boulder officials want to keep NBI, its 1,500 employees and the tax base it provides. City Planning Director Ed Gawf said, "For the first time, we're starting to address the issue of economic health in our community." (Denver Post, Feb. 28, Dave Price)

Thirty residents have applied for permits for accessory housing units. The law is giving an incentive to homeowners with illegal units (additions put in before the law was passed) to come forward and have them legalized. An amnesty clause in the law allows this without penalty until June 20. (Camera, April 18, Kathleen Smith)

The maximum number of permits for 1983, 1,049, has been handed out under the growth control plan. The planning board has approved 273 more by borrowing from next year's quota. (Camera, Sept. 16, Daryl Gibson)

Twenty home builders out of 28 won permits in the rationing process used by the planning board. One of the builders who came away empty-handed said he was "considering action" against the city. (Camera, Nov. 19, Daryl Gibson)

Two home builders have sued the Boulder Planning Board over the way 270 permits were rationed. McStain Enterprises ranked highest in quality points for its proposed project, Whittier Square at 23rd and Spruce streets, but fell victim to a clause in the growth control law that said no project should get more than 75 building permits a year. McStain contends that since extra permits were "borrowed" from the 1984 quota, the company should not be penalized because it had received its 1983 share.

The second builder claims the city owes him building permits for a project that was annexed and approved before the growth control law went into effect. (Camera, Dec. 17, Daryl Gibson)

#### SHOPPING CENTERS

BURA announced it will pay Don Tracy \$410,000 for his property, which already has a May-D&F store under construction on it. In the settlement, Tracy agreed to drop any unresolved claims against BURA. (Camera, Feb. 15, Daryl Gibson)

A Boulder District Court commission ruled that the land owned by developer Stan Connolly was worth almost \$2.2 million, not the \$1.4 million that BURA appraisers claimed. (Camera, May 21, Daryl Gibson)

BURA has spent \$46,000 in legal fees in the Connolly case. The authority will use the city attorney in an attempt to overturn the verdict. (Camera, July 19, Daryl Gibson)

BURA refinanced the Crossroads project with the sale of \$25,615,000 in bonds. The average interest rate was 9.08 percent which will save BURA \$3.9 million over the 20-year life of the loans. The new bonds will be used to pay off the old \$20 million issue that was financed at 13.57 percent. (Camera, March 10, Daryl Gibson)

The MaceRich Co. has awarded more than \$200,000 in commissions to local artists to produce works for the Crossroads Mall. (Camera, June 23)

BURA allocated \$25,000 to build a community room in the Crossroads Mall. It will be used for group meetings.

The board also allocated \$9,000 to the day-care center in addition to the \$130,000 budget already approved for that project.

The MaceRich Co. donated the space for the two facilities. (Camera, July 21, Daryl Gibson)

If negotiations are concluded by next May to bring a fifth major department store to Crossroads, BURA will no longer need a staff. The authority itself would exist as the entity responsible for paying the Crossroads expansion debt. (Camera, Dec. 22, Daryl Gibson)

#### DOWNTOWN

The planning board approved construction of a five-story office building on the southwest corner of Walnut and 11th streets with the stipulation that the developers make every effort to put retail shops on the ground floor.

A height exemption was required to allow a portion of the building to reach 55 feet.

The board also approved two housing projects each with 18 condominiums, one in the 1800 block of Marine Street and the other on the southeast corner of 18th Street and Arapahoe Avenue. (Camera, Feb. 4, Sharon Gillen)

At an August 16th meeting, the council allocated \$20,000 to cover the administrative and consultant costs for a downtown "masterplan" so they may gauge the community impacts of developments and possibly set design guidelines.

Removal of the old RTD bus station at Ninth and Canyon is making way for a \$15 million condominium-office-retail complex called Canyon Center. The condominiums must wait for the Federal Emergency Management Agency to approve of the residences' being sited in the flood plain. (Colorado Daily, Aug. 26-27, Kathleen Smith)

A spokesman for Burlington Northern Railroad Co. said they are

seriously studying plans to build a hotel geared more for conventions than for the resort trade. The railroad company owns the lot on the northeast corner of Canyon Boulevard and Ninth Street. (Camera, Jan. 11, Daryl Gibson)

Trans West Land of Seattle, the real estate subsidiary of Burlington Northern is negotiating with the city's downtown parking district for land adjoining their hotel site. The developers want to build underground parking on the entire site, including the city property. In exchange for taking over the city's land, the hotel parking lot will be open for public parking during the daytime hours as if it were a city lot. (Camera, Feb. 8, Daryl Gibson)

The proposal for the hotel ran into stiff opposition from planning board members who fear it would endanger lives if placed in the Boulder Creek floodway. (Camera, Dec. 2, Daryl Gibson)

City planners, property owners and the Whittier neighborhood have come up with a concept for Pearl Street between 18th and Folsom streets. They propose a mixed-use-redeveloping zone that would encourage combinations of living, working and shopping. The new laws would allow greater building coverage of the lot, requiring no front or side setbacks from the property line. The amount of open space required per residential unit would be half that mandated in most of the area. It would count balconies, decks, usable rooftops, internal atriums and plazas as open space. (Camera, April 3, Sharon Gillen)

The Boulder Board of Adjustment ruled that the Boulderado Hotel cannot add an annex under the guise of a boarding house. The Boulderado is in a business zone, but the proposed annex would cross an alley into a

residential zone where hotels are prohibited. (Camera, May 13, Daryl Gibson)

The planning board approved a zone change from residential to business that will allow the Boulderado to build a separate annex to the north with a bridge connection.

Because the hotel is a historic landmark, the city's Landmark Board is working with the architect to make sure the annex matches it in style.

The planning board also approved condominiums at 620 Pearl Street where several University of Colorado professors will build a retirement community. (Camera, June 3, Daryl Gibson)

A proposed office and retail building on the southeast corner of Broadway and Spruce streets was approved by the planning board. A height exception was granted to allow part of the project to exceed the city's 35 foot limit. (Camera, Nov. 4, Daryl Gibson)

A proposed moratorium on exceptions to the height limit was defeated by the council. (Camera, Dec. 8, Daryl Gibson)

A citizens' task force on the future of the downtown issued its list of proposed changes to be carried out by a "Downtown Boulder Partnership" with a hired executive director. (Camera, April 24, Daryl Gibson)

The Downtown Boulder Plan, to be presented to the planning board and the city council, was prepared by a 15-member Downtown Task Force charged with devising a plan to balance commercial development with residential quality.

Their recommendations include: Making 13th Street a major pedestrian-bicycle route; giving developers a break on taxes and height restrictions for including residential units in commercial buildings; making better use

of the courthouse lawn area; providing downtown employees with a low-cost shuttle from outlying parking lots and requiring permits for parking in downtown residential areas. (Camera, Nov. 24, Linda Cornett)

Ron Donohue, the city parks superintendent, has a maintenance budget of \$125,492 allocated to the downtown mall. This includes the flower planters, cleaning up 1,400 gallons of trash every week, keeping the walks cleared of snow, and keeping the mall toilet and water fountains in operation. (Camera, May 16, Sharon Gillen)

The city council agreed to impose new rules governing street entertainers on the mall. Among others, permits are now required to attach ropes to mall property, effectively prohibiting tightrope walkers. Jugglers are not allowed to throw fire or knives without a special permit and without liability insurance. The intent of the changes is to make the mall a safer place and to keep it from turning into a flea market. (Camera, June 8, Daryl Gibson)

### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

The council designated as local historical landmarks the Odd Fellows Hall, the Wolff House on Elder, and the abandoned Marine and Broadway power substation. (Camera, May 5, Sharon Gillen)

1984

### PLANNING AND ZONING

The historic Mount St. Gertrude's Academy at 10th Street and Aurora Avenue was purchased by the University of Colorado in 1969 for \$150,000,

and used for classrooms. In 1980, fire destroyed part of the building and it was put up for sale.

John Cohagen and his partner Kevin McMahon bought the site for \$1.2 million and are planning to put 17 condominiums in the old academy building and put seven single-family homes on the remaining land.

The partners could tear down the buildings and divide the block into 24 single-family lots under existing zoning laws. (Camera, Feb. 3, Daryl Gibson)

The planning board board approved a revised plan for the site which eliminated one parking lot and expanded the open space for each dwelling. Although board members expressed concern about the traffic impact on 10th Street, Planning Director Ed Gawf said the number of additional cars would not create congestion. Board approval was contingent on the developers adding a second driveway for the underground parking lot. (Camera, March 3, Brighid Kelly)

A 14-acre site on the southwest corner of Valmont Road and Folsom Street served as a trout farm until JLS Enterprises, owned by Joe Sanchez, purchased it for \$1.5 million. JLS wants to construct two apartment buildings on the land. The planning board has rejected three site plans because of questions about height, parking, open space, traffic, and location in the Goose Creek flood plain. (Camera, March 20, Brighid Kelly)

JLS Enterprises has presented proposals for the fifth time seeking approval for its planned unit development. Its earlier plans suffered three defeats by the planning board and one by the city council after neighbors protested. After 14 months, JLS has spent "tens of thousands of dollars on architects alone." (Camera, May 23, Brighid Kelly)

The Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan encourages hotel development at

the city's core, not on its fringes. The planning board adhered to that concept and turned down a second proposal from developers who want to build a 295-room hotel on the northeast corner of the Longmont Diagonal and 47th Street. (Camera, Feb. 3, Daryl Gibson)

The board again rejected plans for the Gateway Hotel despite architectural and parking revisions. The developers now have the option of making further revisions that might meet approval of the planning board, or asking the city council to overrule the board. The council has overturned planning board approvals for another hotel about a mile south of the Gateway site. (Camera, July 7, Linda Cornett)

Of 39 people who have applied for permits for "mother-in-law" apartments, 30 have gained permission. There are strict guidelines in the accessory unit ordinance to make sure that neighborhoods are not adversely affected. (Camera, April 16, Brigid Kelly)

The planning board approved proposed changes to the growth control law and sent them on to the city council for a final vote.

Builders have complained that the present system discriminates against small builders, ignores hardship cases, and practically rules out single-family houses.

Under the proposed changes, the board could set aside 10 percent of the permits available for single-family detached houses. The board could cut from 11 months to just three months the time a builder is allowed to hold a permit without starting construction. (Camera, Jan. 13, Daryl Gibson)

The city council approved the changes to the growth management ordinance. Their decision concludes nearly five months of fighting between

the city and builders, which began after 1983's quota of permits ran out early. Lawsuits filed by two builders are pending in District Court. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 2, Kathleen Smith)

District Judge Michael Enwall ordered the city to grant building permit allocations to two firms. He agreed that the planning board abused its discretion and exceeded its authority. The builders who won their lawsuit will get 149 permits that were denied them last fall. This will leave only 326 permits for other builders for the rest of 1984. (Camera, Feb. 25, Brighid Kelly)

City officials and developers bargained over building permits again. Fifty developers had requested 574 permits; 161 were awarded to 39 individuals based on such quality issues as location (10 points are given for core area sites), landscaping, energy-saving features, plus the number of low- and moderate-income units included. (Camera, June 17, Linda Cornett)

Carol Jensen initiated a petition drive to put a blue line amendment on the November ballot. She wanted voter approval of utility service to her land at 300 West Arapahoe. She stopped seeking signatures on Sept. 3 when the city council gave preliminary approval to the ballot issue.

However, on Sept. 17, Jensen's request was rejected by the council.

Jensen then sued the council. But Boulder District Judge Michael Enwall refused to overturn the council's action. (Camera, Oct. 9, Kevin O'Brien)

NBI has decided not to build its headquarters on a 158-acre site near the Longmont Diagonal, and is considering the possibility of leaving Boulder County. The company "had concluded that the site could not be developed under the conditions being proposed."

Although NBI did not state a reason, speculation was that the costs of the site's main access route to the Diagonal may have been prohibitive. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 28, Kathleen Smith)

The planning board approved expansions at Boulder Memorial Hospital and Frasier Meadows Manor. Both expansions require exceptions to the city's 35-foot height limit. (Camera, Dec. 7, Linda Cornett)

### SHOPPING CENTERS

Boulder District Judge Michael Enwall ruled in favor of BURA and ordered a second trial to determine the value of property once owned by the Regional Partnership. (Camera, Jan. 11, Daryl Gibson)

The lawsuit was settled out of court with BURA agreeing to pay \$1,850,000 for four-and-one-third acres of land owned by the Regional Partnership. (Camera, March 22)

Montgomery Ward & Co. will expand into space on Crossroads south side thus completing the leasing of the old and new wings of the mall. (Camera, Feb. 16, Daryl Gibson)

The BURA board voted to approve a contract with Mervyn's, a California-based department store, to be the final major store in the Crossroads Mall.

In a concession to the developer, the board let MaceRich out of its previous pledge to have this last store open by April of this year. The two year delay before Mervyn's opens will cost the city about \$200,000 in lost sales taxes. (Camera, March 22, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder's first and only urban renewal project is finished. Executive Director Doug Houston has worked himself out of a job. The BURA will exist

on paper only, except for occasional meetings of its unpaid board. (Camera, May 7, Daryl Gibson)

The only named sidewalk in the city surrounds Crossroads Mall. McAllister Lane was named after Louise McAllister who was instrumental in getting the city to recognize the rights of pedestrians 10 years ago. (Camera, June 8, Daryl Gibson)

Eight Crossroads merchants claim that MaceRich Co. made "false representations" resulting in substantial losses for each merchant. The stores are located in one of the mall's east side corridors that will serve as an entrance to Mervyn's department store. Construction has been delayed two years beyond the original date. The merchants are requesting MaceRich to rescind the leases and return all the rent that has been paid since they moved to the mall. (Camera, April 12, Peter Nielsen)

An out-of-court settlement was reached between the MaceRich Co. and 37 Crossroads mall merchants in a year-long dispute over maintenance fees. (Camera, June 7, Susan Smith)

John Cohagen sold the Arapahoe Village shopping center to a San Francisco firm for nearly \$18 million. The sale came to about \$935,980 an acre for the approximately 20-acre site. (Camera, Aug. 3, Lou Chapman)

#### DOWNTOWN

According to some merchants, the Pearl Street Mall has had a drop in revenue since the expanded Crossroads Mall opened last August 11th. Other merchants claim the loss is due to a lack of cooperation and coordination among the retail owners along Pearl Street and to parking problems.

(Colorado Daily, Jan. 6, Karen Bowers)

A survey done by the city's Division of Research and Evaluation shows that city and county residents don't view the downtown as a primary shopping area and give most of their business to Crossroads. Respondents to the survey cited that "convenience", parking, transients, and traffic flow were partial determinants in their decisions not to go downtown.  
(Colorado Daily, March 26, Kathleen Smith)

The city council approved an executive conference hotel at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard. The Glacier Park Co. hired experts to examine Boulder Creek's floodway and their study determined that less of the proposed hotel actually sits in the danger area than originally thought. They said that the public library would split the water flow and divert most of the waters south of Canyon Boulevard. Hotel architects then moved the building so that none of it sits in the floodway. A floodway permit is still needed, however, because of a small portion of the underground garage.

Boulder's downtown merchants voiced strong support for the hotel. They see it as a way to revitalize the downtown.

Hotel developers own part of the site but still need to buy the Chamber of Commerce lot, and want the city to give them its parking lot at Ninth and Walnut. (Camera, April 12, Brighid Kelly)

Glacier Park Co., the development arm of Burlington Northern, said they were revising market information and looking for a national firm to manage their hotel. Since the original study, the city has approved projects which add 500 hotel rooms to the city, and the county commissioners have approved a 300-room convention center near Niwot.  
(Camera, Nov. 4, Linda Cornett)

The Boulder Chamber of Commerce sold its lot and building at 1001 Canyon Boulevard to Glacier Park for \$475,000. (Camera, Business Plus, Nov. 27, William Scanlon)

The sundial on the mall will be removed from its place at 14th Street where it has been for a year. It was commissioned by the Boulder Landmarks Board and paid for by the United Bank of Boulder. The Downtown Mall Commission said the sundial did not match the original design, did not tell time accurately and was too commercial. They had requested its alteration or replacement. The United Bank decided to remove it. (Camera, Sept. 15)

This year's bill for Halloween on the mall cost the city \$12,600 for police patrol and clean-up. Volunteer labor from the Sigma Phi Epsilon fraternity saved the city from an even more costly holiday. The city also paid Community Hospital \$500 for an emergency clinic. (Camera, Nov. 21, Linda Cornett)

An option held on the Boulder Theater by Crystal Square developer Wally Palmer ran out in September. This forced the controversial Crystal Square hotel project back through the city's review process. The project was turned down twice by the Boulder Planning Board, then approved by the city council. (Camera, Jan. 4, 1985, Linda Cornett)

### HISTORIC PRESERVATION

The Boulder architectural firm of Midyette, Seieroe and Associates won three 1984 Preservation in Action design awards presented by Historic Boulder: The renovation of the historic chapel in Old Main, the oldest building on the University of Colorado campus; the redesign of Citizen's National Bank at 1426 Pearl Street; and the addition made to the Squires-

Tourtellot House at 1019 Spruce Street. (Camera, Feb. 11, Lou Chapman)

The block surrounded by 15th and 16th streets and Mariposa and Bluebell Avenues was designated as a Boulder historic landmark. In 1939, the area was purchased by a group of University of Colorado faculty members who drew lots from a hat to decide who would get which building site. They built eight houses around the edges of the block and retained a communal strip of recreational land in the middle. The block has the formal name of Floral Park, but was known informally as "Little Russia". Prior to development it was also known as Jackass Gulch and Skunk Hollow. (Camera, March 15, A. R. Alper)

The Boulder Historical Society is moving again - from the Squires-Tourtellot House to the Harbeck House at 1206 Euclid Avenue. The city council agreed to lease the house to the society, rent free. In exchange, the Society will install a \$20,000 fire sprinkler system, pay utilities, maintenance and insurance costs and provide free classroom and meeting space to the city. The Squires-Tourtellot House is on the market for \$295,000. (Camera, Nov. 15, Linda Cornett)

## CHAPTER V

## ENVIRONMENT AND CULTURE

1980

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board endorsed a \$12 million bond issue to be placed on the ballot this fall. As proposed by the board the bond would be repaid over a 20-year period with a .2 cent increase in city sales taxes. The proposal must go to the city council for a final decision. The council will weigh it with an earlier decision for an arts bond issue. Both issues were considered by council last year and dropped. (Camera, April 22, Daryl Gibson)

According to Mike Segrest, delaying the proposed bond issue could raise the cost of the planned projects \$2 to \$3 million a year due to inflation. (Camera, May 10, Mike Stone)

The city council voted to stick by their earlier decision to hold the arts bond election this year and a recreation bond election next spring.

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of January 22 that:

Birds triumphed over bikes ... when the Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board voted to oppose a bike path route through the Sawhill Ponds bird refuge, part of a state park leased and maintained by the city.

Mike Segrest, city parks and recreation director, advised the board, "I think there are alternatives to the trail without going through the ponds. There are no alternatives to the ponds except to keep (them)."

The county has now been turned down twice in trying to plan a bicycle route between Gunbarrel and the city limits.

The city council approved a slightly modified 12-year master plan for park development and higher park fees. The action will increase one-time park fees from \$145 to \$447 for single-family homes and from \$125 to \$280

for multiple-family homes.

The council voted five to four to reduce the goal of acres of parks per 1,000 people to only 3 acres. Once the issue of acreage was settled, the vote to approve the master plan was unanimous. (Camera, Jan. 23, Daryl Gibson)
