In February, Mike Segrest passed on a suggestion to the mayor and manager that use of city parks be banned from midnight to five a.m. "I would view it as sort of a last resort," City Manager Westdyke said. (Camera, Feb. 15, Daryl Gibson)

The southwest shore of Viele Lake in Harlow Platts Park will be landscaped as a wildlife habitat showing the Rocky Mountain environment in miniature. The Flatirons Kiwanis Club suggested the project and will sponsor and raise money for the work. (Camera, Feb. 27, Daryl Gibson)

When the "cooties" return to Coot Lake this summer they will find lifeguards, portable toilets, paid parking, a litter squad and police officers as seasonal fixtures. (Camera, March 24, Daryl Gibson)

The Flatirons Ice Arena will be sold and turned into a private health club. For more than 10 years, it has served as Boulder's only private ice-skating rink. Skaters are organizing to convince city council to buy the arena and convert it to a public facility.

Last year, \$500,000 was budgeted to buy it when it went on the market, but the idea has since been scratched. A proposed bond issue does not include money for ice skaters. City Manager Bob Westdyke and Parks and Recreation Director Mike Segrest decided the cost of the arena and the annual subsidy it would take to run it would not be worth it considering

the limited interest in the sport. (Camera, May 3, Daryl Gibson)

In January, the council named a "minipark" at Fifth and Spruce streets after Campbell Robertson, a civic leader who died recently. (Denver Post, Jan. 9, Jane Cracraft)

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board has voted to name a new minipark at Fifth and Pearl streets after the late Hiram Fullen. Part of the Fullen Ranch was sold to International Business Machines for their plant north of Boulder. (Camera, June 24)

In February, the city hired a specialist in recreation programs for the handicapped and disabled. (Camera, Feb. 23, Tom Kraus) Her job is to find and cater to people not touched by routine city recreation programs. (Camera, June 24, Daryl Gibson)

At a budget hearing, the city council proposed an increase in nearly all recreation fees in 1981, and pinpointed the playground program as one that has been subsidized for too long. For as little as \$12, parents in Boulder have been taking advantage of eight weeks of summer "day-care" for their children - while the city spends \$63 per child to run the program. (Camera, Oct. 5, Daryl Gibson)

The star on Flagstaff Mountain, kept lighted since Christmas as a symbol of hope for the American hostages in Iran, was vandalized this week. The 300 bulbs that were stolen were returned undamaged, and the star was lighted again. (Denver Post, March 22)

The Chamber of Commerce reported the theft of lights and cables from the star on Flagstaff. A note was received from some person or group called "Boulder Falls" claiming responsibility for the vandalism and calling the star an "eyesore" and a "waste of energy". (Camera, Oct. 14,

Sarah Hoover)

The Chamber launched a fund-raising drive to replace the star, and in just 10 days collected more than enough money. (Camera, Nov. 29, Sarah Hoover)

The 82-year pact between the city and Chautauqua expires next year. Mayor Ruth Correll will appoint a committee to study the lease and make recommendations for renegotiation. (Camera, March 27, Daryl Gibson)

The committee and the city's Parks Board have backed a proposal which would continue the Chautauqua lease, with more city involvement in the management of the cultural and educational center and the rental fees.

"Nobody", says Chautauqua Association president, Helen Wingfield, "wants a real city take-over." As evidence, she points to the situation four years ago when a Chautauqua board offered to turn its functions over to the city and citizens opposed it. (Denver Post, Dec. 10, Linda Cornett)

Grants from the state Historical Society and community development funds have allowed a three-year refurbishment of Chautauqua Auditorium. Previous improvements were primarily structural. The latest step, a new roof and a paint job in the building's original color, symbolizes the new life of the Chautauqua Association over the last few years. (Camera Focus, Dec. 7, Lou Casten)

Councilman Dick Meckley suggested that the special sales tax earmarked for buying up open space land could be diverted to help pay for a bond issue for new parks and recreation facilities. When Councilman Paul Danish heard of the proposal, he said it would guarantee a "horrendous fight from the environmentalists." (Camera, Dec. 11, Daryl Gibson)

Daryl Gibson wrote in the Camera of December 16:

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board voted Monday to ask the City Council not to use open-space tax money to pay for a proposed \$13.4 million parks improvement plan.

Parks Board members ... do not want the two functions of parks and open space mingled together. "Both are real legitimate needs and one should not be sacrificed for the other," said Parks and Recreation Director Mike Segrest.

City Manager Bob Westdyke said it would take ... (a) public vote to change ... policy and divert (open space) money to be used for buying park land. (Camera, Dec. 16, Daryl Gibson)

#### OPEN SPACE

In March, the city council approved the purchase of 88 acres of open space land on the west side of the Diagonal next to Four-Mile Creek. The land will cost \$1.7 million.

Although the city has \$2.4 million left in its open space fund from last year, Jim Crain told the council that more money will be needed to purchase priority land along the Turnpike, on Davidson Mesa and near Six-Mile Reservoir. The city currently owns 9,459 acres ringing the city limits. (Camera, March 12)

Crain said 5,540 more acres remain in the purchase plans for the city and received permission of the council to seek proposals from underwriters to handle a \$3 million bond sale.

The management of the Boulder Valley Ranch (formerly Hidden Valley Ranch) was turned over to a new operator. Rex Walker will assume the lease on the city-owned ranch from Doyle Eaton, who has been unable to turn a profit in nearly a year of operation. (Camera, April 2, Daryl Gibson)

In the Camera dated April 27, Daryl Gibson wrote:

In 1973, friends of ... (Wonderland) ... lake managed to cut back the plans for the Wonderland Hills subdivision and got the city to convert some of the land to greenbelt. As part of the deal the city bought the lake for \$100. Leach and Arnold Homes, the company

developing ... (the subdivision) ... , was to repair the dam on the east side of the lake before turning it over to the city.

More than half of the ... homes have been built, but the dam remains below standard and classed by the state engineer as a "moderate hazard dam".

Two weeks ago, the city assumed ownership of the lake and immediately drained two feet of water to take pressure off the dam.

The city took over without waiting for all the repairs because of another battle shaping up over use of the lake.

The new controversy involved water supply for the Pine Brook Hills subdivision which wanted to pump water from the lake.

Open Space Director Jim Crain chafes at the idea of a greenbelt lake being used as a reservoir for a private subdivision ... . (He) ... recommended ... that the Pine Brook request be denied.

The condition of the dam remains a bone of contention between the city, Wonderland Hills and the state engineer's staff.

(The developer) set aside \$7,500 to fix the dam in 1973 based on what it thought the city wanted done.

Downstream development ... moved the dam from the "low hazard" to "moderate hazard" class, and now the state engineer wants more repairs.

(The developer) ... will not pay for "somebody's version of how to fix the dam up to first class standards so it will hold water for Pine Brook Hills ... ."

In May, the city council approved the purchase of 160 acres of open space on the west side of Flagstaff Mountain. (Denver Post, May 21, Jane Cracraft)

In October, the council approved the \$94,692 purchase of 92.8 acres on the south shore of Marshall Lake for open space. (Camera, Oct.22, Daryl Gibson)

The city started five years ago to purchase the Cottonwood Grove near the 47th Street bypass. Jim Crain has now been given permission to use condemnation, if necessary, to obtain it for greenbelt. (Denver Post, Nov. 19, Linda Cornett)

Daryl Gibson wrote in the December 11 Camera that City Manager Westdyke had asked the Open Space Board of Trustees to approve an amendment to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan to allow the city to include the

proposed SDC site. The board accepted the suggestion but "made it clear they think the SDC land south of the city should remain empty greenbelt and not be developed as a computer software center with 4,000 employees."

In the Camera dated December 26, Thad Keyes wrote:

The Boulder City Council voted today to buy as open space the 475-acre site planned for development by a computer firm at the south Boulder County line.

The city called the special 7:45 a.m. meeting at the request of the Boulder County Commissioners ... (who) ... said they were concerned that the land might be annexed by another city.

The county listed the land as "high-priority open space", but lacked the funds to buy it. The council viewed the purchase as a "holding action until Boulder voters can decide to keep the property as greenbelt land or allow the California-based System Development Corporation to continue its bid to build on the site."

Jim Crain said that the owners of the site chosen by SDC for its plant rejected a \$714,000 offer from the city to buy the land. The city filed condemnation papers in Boulder District Court to take the land. If the court agrees that the city has a need for the land and money to buy it, the city can take control of the land pending a formal trial to determine the exact value of the parcel. (Camera, Dec. 31, Daryl Gibson)

#### LIBRARIES

Boulder's Carnegie Library at 1125 Pine Street opened in 1907. It cost Andrew Carnegie \$14,137 and it served Boulder until 1961 when the current library was built at a cost of \$405,000.

In 1976, as a Bicentennial project, local historians suggested the building be preserved as an archive for historical and genealogical data. The city council approved the project, but allocated no money for it.

Mike Parnell, a library administrative assistant, has accumulated

enough grants to prepare a final design for remodeling the building. Recent acceptance of the library on the National Register of Historic Places should ease the acquisition of grant money for the project. (Camera Focus, March 2, Daryl Gibson)

The rehabilitation project will include strengthening the structure, replacing outdated heating, plumbing and electrical systems, painting the walls and refinishing the hardwood floors. This year's estimate for the work is \$400,000. (Camera, March 12, Linda Cornett)

At a public hearing on the proposed 1981 budget, south Boulder residents let the council know they are not happy with the proposed cutback in operating hours and the elimination of the only professional librarian at the George Reynolds branch library.

The city manager noted that he had originally proposed to close the branch entirely for the year until the library commission agreed to divert some funds from the main library.

The main library will lose some professional staff and \$25,000 for new books, according to Library Director Marcelee Gralapp. (Camera, Aug. 27, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder Public Library has been an innovator in many ways and now it is adding public access to its computer. Director Gralapp thinks the greatest service the computer will provide is instant access to the 175,000 volumes in the library.

Besides wishing to be on the cutting edge of the future, the library wants to stay in the good graces of city hall, which governs its budget. Gralapp says, "Over the long run the computer will mean less money than a clerical staff would cost to perform the same functions." (Camera, Sept.

28, Vicki Groninger)

Last year, the city council gave out \$57,000 in grants to encourage local people in the arts. All but one of the 29 projects funded have been completed. Marcelee Gralapp noted that the projects were allocated to expose a large number of people to local artists.

John Pryor, head of the Arts Advisory Committee which apportions the money, said the committee agreed with this philosophy and consequently chose to give less money to a larger number of projects.

This year another \$60,000 in grants will be given by the city.  
(Camera, March 16, Sue Deans)

##### Communications

The Boulder City Council adopted a new ordinance governing cable television. City Manager Westdyke described it as a "bare-bones ordinance" with few requirements. It will be accompanied by an application form that specifies that cable firms will be evaluated on the basis of their financial stability, plans for local programming, schedule for building a city-wide system, and other plans.

Councilman Paul Danish cast the only negative vote. He said the cable TV ordinance could lead to a monopoly, rather than promoting competition.  
(Denver Post, Jan. 9, Jane Cracraft)

Boulder Cable TV has been cited in Municipal Court for allegedly stringing cable lines outside the boundaries drawn by the council as part of a three-month moratorium imposed on the company.

An attorney for the firm filed a motion to have the moratorium declared unconstitutional and entered a plea of innocent. TeleCommunications, Inc. (TCI), the parent company of Boulder Cable TV

already has filed a \$1 million lawsuit against the city in Federal District Court in Denver charging that the city and another cable company conspired to drive TCI out of business by imposing the moratorium. (Camera, Jan. 29, Daryl Gibson)

City Attorney Joe de Raismes brought the issue into Boulder District Court asking for a restraining order to keep TCI from ignoring the moratorium. Judge William Neighbors refused to issue the order, deferring to the authority of the Federal District Court. (Camera, Feb. 7, Daryl Gibson)

Deputy City Manager Jim Piper reported that city crews have begun tearing down television cable recently strung by TCI. (Denver Post, Feb. 12, Jane Cracraft)

Richard Varnes, the city's cable television consultant, sent application forms to about 50 cable companies and another 50 to consulting firms and attorneys in the cable business. He said maybe a dozen of these had responded, all expressing some reservations and concern about the feud between the city and TCI.

A group of local investors have formed Boulder Communications Co. and are committed to submit a bid. The head of the new company, Jack Kerner, said that he fully expects to be granted a permit, and that it will be the only permit. (Camera, Feb. 12, Daryl Gibson)

Five cable TV firms submitted applications to the city. The TCI application takes apart the draft city cable TV ordinance section by section labeling much of it a violation of First Amendment free press and speech rights. (Camera, March 1, Daryl Gibson)

City Manager Bob Westdyke met with the city council and recommended that a cable permit be awarded to Storer Cable TV Inc. because Storer was the only bidder which said it would compete in the market if the existing

cable company, TCI, stayed in business.

But the Storer representative burst the bubble when he told council that his board of directors had met and cancelled the idea of competition. (Camera, March 11, Daryl Gibson)

City Attorney de Raismes met with TCI officials and reported they showed a willingness to renegotiate the terms of their present contract. They have a permit which expires in September of 1984 and that permit gives the council the right to revoke it without cause. But the lawsuit filed against the city has cast a shadow over the ability of the council to take such action.

The threat of renewed relations between the city and TCI did not sit well with Jack Kerner of Boulder Communications Co. He urged the council to "bite the bullet" and take the legal risk of ousting TCI. (Camera, March 12, Daryl Gibson)

Federal District Court Judge Richard Matsch declared the moratorium imposed by the city on TCI to be an illegal restraint of trade.

Representatives of other cable television companies said they were still interested in a contract with Boulder, but the judge's ruling is a setback in attempts by the city to cancel TCI's permit. They all refuse to compete in the Boulder market if TCI remains. (Camera, March 19, Daryl Gibson)

The 10th Circuit Court of Appeals in Denver ruled that Boulder had a legal right to stop TCI from stringing cable wire in February. That decision reversed the one by the Federal District Court.

Proposals from the other cable companies have been shelved by the city. (Camera, May 30, Daryl Gibson)

The city council slapped another moratorium on TCI to keep them from

extending lines. This moratorium will last until June 23 when the council will consider its options. (Camera, June 4, Daryl Gibson)

The council reached an impasse after more than two hours of debate and extended its moratorium through July 2. (Camera, June 24, Daryl Gibson)

The city council, after trying for one year to rid Boulder of TCI, gave the company a third of the city as its exclusive territory.

The rest of the city may be turned over to another company within three months if all goes according to plan.

~~TCI has had a permit to serve the entire city since 1964, but has~~ wired only portions of University Hill and Martin Acres. (Camera, July 2, Daryl Gibson)

The council can discuss but not carry out its districting plan for cable television until the plan is fully reviewed by the courts according to U.S. District Judge Richard Matsch. (Camera, July 22, Daryl Gibson)

Despite a federal court restraining order, the council adopted a cable television ordinance that restricts TCI to one-third of the city. (Denver Post, July 23)

The council's plan to divide the city into at least two cable TV districts was challenged by TCI in U.S. District Court. Judge Richard Matsch declared the districting plan to be unconstitutional. The city has appealed the decision to the 10th Circuit Court of Appeals. While it is waiting for that ruling, the city must withdraw its invitation to cable television companies to bid on the remainder of the market. (Camera, Oct. 6)

TCI questioned the approval of the districting plan on a technicality, claiming the ordinance was not properly published the first time the council approved it. So the council approved the cable districting plan for a second time and then put it on the back burner pending the Appeals

Court decision. (Camera, Oct. 8, Daryl Gibson)

##### Cultural Center

Library Director, and Project Director for the Arts Center, Marcelee Gralapp said the city council will have to determine the fate of the historic Boulder Theater and pick a site for a new performing arts center. By mid-March she wants the council to decide on a funding option for the center.

Architects have four possible sites: in the old Boulder Theater, in the Crossroads area, at Ninth and Canyon, or next to Central Park. (Camera, Jan. 27, Daryl Gibson)

Festival Two Architects was selected by the city to do the preliminary design on the proposed performing arts center which would have a large 750-seat theater along with a second complex made up of three smaller theaters. The total cost of the center is estimated between \$6 and \$8 million. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 13, Bob French)

Architect J Nold Midyette has recommended purchase of the old Boulder Theater, but said that if a choice has to be made, three smaller theaters to accommodate local performers should have first priority. (Camera, Feb. 13, Daryl Gibson)

The council voted five to two to pay \$37,000 in interest payments on the lease that Historic Boulder holds on the Boulder Theater. If the bond election passes, revamping the old theater will cost about \$2 million as opposed to building a new facility which would cost between \$4 and \$5 million. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 27, Bob French)

The council chose a 2.5 acre site on 13th Street which is owned by the city for two small theaters and practice facilities.

The site was chosen because it is within walking distance of the downtown mall and has the largest parking area of any of the proposed sites. Although it is in the flood plain, the architect told the council he could design a building that will withstand floods and not present other problems downstream. (Colorado Daily, March 6)

The city council agreed to repeal the city sales tax on food as an incentive to build a \$7.9 million performing arts center. Library Director Marcelee Gralapp proposed a half cent increase in city sales tax to pay off the arts bond. She said the change in tax would place the burden of repaying the bond on higher income earners while removing the "regressive" food tax thus easing the burden on low-income families.

Arts center supporters urged the council not to put the \$12 million recreation bond issue on the same ballot in the September special election. (Camera, May 7, Daryl Gibson)

The September 9 bond election will provide funding for a new Boulder Center for the Arts. Included in the plans for the center will be: A new building which will house a 250-seat experimental theater and a 350-seat performance theater, along with lobby, rehearsal space, and other support facilities; the complete renovation and restoration of the Boulder Theater into a 750-seat facility for large local productions, as well as touring shows; the completion of a recycling project that will finish turning a former warehouse into the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts containing three art galleries, classrooms, meeting rooms and workshop space. (Camera, Aug. 29, Eli Nixon)

The purchase and renovation of the Boulder Theater at 2032 14th Street accounts for \$2.4 million of the bond money. A total of \$600,000 would be put into the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts at 1750 13th Street. The remaining \$4.9 million would go for construction of a new performing arts

complex next to the visual arts center.

The new center would have only 50 parking spaces, but is near parking lots and the bus line. It would have to be constructed above ground because it is in the Boulder Creek flood plain. (Camera, Aug. 31, Daryl Gibson)

During the 20-year period it will take to repay the bonds, .15 of the tax will go to construction debt. Another .05 will pay for operation of the center, and the remaining .3 percent will go to the city's general fund to make up for the lost food tax revenue.

At the end of 20 years, the tax increase will be revoked, except for the .3 percent food tax replacement.

The council then will have to find another source of funds for operating the performing arts center, which Marcelee Gralapp admits will never be self-sufficient. (Camera, Sept. 7, Daryl Gibson)

The bond issue was defeated by a 54 percent majority. The city spent \$6,000 for a preliminary feasibility study, \$110,000 in architect's fees for the design of the center, and pledged \$37,000 to support Boulder Theater while the bond issue was pending. (Camera, Sept. 10, Daryl Gibson)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

Toxic and hazardous wastes have become a major problem because so many of them are integral parts of our 20th century lifestyle. Health officials are working to identify them.

There are three "high concern" waste sites in Boulder County and one is considered "the number one priority in the region". Marshall landfill could contaminate Louisville's drinking water. The other two are the Arapahoe Chemical disposal dump northeast of Lyons and a farm west of

Lafayette where 17 barrels of PCB are stored.

There are also two sites - one on the bank of Boulder Creek near the mouth of Boulder Canyon and one near 63rd and Valmont - where radioactive wastes have been discovered. Camera Outlook Edition, Feb. 17, Todd Malmsbury)

A county court jury reversed the verdict of a municipal court jury and found that Feyline Presents Inc., did not violate the city's noise ordinance during a May 1979 Folsom Stadium rock concert. (Camera, May 3)

The council approved amendments to the noise ordinance which exempt from noise laws any events which take place on land owned by another government agency, such as the University or the Boulder Valley School District. If citizens have a complaint about noise from events on such land, they will now have to hire a lawyer and sue under state noise laws. (Camera, June 4, Daryl Gibson)

A committee of 14 citizens were appointed to investigate the problem of noise from Boulder Municipal Airport. (Camera, July 2)

#### ENERGY

Some of the results of the city's energy conservation plan were discussed by Mike Connors in the March 12 edition of the Colorado Daily.

Last year the transportation department cut fuel usage by purchasing four cylinder cars instead of six or eight cylinder models.

In January the city began replacing light bulbs in residential street lamps with more efficient high pressure sodium lights. The sodium lights use 29 percent less energy than the mercury lamps the city had been using.

The sewage treatment plant has installed equipment to collect methane gas produced in the treatment process. The gas is used to fuel most of the plant

By October, the energy task force was winding down its two-year study. The 13-member group has met since September 1978 along with nearly 80

citizens who volunteered technical expertise.

Because of a tight city budget for next year, City Manager Bob Westdyke said the energy office would have to be eliminated when the grant money ran out. However, the council juggled some funds and came up with enough to save it.

City Energy Director Susan Stolz said the task force has not formally adopted the set of projects it will submit to council, but the list includes 60 ideas for reaching the goal of zero energy growth. (Camera, Oct. 19, Daryl Gibson)

In April, a retrofit solar system was installed in the city housing project at 1940 Walnut Street. In October, the city received a grant for solar heating and hot water systems in the planned units for the elderly at Canyon Pointe.

Boulder's first No-Drive Day was scheduled for July 16. The Boulder Energy Conservation Center asked everyone in the city to leave their cars at home to show just how committed they are to reducing oil imports and air pollution. (Camera, July 9, Todd Malmsbury)

There were more people on bikes, more walkers and carpools on July 16. However, ridership on the buses showed no increase and it was not evident that there were fewer cars on the road. (Camera, July 16, Todd Malmsbury)

The city applied to the federal government for a grant to conduct a feasibility study on the use of chicken manure for conversion to methane. The old wastewater treatment plant on East Pearl Street was suggested as the site for the experiment. (Camera, Nov. 17, Jeff Schier)

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board discussed at length the closing of the Bluebell Shelter road. The 43-year-old shelter was built as a Lions Club project for picnickers but has been used recently by illegal campers and beer drinkers who "unfavorably impact" the area.

One year ago, damage to the road made it impassible and the snowgate at the entrance has been closed since then while the parks department waited for funds to rebuild. The advisory board directed the staff to find other uses for the \$38,200 allocated to upgrade the road. (Colorado Daily, April 28)

A \$16.9 million bond proposal was defeated overwhelmingly April 28. The city council instructed staff to prepare a cheaper bond issue in the range of \$7 million in time for the November election. (Camera, May 21, Daryl Gibson) A \$7.4 million bond issue passed in November.

The city employed about 100 teenagers in the Junior Ranger program. They haul timber, reseed hillsides and maintain trails. Starting pay is \$1.75 an hour plus free passes to Boulder Reservoir. The total cost of the program is \$53,000, a fraction of what the city would have to pay to have regular employees do the work. (Camera, June 24, Todd Malmsbury)

The Long's Gardens property has been suggested as a logical place to expand the Frederick Law Olmsted Park. City councilmembers have declared informally that they will not condemn the property. The Long family has grown irises and kept the gardens open to public tours since 1916. (Camera, July 9, Daryl Gibson)

Coot Lake has become a quiet "swim-suit-optional" beach and the novelty has worn off. The word for the lake according to its lifeguards, is "boring". (Camera, July 19, Daryl Gibson)

The Parks and Recreation Department got permission from the Boulder Valley School District to distribute flyers outlining the two parks bond issues through the schools. Some principals and teachers refused to distribute them in their classrooms.

The flyers were paid for with tax money and therefore did not take a stand on the issues. (Camera, Nov. 3, Vicki Groninger)

Boulder has an estimated 75,000 trees of nearly 100 different species. About a dozen species comprise the majority, and about a fifth are on city property.

The forestry section of the Parks and Recreation Department was created to address the mountain pine beetle problem. City Forester Dick Shannon foresees plenty of work in town after that program is completed. There are 40 to 50 trees now that pose a threat in public rights-of-way. (Colorado Daily, Nov. 9, Jim Bock)

#### OPEN SPACE

The Open Space Board of Trustees recommended to the council that the boundary of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan be expanded by 2,515 more acres, including the 475 acre System Development Corp. site. (Camera, Jan. 15, Daryl Gibson)

A contract with the Boulder Rural Fire Department to fight fires in city parks and on open space land was endorsed by the Open Space Board. In May the board had approved pacts with the Pine Brook Hills and the

Cherryvale fire departments. A similar agreement is being negotiated with the Eldorado Springs Fire Department. The rural departments respond to fires in their service areas, but the city never has had a formal contract to pay them. (Camera, June 9, Daryl Gibson)

The city council voted to put Rex Walker, the manager of the city-owned Boulder Valley Ranch, on "probation" for poisoning prairie dogs with strychnine. He will have to pay \$5,000 and will not be able to lease any other city open space land for the next three years. (Camera, Aug. 5, Daryl Gibson)

The council authorized the city manager to make a lease-option agreement to purchase 277 acres of open space land south of Eldorado Springs Drive. The present owner is Thomas H. Dunn who will be paid \$2,100 and acre. (Camera, Oct. 7, Thad Keyes)

The city purchased the rights to control development, a "conservation easement", on 195 acres of land near the White Rocks for \$331,500. Energy Resources Technology Land Inc. (ERTL) owns 928 acres in the area. (The White Rocks, near Heatherwood between 75th and 95th streets shelter a rare fern and a unique bee.) The company applied to the county to build 51 homes on the land and began drilling for oil and gas there. The threat of development prompted the Open Space Board to upgrade its priority for acquisition. (Camera, Oct. 21, Daryl Gibson)

#### LIBRARIES

The Carnegie Library has become the object of a tug-of-war between the Boulder Public Library and the Boulder Historical Society.

The library has \$25,000 in grants set aside towards phase one of the

remodeling of the Carnegie building. Library Director Marcelee Galapp has had a plan for two years to use the old building as a city-managed archives.

The Historical Society submitted a proposal to the city manager for permission to use the building to house their documents and photographs. They have two archivists on their staff and are willing to cooperate with the city to make the Carnegie Library a repository for both city and society materials under the management of the Historical Society staff.

The city has thousands of documents, photos, and oral histories to go into the Carnegie building and is willing to add Historical Society items to the collection under the management of the city library archivist, stated Library Director Galapp.

She and the library commission showed little enthusiasm for the offer from the Society. It would have required the city to remodel the building, pay the maintenance and pay the Society \$25,000 a year "for them to administer what is essentially a library program," Galapp said.

She is convinced that state laws and the city charter would not allow the city to turn its documents over to anyone else. Nor could the library commission legally turn the management of a library program over to anyone else. (Camera, April 13, Daryl Gibson)

According to Library/Arts Director Marcelee Galapp, the city's temporary Arts Advisory Committee which has served two years, has helped promote civic involvement in the arts in Boulder. She suggests there should be a permanent 9-member commission. Along with the establishment of a permanent commission, Galapp is proposing adoption of a city policy to guarantee a source of income for the arts. (Denver Post, Jan. 21)

The city council awarded grants totaling \$60,000 to 34 Boulder artists

and performing groups. Projects approved include 21 in the performing arts, 12 in the visual arts and one literary arts project. (Camera, April 9, Daryl Gibson)

The Colorado Council on the Arts and Humanities selected three recipients for the 1981 Governor's Awards. One of the winners is Marcelee Gralapp, director of the Boulder Public Library and advocate of the arts.

She has succeeded in making the public library the center of a vital local arts network. The library has a fine collection of video tapes, a small gallery, and also holds the Colorado Artists Register.

In addition, the library hosts classical music concerts, films, performances in dance, theater and mime, poetry readings and discussion groups.

Working with the city council, she helped create a granting program for the city to distribute funds to Boulder artists. She was instrumental in creating the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts by helping obtain the use of a city-owned building. She also initiated and worked on plans for a performing arts center. (Colorado Daily, May 6)

For 1982, City Manager Westdyke has suggested a 13.1 percent cut in library services from 1981 levels. That translates into closing the Reynolds branch to half-time, closing the main library on Fridays, reducing the book budget by \$12,000 and trimming the equivalent of nine employees. (Camera, Sept. 4, Daryl Gibson)

##### Communications

Despite the fact that the 10th Circuit Court of Appeals has not reviewed the cable districting plan in Boulder, the U.S. Supreme Court agreed to hear the dispute. Its decision would determine whether a city is

immune from antitrust liability when it imposes a moratorium on cable television construction. (Colorado Daily, April 7, J. Maxwell)

In Boulder's attempt to get competitive cable TV, it has spent \$154,750 and may face legal bills of another \$55,000 to win a battle before the U.S. Supreme Court. (Camera, May 3, Daryl Gibson)

The 10th Circuit Court of Appeals issued an order to TeleCommunications Inc. to halt cable television hookups in Boulder while the judges consider the case. (Camera, May 16, Daryl Gibson)

The Court of Appeals ruled that the city can restrict a cable television company's service to certain geographic areas in Boulder. (Camera, Sept. 24)

##### Cultural Center

The council rejected a proposal from Assistant City Manager Jim Piper to use a building at 1728 13th Street for warehouse space. The building formerly housed the A-1 Ambulance Co. Mr. Piper said it was needed to house city park rangers who now share an office at the city yards, and the city parking control officers who work from the Regional Transportation District garage. He said the warehouse space in the metal building should be used for the rangers' fire trucks and for the boxes of old city records now stacked in the Carnegie Library.

The majority of councilmembers want Piper to consider proposals from two local groups who want the building for arts space. (Camera, April 23, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Association of Performing Artists and the Boulder Repertory Co. told the council they were ready to spend \$12,000 to remodel the building into a 100-seat theater. City fire marshals estimated the

cost to bring the building up to code at \$20,000 to \$30,000. (Camera, July 9, Daryl Gibson)

Richard Varnes, the city telecommunications officer and coordinator of city arts programs, said the cost for remodeling the building far exceeds what was originally estimated. So city officials have abandoned the idea of turning it into a little theater. (Camera, Dec. 3)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

The city council approved a noise abatement measure prohibiting tow planes from flying over residential areas. However, the city has no authority to enforce the regulation, since the Federal Aviation Administration governs aircraft once they leave the ground. (Denver Post, May 6, Bill Scanlon)

The only way the city can enforce a ban on tow planes over the city is to write it into the leases of glider schools and tow plane operators who rent space at the Municipal Airport.

The lessees say they have been cooperating in trying to avoid residential areas. They feel they are being discriminated against since the ban applies only to tow planes. (Camera, Aug. 1, Daryl Gibson)

#### ENERGY

Boulder has withdrawn its application to take over the hydroelectric generating capacity of Barker Dam. In September of last year, city officials learned that the Public Service Company's license was up for renewal and that federal law gives cities some preference in assuming those licenses. An application was prepared to preserve the city's option and to buy time to consider the ramifications of such a move. It was decided that the costs would outweigh the benefits. (Camera, Jan. 7, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Energy Conservation Center, a private, non-profit agency, is experiencing a sharp cutback in federal money. In 1980, the office had four full-time employees. Now it has only one and one-half. The center encourages people to use energy resources such as solar power, wind power and wood. It pushes for fuel-efficient cars, a good bus system, and bicycle paths. (Denver Post, Feb. 4, Jane Cracraft)

The city is experimenting with conversion of some of its vehicles to run on propane gas. Propane is much cheaper than gasoline at this time but the gap may close as demand increases. (Colorado Daily, July 3, Timothy Lange)

1982

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The city has purchased the former Boys' Club at 2445 Mapleton Street. It will be the home for the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department's gymnastics program. (Denver Post, March 31, Dave Price)

The city is improving accessibility for handicapped people to recreation facilities by installing cutaway curbs and ramps and by renovating parking spaces and restrooms. (Camera, April 19, Mike Connors)

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Foundation has taken on the job of creating an \$80,000 "sculpture park" at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard. The foundation is a private fund for receiving gifts to city parks programs. Its board is the same group as the city's Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. It has maintained a small fund for the historic train in

Central Park. (Camera, May 25, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Department has completed improvements to the land on the southwest corner of Ninth and Canyon. The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board and the Boulder Arts Commission have formed a joint committee to develop guidelines for completion of the sculpture park. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 24, Kathleen Smith)

Engine No. 30, its antique coach and caboose were moved to the north, away from the bank of Boulder Creek in Central Park. The parks department funded the \$20,000 moving project, but additional site improvements must come from private donations. (Camera, Feb. 28, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Jaycees and the Boulder Parks and Recreation Foundation sponsored a fund-raising "Casino Royale" to raise \$15,000 to buy a van for the city Parks and Recreation Department. It will be used to transport handicapped and low-income citizens to city recreation programs. (Camera, May 30)

A painting depicting a montage of Boulder parks scenes has been completed by Denver artist Jim Jansen. Limited edition prints and poster reproductions will be sold to benefit the Boulder Parks and Recreation Foundation. (Camera, June 24)

A statewide lottery with proceeds earmarked to parks programs is expected to bring Boulder \$200,000 a year beginning in 1984. City budget officials have proposed a cutback in the local tax money it puts into the parks department and using lottery money to fill the gap.

The city council has suggested that not all the 1.5 percent sales tax increase approved by the voters to pay back the parks bond issue will be needed for that purpose, and that any extra income can go into the city's

general fund.

The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board does not view these proposals "with much enthusiasm" and is drafting a letter to council registering its protest. (Camera, May 25, Daryl Gibson)

City Manager Bob Westdyke submitted a proposal to the council for the city Parks and Recreation Department to sell 3.2 beer at adult athletic events. It would net the department \$12,000 each year. The council said "no". (Camera, June 3, Daryl Gibson)

The city owns the golf course land at 5706 Arapahoe Road and leases it to the Flatirons Country Club for \$1 a year. In turn, public players are allowed to use the course for the same annual fees that country club members pay. The club is allowed to operate its private club on land it owns in the middle of the golf course.

An audit of the country club, requested and paid for by the city, has shown some discrepancies between actual income and income reported to the city over the past four years. (Camera, Sept. 29, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder City Council agreed to close Coot Lake to nudists in 1983. The decision was based on the reluctance to spend money on lifeguards, maintenance and policing of a beach for a select group during a tight economic period. (Camera, Dec. 15, Daryl Gibson)

At a joint meeting, the Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board and the Boulder Arts Commission voted unanimously to invite Andrea Blum, a New York sculptor, to develop a work for the city-owned site at the corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard. (Camera, Dec. 10, Donna Joy Newman)

#### OPEN SPACE

The Open Space Board reluctantly voted not to buy land at the mouth of Sunshine Canyon. The Public Service Company will pay Memorial Hospital \$262,000 for 1.44 acres of land and will install transformers and a control building. (Camera, Feb. 11, Daryl Gibson)

The Nature Conservancy will loan the city \$200,000 at 8 percent interest for two years to assist the city in the purchase of a conservation easement in the White Rocks Natural Area. The Martin Exploration Management Corp., which has the drilling lease on the land has cooperated in protecting White Rocks. (Denver Post, May 9, Joanne Ditmar)

For the third time in the fifteen year history of Boulder's greenbelt program, the city council has voted to condemn proposed open space land because negotiations with the owners have broken down.

The city will initiate proceedings to acquire 28 acres along Boulder Creek west of 55th Street known as Cottonwood Grove.

Last year one condemnation action was dropped when a sale was settled out of court. Still pending is the case against Flatirons Vista Inc. which owns land where System Development Corp. wanted to build its plant. (Camera, Aug. 4, Daryl Gibson)

The Open Space Board agreed to a two-year study to document Boulder County's deer herd, its health status, migratory routes and reproductive patterns. (Colorado Daily, Oct. 21, Kathleen Smith)

#### LIBRARIES

The hours of service at the George Reynolds branch library will be reduced from 69 to 40. The main library will be closed on Fridays

throughout the year. The cuts were made to meet the city's current budget for library services. (Colorado Daily, Jan. 21)

Councilmembers have gotten "a lot of heat" since 1982 budget cuts forced shorter hours at the libraries. They will consider charging for library cards to restore those hours.

Neither the library staff nor the library commission supports the implementation of fees for cards according to Library Director Marcelee Gralapp. She said library card fees would reduce the use of the library to those who could afford it and would increase the tendency to "rip off" books instead of paying the fee.

Nine full-time library staff positions were eliminated because of the budget cut. (Times-Call, March 29, Toni Kizer)

The city manager instructed all city department heads to base their budget requests for 1983 on an increase of only 8 percent over this year.

Library Director Marcelee Gralapp submitted a budget in accordance with the request. However, for the first time in her 22 years at the library, the proposal was not approved by the library commission. She does not like the proposal any more than the commissioners. It means the library would operate five days a week, have no concerts or films and would cut back services to the homebound and programs for children.

The commission will submit an alternative budget asking for an increase that would allow the library to reopen on Fridays, restore hours at the Reynolds branch and restore the book budget to the 1981 level. (Camera, June 8, Daryl Gibson)

The city council voted five to four to keep surplus library money generated by the general fund in that fund rather than appropriating it to the library fund, as has been done for years.

Under the change, the surplus will be separated into what was generated by the general fund (85 percent of the library's dollars come from the general fund) and what was generated by the library fund (the charter stipulates that one-third of a mill in property taxes plus the income from library fines, fees and gifts must be put in the library fund under the control of the library commission). Then only the income earmarked by the charter would go to the library fund.

The council's vote was considered to be on a policy change and was not on the council agenda for a public hearing. (Camera, April 23, Mike Stone)

Councilwoman Annette Anderson initiated the motion to change the way the library's funds are handled. She says her resolution brings the library "into conformance" with other city departments, but former library commission member Doris Hass calls the change "appalling".

In the past, rather than reverting back to the general fund at the end of the year as do balances from other city departments, the library fund balance has been retained and used to cover unexpected expenses, such as repairing the roof and adding shelving.

This has given the library commission a flexibility and autonomy lacking in other departments, in addition to an opportunity to save for long-term major projects.

Doris Hass says, "In the wisdom of the early citizens of Boulder who framed the charter, the library was separated from the political body." (Colorado Daily, May 3, Marty Durlin)

The library commission voted three to two to ask the city council to change a city ordinance which requires the library to maintain a smoking section. (Camera, March 9, Mike Connors)

The council adopted an amendment to the city code regarding smoking in

public places. Instead of saying that the library director must designate a smoking section in the library, the new version says the director "may" designate such an area. (Camera, April 7, Daryl Gibson)

The library commission asked the city council for \$9,644 to hire a staff person to study the feasibility of using volunteers to fill the gaps in library service.

City Manager Westdyke recommended the council reject the proposal and the council took his advice. (Camera, June 16, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Public Library offers more than printed resources. An Atari 800 microcomputer is available to anyone 10 years of age or older after a half-hour orientation session in the media center. (Camera, Feb. 28)

Four microcomputers are now available for free public use. They were donated by the Boulder Public Library Foundation and other private donors. The children's library has an Atari 400. In the reference room, adult patrons may use an Apple II Plus. The Atari 800 and an Apple II Plus are located in the media center. Speed reading, algebra, and college board preparation are some of the software in stock. (Camera, Sept. 19)

The library received an award from the Denver Regional Council of Governments in its regional cooperative service category. The IRVING consortium interconnects the library's computer with four other libraries and is housed in and managed by Boulder Public Library. (Camera, April 4)

Thirty-five local artists were awarded \$57,000 for 1982. The money comes from an annual fund set aside by the council to promote local artists. The Boulder Arts Commission received 84 requests and recommended the recipients to council. (Camera, April 10, Daryl Gibson)

The five-member Arts Commission is now established by ordinance. In addition to supporting and encouraging local artists, the commission supports mini-grants and rental assistance programs. The latter helps groups who have difficulty finding suitable or affordable performing space which is lacking in Boulder. (Camera, Sept. 12, Jeanne L. Manning)

Local artist Nancy Metcalf will recreate the large book soft sculpture, which was first done five years ago, for the children's section of the library. (Camera, Oct. 22)

##### Communications

On January 13, the U.S. Supreme Court voted five to three in favor of a claim by TeleCommunications Inc. (TCI) that the Boulder City Council had no immunity from federal antitrust laws when it attempted in late 1979 to keep TCI from wiring the city for cable TV.

The ruling did not find Boulder guilty of violating antitrust laws, but it sends the case back to U.S. District Court in Denver for a jury trial to determine whether that happened.

Under the new ruling, cities will have immunity from antitrust laws only if a state law grants them that immunity. (Camera, Jan. 14, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder bowed out of the cable TV regulating business when a settlement was agreed to between the city and TCI. In the proposed settlement, TCI will drop its lawsuits against the city. Its new permit will give TCI another 15 years on its permit. They will pay the city the same three percent of gross revenue as under the old permit. They will provide two public access channels and a production studio. The city cannot control programming or rates. (Camera, July 23, Daryl Gibson)

The council on an eight to one vote agreed to the settlement which gives TCI two years to wire the entire city. Councilman Phil Stern cast the only dissenting vote. (Camera, Sept. 8, Daryl Gibson)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

In order to monitor the success of the noise abatement program, the council appointed a seven-member committee. The committee hopes to develop the means by which the community can maintain low levels of aircraft noise pollution. Members hope to educate the public, pilots and users of the airport as to which flight patterns and landing and take-off procedures can be used to reduce aircraft noise. (Camera, Dec. 5)

#### ENERGY

The city is planning to increase the number of propane-fueled vehicles in its municipal fleet. Four vehicles experimentally converted last year have logged fuel cost savings of nearly 50 percent. (Colorado Daily, Jan. 28, Don Olsen)

The city council adopted a comprehensive solar access law that limits the shade a building can cast on its neighbor and requires new houses to be oriented to the south. (Camera, May 20, Daryl Gibson)

In the Colorado Daily dated June 9-10, 1982, Rosey Harty reported:

The City of Boulder's Energy Office is now at a major crossroads.

Since the Energy Office was founded in 1979 as the result of a \$242,000 grant from the Department of Energy, the Energy Office has served as a spawning ground for new ideas, a community-city government liaison and an information-gatherer of energy use and conservation measures. While a respectable amount of ground-laying work has been done, including the adoption of the solar access law, the role of the Energy Office has remained a planning one.

With the move of the office (announced in April) from the

Planning Department to the City Manager's Office, and the search for a new director, the office is preparing to set the strategies formulated over three years of planning into motion.

While the Energy Office will continue to coordinate some projects, others will be spun off into various city departments. Because so many current programs will fall into other departments, the logical place for the Energy Office, according to Deputy City Manager Dave Knapp, was the City Manager's office.

Another factor in the organizational move was that several of the programs require financial expertise and the city manager's office, home of the budget, is the best place to find it. In actively studying the city's proposed hydroelectric plant, a plan to use the pressure in the city's water system to spin electrical turbines and generate electricity, the city has to consider unanswered questions in terms of cost projection and financing strategies.

The Boulder Energy Project, a collaboration of the Energy Office and the state-funded Boulder Energy Conservation Center (BECC), will continue with most of the city's work in residential conservation, including the Stopgaps program.

According to (the Planning Department's energy coordinator Susan) Stolz, Stopgaps ran 25 workshops and gave out 600 weatherization kits through the Colorado Public Interest Research Group. Stolz said most of the education and demonstration duties of the Energy Office will be handled by Stan Zemler and the BECC.

Peggy Wrenn was named the new director of the City of Boulder Energy Office. (Colorado Daily, June 30-July 1, Rosey Harty)

1983

### PARKS AND RECREATION

The conceptual plan for \$2.4 million in improvements at Boulder Reservoir was approved by the city Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. The money will come from the parks bond issue passed in 1981. (Camera, Jan. 19)

Boulder's foresters are showing off the results of three years and

\$90,000 worth of work. Project Greenslope, a cooperative effort between the city and the Colorado State Forest Service has Boulder's forests on the mend. By selectively cutting trees, foresters have duplicated the thinning that nature accomplishes with fire, wind and insects. The result will be larger and healthier trees. (Camera, Feb. 20, Todd Malmsbury)

The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board passed a gently worded resolution aimed at keeping the council from diverting lottery funds from the parks budget to the general fund. The board suggests priorities for spending all the money on parks projects.

The budget office has suggested the council might cut the city's contribution to the parks and the open space departments by an amount equivalent to the lottery revenues to help the city through a tough financial time. (Camera, March 22, Daryl Gibson)

Sunbathing is allowed at Coot Lake, but nudity is not. The council's definition of nudity means that topless is acceptable for both sexes. The city will hire a new park ranger whose full-time job will be to patrol Coot Lake. The cost for enforcement personnel is estimated at \$47,000 as opposed to the \$121,750 to upgrade the facilities and hire lifeguards and police officers. (Camera, April 29, Daryl Gibson)

The fishing pond near Sixth Street and Canyon Boulevard has been around since the mid-1940's, stocked and maintained by the Boulder Fish and Game Club. Once available to adults, the pond is now open only to children from ages 6 to 12. A limit of three "keepers" per day is set for each fisher.

The Fish and Game Club maintains a rearing pond at Lincoln Place and Arapahoe Avenue and, from May to September, moves about 75 trout each week

to the fishing pond. (Colorado Daily, May 27-28, Kurt Chandler)

New York sculptor Andrea Blum is coming to Boulder to meet with people and gather ideas for the sculpture she has been chosen to create for the southwest corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard.

A five member panel of art experts selected Blum and their choice was endorsed by the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board and the Boulder Arts Commission. The Boulder Arts Foundation will attempt to raise \$50,000 in private funds to be matched with a grant it hopes to get from the National Endowment for the Arts. (Camera, Jan. 12)

The Boulder Arts Commission and the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board met jointly, then voted separately to accept Andrea Blum's proposed sculpture for the city

The design consists of three pavilions. Concerns expressed at the meeting were primarily about the safety of the sculpture (which some said was rather an example of modern architecture). One of the pavilions was three feet below ground and another eight feet off the ground. (Camera, April 12, Donna Joy Newman)

The controversial work of sculptor Andrea Blum may end up as a ballot question in the November municipal election. A group of protestors want the council to either remove Blum as the artist or take the issue to the voters. (Camera, April 23, Daryl Gibson)

The city council will stay out of the Blum dispute, according to Mayor Ruth Correll. "The council does not have jurisdiction here" since no city money is involved. Typically the council does not question park planning decisions and has no control over the Arts Foundation. "We feel that this is something the two boards have the responsibility for and we would like to see them work it through."

Boulder businessman Steve Tebo said he will start the petition to put the issue on the ballot. (Camera, May 4, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Arts Commission voted unanimously to approve Blum's latest design for the sculpture park. The revisions included coating the concrete with a washable surface, lighting the three pavilions from the inside and removing some features that city officials thought were dangerous such as a platform and a deep pit. (Camera, June 16, Daryl Gibson)

The city Parks and Recreation Advisory Board voted six to one to reject the Blum sculpture and start from scratch to find another artist. (Camera, June 21, Daryl Gibson)

The city Parks and Recreation Advisory Board decided to take over the Flatirons Country Club golf course management by 1986. (Camera, July 19, Daryl Gibson)

The council approved the proposed Sixth Street and Locust Avenue site for the North Boulder Community Park. Between 50 and 84 acres will be purchased. (Camera, July 20, Peg Maris)

Railroad Engine No. 30 was presented to the city exactly 30 years from the day in 1953 when it was first dedicated. Several of those who attended the original ceremony were at Central Park, including John B. Schoolland, who saved the locomotive from the scrap heap in 1951. (Camera, Aug. 7, Valerie Finholm)

The Wonderland Lake Park is part of a 30-acre site given to the city by the developer. All but the nine-acre neighborhood park will be kept in a natural state as trails and open space. The playground and open play areas will cost \$280,000.

The Shanahan Ridge Park includes 4.4 acres given to the city. Only

1.5 acres are being planted now because of the costs and because of some rare plants found on the site which the city wants left in their natural setting. It will have no playground equipment, just open play areas. (Camera, Sept. 13, Daryl Gibson)

The city and the state Division of Wildlife are cooperating on a study to determine the size of the deer herd near Boulder as well as some of its habits. Between 750 and 800 deer have been found in the study area west of Boulder. When the study is completed, the council will have to decide what needs to be done about Boulder's urban deer. (Camera, Nov. 17, Todd Malmsbury)

Boulder park rangers are asking for help to protect animals living in the mountain parks from illegal trapping. Last winter, fifteen leg-hold traps were confiscated. Three lodges of beaver apparently were trapped out of Sawhill Ponds and three dogs were caught in traps. (Camera, Dec. 13, Todd Malmsbury)

#### OPEN SPACE

The SDC land condemnation case will be decided by a three-man commission appointed by a district judge. At issue is whether the site should be valued according to its current zoning or whether it is worth much more because there was a "high probability" the land would have been rezoned for industrial use. (Colorado Daily, Jan. 19, Julith Jedamus)

The council approved the purchase of 24 acres of open space land on Heatherwood Drive, across the street from the elementary school. The purchase completes the acquisition of development rights and open space that buffers the White Rocks area. (Camera, Jan. 19, Sharon Gillen)

The council reluctantly added \$257,000 to the \$178,734 already budgeted to upgrade the dam at Wonderland Lake. When engineers drained the lake after making initial cost estimates, they found that more extensive repairs were necessary. (Camera, Oct. 19, Daryl Gibson)

#### LIBRARIES

For the first time in a year, Boulder Public Library will be open on every Friday. The council put \$136,000 back into the library budget after a year of public protest.

The budget is still tight. The number of free films shown at the library was trimmed by one-third last year and will stay at that level this year. The number of concerts was cut by one-fourth last year and will not be increased this year.

Nor will materials be increased. In 1966, the average cost of a book was \$8.18. In 1981, the average book cost \$25.30.

Library Director Marcelee Galapp noted that even with a 12 percent cut in operating hours at the main library last year and a 43 percent cut in hours at the Reynolds Branch, adult circulation of materials dropped only 1.5 percent in 1982. (Camera, Jan. 6, Daryl Gibson)

The city council, which cut the George Reynolds branch operations in 1982 to save money, voted to restore full-time status to the branch for 1984.

Marcelee Galapp said the Boulder Public Library Foundation has donated an IBM personal computer for public use to the Reynolds branch. (Camera, Dec. 31)

Marpa Landscaping is building a Japanese tea garden in an enclosed

patio of the library. The company brought a master gardener from Japan to conduct a series of workshops. Funds for the garden came from students' registration fees and from Marpa. The garden is a \$20,000 project. (Camera, Aug. 2, Peg Maris)

Marian Cooper, Director of Homebound Services, will be retiring after 17 years of dedicated service. The program has served the community since 1969, providing large-print books and audio materials to the blind and other disabled persons. (Camera, Jan. 30)

Cooper's primary job was to select the books that 78 active clients might want to read. She and volunteers delivered an average of 500 books each month to the homebound. Her successor will be Priscilla Simmons. (Camera, Feb. 7, Daryl Gibson)

A new volunteer program "to enhance the work of the library staff and to provide the public with an increasing range of services" will begin in 1984 under coordinator Nancy Morgan. (Denver Post, Dec. 28, Barbralu C. Manning)

A huge looseleaf "book" lies open on the floor of the children's room of the library. It is a three-dimensional, larger than life collage of upholstery fabrics, string, plastic and usually has children crawling about on it. It was designed and created by Boulder artist Nancy Metcalf. (Denver Post, Feb. 23, Barbralu C. Manning)

After two years of planning and \$100,000 in grants from federal and state sources, the Carnegie Library is back in the book business and open to the public for research. (Camera, Jan. 23, Daryl Gibson) The official dedication was held April 17.

The Colorado Room at Boulder Public Library housed many of the collections now contained in the Carnegie Library. Lois Anderton, Carnegie Library Coordinator and archivist said there are two special reference collections: Colorado history books and archives and a genealogical collection maintained by the Boulder Genealogical Society. These books are strictly for reference because they are too valuable to circulate. The library contains almost 300 oral history tapes. Federal grants obtained during the Bicentennial enabled volunteers to record many hours of history. (Boulder Summer Guide, 1983, Barbra Cohn)

The Carnegie Library staff launched a fund drive to fill the library with about \$15,000 in furniture without using tax money. In 1981, the city council agreed to a restoration project on the condition the library staff do all the work with donations and grants. Money was raised for building restoration and now Lois Anderton, librarian for the Carnegie, is confident that funds can be raised for furniture that will match the building's dark oak decor. (Camera, Nov. 8, Daryl Gibson)

This year the Boulder Arts Commission culled 120 applications down to 31 projects to share in the \$57,000 grants provided by the city. About \$20,000 of the arts budget goes toward staffing the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts. (Colorado Daily, May 11, Marty Durlin)

##### Communications

On October 29, 1982, the city and TCI ended their costly and bitter legal battle with a settlement in the U.S. District Court in Denver. As a part of the settlement TCI agreed to have the entire city wired within two years.

At the halfway point in that period, the majority of Boulder's

neighborhoods have cable access and the rest should be finished by June. TCI has about 6,000 subscribers.

TCI is fulfilling its local access requirement by offering three channels for Boulder programming. The University of Colorado is already using Channel 27 for limited programming. TCI wants to give Channel 28 to the city with a direct link to the library for municipal programming, but Richard Varnes, the city's TV liaison officer, said the city council would have to budget money for these programs. (Camera, Oct. 30, Daryl Gibson)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

The city will put up some money to protect neighbors of South Broadway from increased traffic noise. The council wants residents to consider forming a special assessment district that would collect a tax to help fund the project. (Camera, March 16, Sharon Gillen)

The city agreed to put up the \$151,000 estimated cost for guard rails and a noise wall between Ludlow and Greenbriar streets. The 19 residents whose homes back up to South Broadway will pay a maximum of \$25 a month for 15 years to share the cost with the city. The city will maintain the wall and pay for repairs from runaway vehicles. (Camera, May 4, Sharon Gillen)

#### ENERGY

Several years ago Phil Stern provided the city with a report on the potential of generating electricity through the city water system. His survey identified five sites at water treatment plants and pressure reduction stations with hydrogenerating potential.

Now a councilmember, Stern is encouraging the city not only to conserve energy but produce it. The most promising site for installation of an electric generating turbine is the Betasso Water Treatment Plant.

Stern says that a turbine there could generate up to 2.6 megawatts of power providing approximately \$300,000 per year in power profits. (Camera, Jan. 16)

Before the city begins work on Betasso, the Public Service Company and the city must agree on a price the utility will pay for the electricity. The case is under review by the Public Utilities Commission. (Camera, Oct. 26, Todd Malmsbury)

A new waste oil furnace was installed at the city yards to help lessen the fuel bills. After a year of efforts to meet fire regulations and to make the furnace operate efficiently, the furnace went into operation on February 8. (Camera, Feb. 18, Karen Boush)

The city council approved the donation of a used truck to Eco-Cycle to replace one of their vehicles destroyed in an arson fire. In exchange, Eco-Cycle will provide two hundred gallons of waste oil a month for the city's heater at the east Pearl Street yards. (Colorado Daily, March 1, Kurt Chandler)

A study showed that the city decreased energy consumption in its five main buildings last year by 2.4 percent overall. Despite factors of a colder winter and increased utility rates, the expenditures for energy use increased by only 7.5 percent. (Camera, Feb. 27)

The city has converted 20 of its vehicles to propane. Fleet Manager Dave Hopp figures that after two years, the pay-back point has been reached and the city is now breaking even on the conversions. Not only does the city save on fuel costs, but service on the vehicles' engines is required at lesser intervals.

However, the city has recently concentrated on buying sub-compact autos which are too small to accommodate the bulky conversion equipment, so it is doubtful any more vehicles will be changed to propane use. (Colorado Daily, Oct. 20, Kurt Chandler)

A city-sponsored low-interest energy conservation loan program has \$500,000 available for home weatherization projects. The loans will be made by the city's Housing Department at an interest rate at three-quarters the prime lending rate. Bankers who are providing the line of credit to the city can afford the lower rates because of the tax write-off they receive. The loan money will not apply toward solar retrofitting. (Denver Post Feb. 16, Dave Price)

The Boulder Parks and Recreation Department and the Energy Office hope to save \$9,823 a year with a solar swimming pool cover over Scott Carpenter Pool. It will be placed on the pool each year before the swimming season opens to heat the pool with solar energy. During the summer the cover will be rolled out when the pool is not in use to reduce heat loss. (Camera, June 13)

Although energy conservation is a stated goal of the city council, most councilmembers backed off from clearly supporting a proposal to require low-cost weatherization of the city's 15,000 rental units. They also rejected a proposal that would have mandated time-of-sale energy audits in owner-occupied homes.

Concerns were expressed for both the cost to renters and to landlords despite data collected by the Energy Advisory Board. (Colorado Daily, Oct. 13, Kathleen Smith)

In September, the city received three energy innovation awards from

the Colorado Council of Local Energy Officials. They were for the Betasso hydroelectric project, the solar access ordinance, and the energy loan program. (Camera, Oct. 2)

A solar heating system at Fire Station No. 6 will be put back into service two years after it was unplugged because it increased the station's utility bills. The system at the Gunbarrel site was funded by a federal grant. The problem was that the fluid running through the solar collectors would reach boiling temperatures in the summer and would have to be cooled by electric fans.

Another failed solar project was the hot water system at the Canyon Pointe senior citizens' housing project. That was disconnected in 1982 when the tube collectors on the roof kept exploding and had to be replaced. That project was also federally funded. Replacement of that system is being negotiated. (Camera, Nov. 11, Daryl Gibson)

1984

### PARKS AND RECREATION

Anna Dooling reported in the Camera dated August 5:

Under the old method of selecting a sculpture for a city park, a committee reviewed the work of a number of artists from around the country and picked one. The artist then did a model of the piece he or she would like to build, and faced the usual resulting storm of controversy.

That is the process that gave birth last summer to Andrea Blum's proposal, a three-part construction of concrete that was eventually shouted down by the public.

Under the new method of selecting a sculpture for a city park, the Arts Commission asks Boulder artists to submit models. It then gives the resulting designs to the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. The ... Board discusses them at a public meeting before sending them back to the Arts Commission. The Arts Commission then discusses them once more at another public meeting. It later makes a decision based

on the compatibility of the piece with the recreational function, historic character and natural features of the spot to be decorated; on the sculpture's representation of a broad variety of tastes within the community, on its safety and on its resistance to vandalism.

The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board recommended that the sculptures of local artists be erected at the Reservoir and the Fishing Pond. A 20-foot metal wave sculpture costing \$7,500 and a six-foot trout rising from the rocks costing \$2,500 were reluctantly chosen. Board members speculated that the recent adverse criticism from citizens may have limited the number and quality of submissions. (Camera, Aug. 21, Bruce Langer)

Boulder taxpayers got an extra park for their bond money. There were enough funds left over from those allocated for the north Boulder park to make a down payment of \$176,000 for 78 acres northeast of the Diagonal Highway for a future community park. The balance over the next 10 years will come from the permanent parks fund. (Camera, Oct. 13, Linda Cornett)

Ten percent of Boulder's 1984 lottery proceeds, about \$50,000, will go to a tree nursery located at East Pearl and 55th streets. The nursery will double the number of trees the city can use for parks and rights-of-way. (Camera, Nov. 19, Debora Getz)

The transition of Flatirons golf course to city operation won initial approval by the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. The agreement is subject to final approval by Flatirons Country Club, the city council and the city planning board. (Camera, Nov. 20, Debora Getz)

#### OPEN SPACE

The city council authorized \$1.5 million to buy the Teller Farm, 345 acres bounded by Valmont and Arapahoe roads, for open space. The land will

continue to be used for agriculture. It contains a 26-acre lake, as well as two oil and gas wells which are expected to generate \$6,200 in royalties for the city this year. (Camera, Feb. 22, Brighid Kelly)

Flatiron Industrial Park Co. has agreed to donate \$2.5 million in land along Boulder Creek for open space. It encompasses a three-mile stretch from the city's newly acquired Cottonwood Grove near 55th Street eastbound across 61st Street. The donation was arranged through the non-profit Colorado Open Lands which will sell the reclaimed land to the city for the "bargain sale" price of \$350,000. (Camera, March 29, Brighid Kelly)

The council approved paying Colorado Open Lands for the reclaimed 81 acres which was appraised at \$2.4 million. (Camera, May 16, Brighid Kelly)

The city purchased 75 acres of open space on Davidson Mesa north of South Boulder Road. Boulder County and the city of Louisville will each buy back a one-third interest in the land. Louisville wants part of the land so that it can expand its reservoir. (Camera, Aug. 22, Sharon Gillen)

The city council authorized Jim Crain, Boulder's open space director, to go ahead with an \$18 million bond issue to speed up the purchase of open space land for the city. That sum, plus \$5 million already in the open space fund, will pay for 4,200 acres around the city's eastern edge within the next three years. Ten new employees will be hired, almost doubling the current staff of 11. Three of these will be temporary. The seven permanent employees include two more office staffers, two rangers, two maintenance people and a farm and ranch manager. (Camera, June 16, Linda Cornett)

Eight plots of prairie totaling 269 acres have been designated as a

Colorado Natural Area. They are on city of Boulder open space in the flood plain of South Boulder Creek, between Cherryvale Road and the Boulder-Denver Turnpike. The land will receive special protection through a management plan agreed to by the state and the city because they contain a variety of grasses native to the western Great Plains.

The tall grass prairie is the second natural site in Boulder County to be protected under a 1977 state law. The White Rocks formation along Boulder Creek was the first. (Camera, Nov. 21, Todd Malmsbury)

The deer study, conducted over the past two years, is complete. Nearly 1,000 deer live on Boulder's western edge. Despite the large population, the animals are healthy. Councilmembers do not favor control based on hunting or trapping. They will focus on public education. (Camera, Dec. 4, Todd Malmsbury)

The council approved open space purchases totaling \$758,200. They include 80 acres on Gunbarrel Hill just north of Heatherwood subdivision and 56 acres east of 51st Street next to Six-Mile Reservoir. (Camera, Dec. 17, Linda Cornett)

#### LIBRARIES

The Boulder Public Library announced the publication of the first newsletter in its 78-year history. Its purpose will be to link the library with its patrons and to acquaint the public with all the library's services. It is being written entirely by volunteers under the supervision of Volunteer Coordinator Nancy Morgan. (Boulder County Edition, Feb. 16)

An IBM PCjr. will make its public use debut at the George Reynolds branch. It will be available free to the public upon completion of a half-

hour orientation. The Boulder Public Library Foundation is sponsoring development of the library's microcomputer program. (Boulder County Courant, March 21)

The Homebound Services division of the library announced that closed caption video tapes and films and a teletypewriter phone are now available for the hearing impaired. (Camera, April 29)

The city has reached a settlement with DataPhase Corporation. In 1979, Boulder paid \$219,000 for a computer system for the library which would streamline circulation, provide information to patrons, keep track of magazines and newspapers and perform other book-keeping jobs.

The circulation system was installed and has been in use since 1979. But DataPhase never delivered the remainder of the promised services.

Over the past four years, the city has negotiated with three sets of DataPhase governors as the company has undergone two reorganizations.

The latest round of negotiations brought about the settlement approved by the city council.

The agreement provides that the city will pay \$285,000 for a new Tandem hardware system. The hardware would cost over \$400,000 on the market. DataPhase has agreed to throw in software worth \$200,000.

The library will provide \$205,000 of the purchase price; \$78,000 of that is money withheld from DataPhase because of the city's dissatisfaction with their performance. The city will provide \$80,000.

The library's card catalog will be entered into 15 to 20 computer terminals for library patrons to use. A crew of trained volunteers will help computer-shy readers get used to the terminals. They will be placed at the main library, the George Reynolds branch, the Carnegie branch and at the Broomfield and Louisville public libraries.
