In a few years the computer system may be available to residents with home computers. (Camera, June 27, Linda Cornett)

The first in a series of rotating exhibits is on display at the Carnegie branch. In the display are Boulder County government documents dated 1859-1875 that were saved from the old Courthouse during the fire of 1932.

The oak and glass case used for the rotating exhibit was donated by the Buckingham Trust. (Lafayette News, May 9)

The Boulder Public Library has re-established the information desk on the first floor of the main library. The service was discontinued several years ago as a result of budget cuts. It has returned due to the efforts of a volunteer staff under the supervision of Nancy Morgan. (Boulder County Courant, July 18)

Space in the Boulder Public Library and its two branches comes to less than half the 90,000 to 100,000 square feet recommended for a city the size of Boulder.

The Library Commission has come up with a plan to add a third story to both wings of the original main library (adding about 15,000 square feet), to build a 5,000 square foot addition to the Reynolds branch, to build an east branch of about 15,000 square feet and a north branch of about 8,000 square feet by 1995.

Their goal for 1989 is 44,000 additional square feet. (Camera, Dec. 10, Linda Cornett)

The Boulder City Council approved new guidelines for selecting artwork for city parks. They gave the Boulder Arts Commission final say on the

projects after the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board screens and accepts all candidates. (Camera, April 4, Brighid Kelly)

##### Communications

There is the possibility the city may schedule a cable election in 1985 to determine whether voters want to extend a 20-year franchise to Boulder Cable TV, a subsidiary of TCI.

In February, the city of Cortez was ordered by the state Supreme Court to hold a franchise election. The court ruled that a permit, which can be granted without an election, is a temporary use of a public right-of-way. A cable system, which involves stringing or burying cables, is a permanent use and requires a franchise. A franchise can only be granted by a public vote. (Camera, Oct. 12, Linda Cornett)

##### Cultural Center

Macky Auditorium will receive \$375,000 from the city to help renovate the 75-year-old building. The council also approved spending \$100,000 to update plans for a proposed performing arts center and \$20,000 to improve the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts.

In exchange for helping with the renovation, the city will get 16 rent-free days yearly for the remainder of the auditorium's life. In addition, the city can use the auditorium's gallery five months each year and will get 75 days free yearly, for five years, at the Old Main Chapel.

Other uses the city will receive include the Music Hall, Opera Theater, University Theater, Henderson Museum, and rehearsal rooms. The university also agreed to give the city the children's fishing pond between Sixth and Ninth streets and Canyon and Arapahoe. (Camera, March 21, Brighid Kelly)

Rent-free space was available for the first time to selected local artists through the 1985 City of Boulder Arts Program. The space is located in University of Colorado facilities. Some limitations on dates will be imposed. (Boulder County Courant, Dec. 5)

Meetings have begun among several groups interested in a performing arts center - the city council's newly formed steering committee, a citizen's group, the Boulder Assembly for the Arts and Humanities, the Downtown Task Force - and the members of each are attempting to decide how the center should be paid for and what size it should be.

Opinions vary widely and some say the only way a performing arts center can be built in Boulder is if it is not a performing arts center. If it is to succeed at all, it must appeal to more than art lovers.

The city's steering committee consists of the five members of the Arts Advisory Committee, two representatives from city council, and nine people from the community. Library Director Marcelee Gralapp is the staff liaison. (Camera, June 10, Anna Dooling)

The steering committee on the Cultural Center has been meeting for three months to find out what everybody wants in the way of a cultural center. After being asked by Co-Chairperson Kaye Howe to "just imagine yourself making a decision", committee members made tentative plans for two small theaters, a larger theater, rehearsal rooms, etc. They also said the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts should be renovated. (Camera, Aug. 2, Linda Cornett)

The committee proposal to the city council included a \$10 million cultural center at 1750 13th Street, a \$450,000 renovation of the Center for the Visual Arts, the purchase of the Midland Federal Savings building for use as exhibition and meeting space, and the setting up of a \$2 million

endowment fund to produce operating income.

Funding for the proposal would come from either an additional one cent of sales tax for 12 to 14 months or and extra half-cent for 24 to 28 months.

Chamber of Commerce Executive Vice-President Rich Hadley said a Chamber survey showed more than 60 percent of city residents polled would support the cultural center proposal.

Still to be decided is who would design the center. Boulder architect J Midyette, who designed the rejected \$7.8 million center a few years ago, has been hired by the city to work with the planning committee. His focus is on a "multi-pod" theater - a main theater with 400 seats and two circular theaters with 175 and 225 seats. Those smaller areas could be joined to the main theater or used for small productions or meetings.

The committee rejected several other proposals, among them: Building a large theater; buying the Boulder Theater; creating a public-private partnership to establish shops or other private spaces; and backing a proposal for a 13th Street pedestrian corridor from the Boulderado Hotel to Macky Auditorium. (Camera, Sept. 7, Linda Cornett)

The proposed \$14 million cultural complex was approved by the council. They also voted to have J Nold Midyette complete his conceptual design for the building, but voted to ask a consultant to advise them on how to pick an architect to complete the project. The council delayed a decision on how to pay for the complex. (Camera, Sept. 19, Linda Cornett)

The council tentatively decided to set a city-wide vote on a proposed cultural center, but not to commission an architect to design the building until after a vote. (Camera, Oct. 10, Linda Cornett)

The council approved a revised contract with J Nold Midyette. He will

be paid \$39,000 for planning the interior of the proposed cultural center.  
(Camera, Oct. 17, Linda Cornett)

#### ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION

Councilman Spenser Havlick will ask the city to test the high-country lakes in Boulder's watershed for any increase in acidity. (Camera, Nov. 13, Todd Malmsbury)

The Eighth Street landscaping project, a successful public/private partnership, has been completed. The landscaped median was constructed to lessen traffic impact in the neighborhood as well as to create a visual barrier between the commercial zone on Pearl Street and the residential area of Mapleton Hill. (Camera, Dec. 2)

#### ENERGY

The city will enter into a dam repair contract with the Public Service Company (PSC) and will gain perpetual right to store 8,000 acre feet of water in Barker Reservoir plus the use of PSC's pipeline to transmit the water to Betasso Treatment Plant. In return for the city's participation in the repairs, PSC has agreed to give the city a special power purchase rate structure for hydroelectric generating stations on raw water supply lines above the treatment plant. (Times-Call, March 14, Juliet Bourne)

A hydroelectric generator has been installed and is operating at the Maxwell Pump Station site near Linden and Wonderland Hill avenues. The amount of power generated from the facility is enough to heat 600 Boulder homes. (Public Works, Vol. 1, No. 4, Nov. 1984)

The city announced a program to provide special energy grants to moderate- and low-income homeowners and tenants. It provides reimbursement

of 50 percent of the costs of weatherization with a maximum grant of \$1,250 per family. The moneys come from the Federal Energy Bank of the U.S. Dept. of Housing and Urban Development. (Camera, March 18)

The council approved an ordinance mandating landlords to install draft sealers behind electrical outlets, insulation on water heaters, and door and window weatherstripping. They also must caulk cracks and holes and have tune-ups on furnaces every three years.

The measure will become effective October 1. City inspectors will check to see if the law has been carried out only when rental licenses are up for renewal. Licenses are staggered, expiring at different times of the year. (Denver Post, May 9, Pam Kruger)

Low interest loans are again available for rehabilitation and energy conservation projects in residential homes. The program was suspended nine months ago when federal legislation expired. Congress has now restored the program. Participants can borrow at a low interest rate and may apply for loans up to \$25,000 for 15 years. They are available only for homes built before 1960, and 70 percent of the loan must be devoted to improving the home's energy efficiency or removing code violations such as faulty wiring or plumbing. (Camera, Oct. 11, Linda Cornett)

# CHAPTER VI

## HOUSING AND HUMAN RELATIONS

1980

### HOUSING

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of April 1 that:

High land costs have priced Boulder out of a proposed low-income housing project on Spruce and 21st Streets ... .

Last year the city set aside \$1 million in community development grants ... to buy the property ... .

City Manager Bob Westdyke will recommend ... that the Spruce Park development be dropped and that ... (the) funds ... be given to other housing developments.

In May, the city council adopted a policy to regulate the price of moderate-income homes for 15 years if those homes were built to fulfill city requirements to have a certain number of moderately priced homes in all new subdivisions. The policy is aimed at discouraging the immediate resale of the homes for windfall profits. (Camera, May 7, Daryl Gibson)

The policy affects almost 40 new homes per year built under city laws. One requires developers to put 15 percent of the homes in new subdivisions in the moderate price range and the other, the Danish plan, gives incentives to developers who build cheaper homes.

When a buyer purchases a price-controlled house, he can resell it only under the moderate-income program. The house's price can escalate at the same rate as median income for the Denver metro area, but not at the rate of most housing, which has been twice as high. (Denver Post, May 22, Jane Cracraft)

In the Camera dated September 23, Daryl Gibson reported:

The city of Boulder finished the 1979-80 fiscal year as the owner of 386 rental housing units for the elderly, handicapped and poor ... .

Another 580 units for low- and moderate-income families are owned by private, non-profit corporations. A total of 165 households receive federal rent subsidies through the Housing Authority to live in privately owned rented homes.

A handful of housing units are provided by the authority under

special programs such as Echo House for families needing emergency housing.

... the authority opened a condominium conversion fund to buy the apartments of low-income renters who are threatened with eviction when the unit is converted to condominium ownership. The policy of the city will be to use an initial pool of \$150,000 to make down payments on the condominiums. The pool will be replenished by money from the sale ... when the low-income tenant moves out.

During the fiscal year the authority spent \$208,976 rehabilitating 19 homes privately owned by low-income families.

The Emergency Family Assistance Association (EFAA), which took over the Free Inn and renamed it Echo House "runs it beautifully, but ... have no money to pay the rent," said John Hooyer, director of the Boulder Housing Authority. The city has agreed to carry the mortgage payments through 1980, while EFAA tries for grant money to buy the house and remodel it. (Camera, July 6, Kathy Sipfle-Taylor)

Apartment vacancies in Boulder, especially in the area near the University, increased dramatically this year.

Miles Silverman, city housing planner, attributed the rise in vacancies to higher rents. (Camera, Aug. 5, Sharon Massingill)

The average house price in Boulder in 1980 was over \$100,000. (Camera, Aug. 25, Betsy Bates)

### HUMAN RELATIONS

Karen Thoreson, Boulder's day-care planner, prepared a list of suggestions for workers who want employers to provide day-care programs. These vary from setting up a referral service and subsidizing cost of day-care to providing on-site centers. (Camera, Outlook Edition, Feb. 17, Michelle Martin)

Although day-care is considered a contemporary issue, the Boulder Day Nursery has provided this service continuously since 1918. It has occupied

its present home at 1518 Spruce Street on city-owned land since 1930.  
(Camera, Aug. 17, Glennys McPhilimy)

The "Little School" education program for children living in the San Juan del Centro housing project was awarded \$10,000 in operating expenses for 1980 by the city council.

The program is an after-school experience to teach mostly minority children who need language skills, the basics and cultural adjustment.  
(Camera, Jan. 9, Daryl Gibson)

The city has an ordinance prohibiting discrimination in housing, employment, and public accommodations based on race, color, religion, sex, and national origin, and a separate prohibition on age and marital status discriminatory practices in employment.

Complaints received by Kon Damas of the Human Resources Department are about discrimination in employment against the 40-65 age bracket and discrimination in housing against students, the handicapped and single people. Damas has received some complaints about retaliatory evictions by landlords. (Camera, March 8, Daryl Gibson)

Two ordinances proposed to the Human Relations Commission deal with landlords' right of entry and written leases for rental properties. Boulder Tenants' Union Director Mark Fearer claimed the laws are necessary because landlord-tenant mediation is inadequate to resolve the problems of privacy violation and landlords not providing leases. (Camera, July 19, Peg Maris)

Kon Damas, director of the Human Rights Office, said that a study shows that much of the rental market is not available to people with children. He said the Human Relations Commission is considering an

amendment to the city's anti-discrimination ordinance to try to change this pattern. (Colorado Daily, March 14, Bob French)

The proposed law was still in the planning stage in the fall. It will probably cover discrimination in housing on the basis of family status, marital status, student status, and disabilities. It would apply not only to rentals but to housing purchases also. (Times-Call, Sept. 24)

A group of activist Boulder renters is circulating a petition to require the council to vote on the subject of standard leases. They want a committee of landlords and tenants to draft a model lease which the landlords would be forced by law to use.

One possible lease drafted by the Renters' Rights Project includes a provision requiring landlords to pay interest to the renter on security deposits.

The group is also pushing the council to adopt an anti-discrimination ordinance which would prohibit landlords from banning children from rental housing. That ordinance is being drafted by the Human Relations Commission. (Camera, Oct. 29, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder senior citizens told the city council that they have a fine Senior Citizens Center, but they can't use it.

The center has only 36 parking spaces and permits issued to 1,250 cars to use the lot.

The council responded with a unanimous vote to approve \$30,000 to turn part of the proposed landscaping around the center into 17 more parking spaces. (Camera, Feb. 20, Daryl Gibson)

The ordinance establishing a Department of Human Resources which was adopted in October 1973 makes the statement that "changing social conditions, issues and problems require local government leadership and

participation." It charges the department with the responsibility of providing city government with continuous evaluation of such conditions. Since 1973, the department has issued annual Social Reports listing areas of concern and recommendations for action.

The report published this spring contradicts some of the Boulder myths about the community. It states that nearly 36 percent of the city's households have a yearly income of less than \$10,000. The divorce rate in Boulder is lower than Denver's and a significant number of Boulder residents are "anxious, worried, nervous or depressed." (Camera, March 27, Daryl Gibson)

A \$15,000 Community Development grant channeled through the city is providing assistance for eight handicapped persons. The grant money is set aside specifically to remove the architectural barriers which make independence difficult or impossible for many handicapped residents. (Denver Post, July 30, Linda Cornett)

In December, the Human Relations Commission voted to recommend that the money previously given to Planned Parenthood for birth control for low-income women be transferred in 1981 to the People's Clinic.

Internal disputes at Planned Parenthood had led to the firing or resignation of employees and a consequent disruption of services. (Camera, Dec. 9, Mike Stone)

1981

### HOUSING

San Juan del Centro, a privately owned housing complex, has run into

problems of the overcrowding of its units by Asian refugee families. City Housing Authority Director John Hooyer ordered an investigation by housing inspectors after police reported five families were living in one apartment. San Juan officials promised to come up with a plan to disperse the families. (Camera, Feb. 3, Daryl Gibson)

Last year the council gave the Housing Authority power to impose secondary sales restrictions on houses built under city moderate-income pricing regulations. Until then, first-time buyers could turn around and resell the units for market value and reap a windfall profit.

The first development to market condominiums under the new policy was Aspen Grove at 28th Street and Kalmia Avenue. The owners of that project claim the units are not salable with resale price controls. The authority agreed to reduce the control time from 15 years to 10 years and agreed to some other changes. (Camera, April 30, Daryl Gibson)

The Housing Authority has owned the Echo House emergency shelter since 1976. It has voted to sell the house at 929 Marine St. to the city for \$102,533. The city, in turn, will sign a contract with the Emergency Family Assistance Association to manage the house for 20 years.

The purchase money comes from the city's Community Development program. (Camera, May 5, Daryl Gibson)

Two new city-owned, low-income housing projects are ready for construction. A 46-unit apartment and townhouse complex is scheduled for 3150 North Broadway. The U.S. Dept. of Housing and Urban Development is reviewing plans for 30 townhouses, called Diagonal Plaza, on 30th Street near the Longmont Diagonal highway. (Camera, Sept. 7, Daryl Gibson)

A family would need an income of \$23,550 per year to qualify to buy the cheapest home under Boulder's Moderate-Income Sales Program prices approved by the Housing Authority. The least expensive home was set at \$44,300. The buyer is required to keep the price of the home in a moderate range in future resales for at least 10 years. (Camera, July 31, Daryl Gibson)

### HUMAN RELATIONS

Karen Thoreson is head of the Boulder Child Care Support Center, the city-sponsored program which is three months into the task of training and encouraging child-care providers.

The center, in the old City Electric building at 2160 Spruce Street, offers workshops for day-care providers, circulates information, and lobbies on behalf of day-care. It is the only resource center of its kind in Colorado.

The city began discussing the project five years ago, but by the time it opened last November, federal funding had disappeared. Through an exchange with two child-care agencies in the community, the center was able to start its training program.

The city helps with \$38,000. Local businesses and service organizations contribute to the center and a federal lunch subsidy program, funneled through the center to day-care facilities, adds another \$38,000. (Denver Post, Feb. 18, Linda Cornett)

Senior Center Director John Torres has suggested a membership fee to use the senior center after losing grant money for two staffers. The center has a \$348,000 operating budget this year, \$151,000 of which Torres must raise through fees for classes, special events and field trips. The

proposed fee has drawn numerous complaints from senior citizens who already pay city taxes to operate the center.

Torres is also under fire for a decision to close the indoor shuffleboard courts at the center and use the space for a senior day-care program. (Camera, July 4, Daryl Gibson)

The Senior Center was awarded \$18,600 by the city council to make room for a senior day-care program which has divided center users as no other issue ever has.

Director John Torres asked for the grant to convert part of the center into space for an innovative day-care program that diverts seniors from nursing homes.

The grant is part of a package of Community Development Block Grants coming to the city in 1982.

For more than a year, the center has sponsored the day-care program which has about 15 clients who are either physically handicapped or emotionally withdrawn.

Torres has secured state Medicaid money to expand the program to take in some low-income clients but needs more room to accommodate the increased work load. (Camera, July 22, Daryl Gibson)

The Senior's Employment Referral program at the Senior Center put more than 200 senior citizens back to work last year. (Denver Post, Aug. 19, Linda Cornett)

A proposal to ban adults-only housing in Boulder was under fire at a Human Relations Commission meeting. The commission has drafted an ordinance which would prohibit discrimination against students, families or the handicapped in housing rentals or sales. (Camera, Jan. 13, Daryl Gibson)

The city council tabled the controversial adults-only housing ordinance for six months. Two weeks ago, the council enacted an ordinance banning housing discrimination based on physical or mental handicaps. (Camera, May 6)

An existing state law covers the physically handicapped, but previously, complaints of discrimination had to be filed in Denver. After August 1, complaints can be filed at the city's Human Resources Department. (Camera, June 21)

The Boulder Alliance for Community Awareness plans a petition drive in an attempt to force the city council or voters to decide for or against a ban on adults-only housing. (Colorado Daily, May 13, J. Maxwell)

The city council voted 6-2 to ban discrimination against children in housing. Boulder is the first city in Colorado to prevent landlords from refusing to rent to families with children, except in special cases such as a small, owner-occupied rental complex or an apartment building restricted to tenants 55 years of age and older. (Denver Post, Nov. 18, Jane Cracraft)

Members of the Boulder County Tenants' organization and the Renters' Rights Project have begun circulating a petition to initiate a city ordinance that would force landlords to pay interest on security deposits.

They hope that the city council will enact the ordinance. If not, the petition, with enough signatures, would require council to place the issue on the November election ballot.

The Renters Rights Project is circulating another petition asking council to hold an election on a proposed rent-control plan. (Camera, April 18, Daryl Gibson)

Governor Richard Lamm reluctantly allowed a bill banning rent control in Colorado to become law without his signature.

The new law kills a rent control petition drive under way in Boulder.  
(Camera, June 20, Thad Keyes)

A group of 15 citizens were appointed by the city council to prepare a fair standardized lease. (Colorado Daily, Feb. 13, Chip Hinds)

The 12th annual spring debate in Boulder - what to do about the transients - is under way again. The police department position is that a crash pad or soup kitchen or a referral center will only attract more transients to Boulder.

George Bernzen, a former city councilmember is working to help establish a new privately run crash pad. (Denver Post, April 27, Jane Cracraft)

The annual social report concentrated this year on reasons why people leave Boulder. Only 10 percent gave high housing prices as the reason.  
(Camera, Sept. 30, Daryl Gibson)

1982

### HOUSING

An agreement to save seven Canyon Pointe apartments for mentally retarded tenants was canceled by the Boulder Housing Authority.

Under the agreement with the Boulder County Board for the Developmentally Disabled, seven clients under the supervision of a live-in counselor were learning independent living skills.

When, after six months, the counselor resigned, conflicts arose over the lifestyles of the retarded tenants. (Camera, Feb. 25, Daryl Gibson)

The Housing Authority has purchased two condominium units to keep two low-income tenants from being displaced.

The program was established to help tenants when their apartments were being converted to condominiums. Although the program was advertised to tenants each time an apartment complex was converted, no one applied until late 1981.

About \$35,000 of the \$150,000 starting fund came from the Colorado Division of Housing. (Camera, March 13, Daryl Gibson)

The Housing Authority offers two types of financial help for housing rehabilitation. Loans below market interest rates may be obtained or grants are available to low-income persons. (Camera, May 16)

In 1982, the qualifying income will be \$28,300 in order to purchase a house for \$51,450 under the Moderate Income Sales Program. However, there are currently no finished units available for sale under the program. (Camera, Dec. 30, 1981, Daryl Gibson)

Between 1970 and 1980, the average value of a house in Boulder rose from \$23,400 to \$86,500, an increase of 270 percent. During the same decade, the average home value in Colorado increased 273.4 percent and the national average jumped 177.6 percent. The prices are from the 1980 census forms and are not actual sales prices. (Camera, June 16, Daryl Gibson)

### HUMAN RELATIONS

A model lease for use by Boulder landlords on a voluntary basis was approved by the city council. The lease was the product of a year and a half of negotiations between local landlords and tenant activists. After an 18-month test of the non-mandatory lease, the council will review the

record. (Camera, May 4, Daryl Gibson)

This fall the Learning Center at San Juan del Centro will "graduate" its first class into kindergarten.

When resident Brenda Lyle and others heard complaints last year about how poorly San Juan children were performing in their first year in school, she turned the recreation hall into a version of a Head Start program. There was little money, but plenty of volunteer labor and donated supplies. The city offered the school the use of a neighborhood center owned by the city near San Juan. It charges no rent but the Learning Center must pay for utilities and insurance.

Karen Thoreson, city child-care coordinator, is helping school personnel to meet state day-care standards and to contend with other red tape that comes with growth. (Camera, July 5, Daryl Gibson)

John Torres, director of Aging Services for the city, has assumed the position of president of the Colorado Gerontological Society. (Camera, Sept. 29)

Although the Youth Services Division, as a part of the city's Department of Human Resources, is facing federal and state funding cuts, it still has a number of programs through the Youth Action Center.

For the past 11 years the center has provided coffeehouses and counseling for Boulder's youth, according to Youth Services Director Randy Gunnoe.

In June, the center began to train 30 volunteer youths to manage a phone line called YOUTHREACH. The line will provide resources and information about counseling and activities in Boulder. The teenagers involved will be able to receive credit for their services from their high

school.

To pick up some slack in the action center's counseling of troubled teenagers, Gunnoe said the city's Family Resource Center will provide counseling for youths, and a newly-formed Youth Support Center will counsel individuals, groups and families.

The Youth Services Division is also planning a non-profit business called Youth Enterprises, Inc. "to provide training and management opportunities". (Colorado Daily, June 30-July 1)

The indoor shuffleboard courts at the Senior Center will be remodeled with roll-up carpets so they can be used in the mornings by players and in the afternoons as an activity room for the senior day-care program. (Camera, July 21, Daryl Gibson)

The Human Relations Commission recommended withdrawing funds from the Sex Information and Resource Center. The center has not met the criteria established by the city council that an organization receiving city money be private and non-profit and have an established board of directors.

Since the center opened in October 1977, its staff members have worked to promote positive, non-exploitative relationships and prevent sexual crises among Boulderites. (Camera, July 29, Jan McCoy)

Next year the city will spend nearly \$1.4 million on human services, about \$450,000 of it through private non-profit agencies.

Budget recommendations for such agencies come from the Human Relations Commission, a group of nine citizens appointed by the city council.

Allocations this year are unusually difficult. Human Services programs are suffering from the effects of reduced funding due to the Reagan administration cuts, a higher client rate because of rising

unemployment and an unwillingness from the private sector to give because of inflation and the economy in general.

The commission had to make tradeoffs. It recommended support of 18 programs in 14 agencies which "provide critical health and social services to Boulder's citizens." It based its decisions on seven criteria established by the city council, including: A need for the program in Boulder, realistic goals, a lack of duplicating services, community support, adequate management, reasonable cost, and fiscal responsibility..

Agencies funded at their requested levels include Attention Homes, Boulder County Legal Services, The Center for People with Disabilities, the Emergency Family Assistance Program, the Family Resource Center, the People's Clinic and the YWCA Children's Alley. (Colorado Daily, Sept. 29, Marty Durlin)

The Human Relations Commission rejected a law which would require landlords to pay tenants interest on security deposits. Tenant activists and Councilman Homer Page vowed they would bring it up again. (Colorado Daily, July 21-22, Kathleen Smith)

No one knows how many homeless people there are in Boulder. Their numbers are increasing with unemployment, escalating housing costs and the spreading practice of discharging less severely afflicted mental patients.

Although the Emergency Family Assistance Association provided shelter to 203 families last year, the organization cannot begin to meet the needs of all the people who need beds.

Sister Donna Ryan has formed the Task Force on Shelter and Food for the Poor made up of people from the private and public sector. The task force, which is looking at cheap motels, quonset huts, warehouses and other empty buildings, would like to provide minimal shelter this winter on a

short-term, emergency basis. The shelter would be supervised by volunteers and be funded by churches, charitable organizations and individual gifts.

The entire subject of providing shelter is perceived as a political hot potato. On the one hand are those who insist that shelters are nothing more than magnets for "undesirables". Others argue that every community on the face of the earth has people who are homeless and that to deny their existence is to ignore reality.

Conceding that it is difficult to find a shelter in Boulder which meets zoning requirements, City Manager Bob Westdyke pointed out that the city takes neither a pro nor a negative stance on emergency housing. The city's obligation is only to determine whether or not such a shelter meets building codes and zoning requirements, he said.

As one of the city's veteran "crash pad" hosts, Loren Schnell has provided shelter to thousands of homeless people in the last 15 years. He strongly believes that Boulder needs both a crash pad for the disruptive people who aren't welcome anywhere else and a shelter for those who aren't streetwise.

He said he had given up trying to operate his organization PAD Inc., legally. In July 1981, the county closed down his pad on South Boulder Road - his fifth eviction in recent years - because of county zoning violations. But as long as there are people who need a floor to sleep on, Schnell is determined to provide one. (Camera, Nov. 7, Janet Wiscombe)

Beginning in December, the abandoned RTD bus depot at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard will be open to Boulder's homeless.

An employee trust group of Downing Leach and Associates, the local firm that owns the depot, agreed to allow the Task Force on Shelter and Food for the Poor to use the building for a winter shelter.

The task force will provide temporary, emergency shelter and one hot meal a day. The executive committee of the Boulder Council of Churches and Synagogues voted to sponsor the shelter. (Camera, Nov. 23, Janet Wiscombe)

A religious sect called the Circle of Friends Inc. has been ladling out generous portions of free stew to anyone who is hungry. They have set up a card table kitchen under the children's wing of the Boulder Public Library. The group operated two other soup kitchens in the area, but had to drop the service when they could not find support.

Rather than be saddled with all kinds of governmental food service regulations, they just call the free hot lunch program a "community picnic". (Camera, Nov. 17, Janet Wiscombe)

1983

### HOUSING

Housing sales prices controlled by Boulder's Moderate-Income Sales Program require a minimum annual income of \$23,500 to qualify in 1983.

The program, administered by the Housing Authority regulates the prices of a limited number of homes in every new subdivision to keep affordable housing on the local market.

In the past, the federal Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD) would permit buyers to qualify only if their mortgage payments were no more than 25 percent of their monthly income. Bowing to rising housing costs, HUD has agreed to raise the limit to 28 percent.

That means that the cheapest unit available under Boulder's program will be a 400-square-foot efficiency apartment at \$46,065. (Camera, Feb. 3, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder has more low-income housing and more programs to encourage

middle-income housing than any of its neighboring cities, yet Boulder is still the most expensive place in the county to live.

A major issue of debate for the council is whether the city has an obligation to provide not only shelter for its inhabitants, but also an opportunity for home ownership among the middle class. A survey conducted by the planning department indicated that Boulder's middle class is dwindling. (Camera, March 27, Daryl Gibson)

Because federal housing money has dried up, the city has limited means to increase the supply of affordable housing. Housing Authority Director John Hooyer said the city is exploring various methods for solving the problem including a closer look at "cooperative housing". (Colorado Daily, April 1-2, Kathleen Smith)

John Hooyer defended his record for placing minorities in city low-income housing. He referred to the recent controversy over the excess number of Asians living at the San Juan del Centro, a privately owned low-income complex.

Hooyer reported that 11 percent of the people living in city-owned family housing are Asian. Another 21 percent are Hispanic and 7.7 percent are Black. With all minority groups counted, their total occupancy in city family housing is 41 percent. (Camera, April 28, Daryl Gibson)

### HUMAN RELATIONS

Operators of Boulder's shelter for the homeless are turning away 10 to 20 persons a night. Boulder codes allow the abandoned bus depot to house only 24 persons.

Like its guests, the shelter has fallen upon hard economic times. For instance, December's heating bill exceeded \$1,300. (Denver Post, Feb. 16)

The county and city of Boulder and the state Division of Employment and Training will co-sponsor the Boulder Day Labor Office this summer. The city will lease a trailer for the office. The county owns the land where the trailer will be located and the state will pay two employees to staff the office. (Camera, May 9, Laura Turnbow)

A total of 226 people stayed at the shelter during its four-month existence. The average resident was a 30-year-old man, recently arrived in Boulder with less than \$10 in his pockets and no plans to find a home. (Camera, June 29, Margie McAllister)

Rats searching for leftovers have forced evacuation of the Circle of Friends lunch program under the west wing of the library.

The Boulder County Health Department told the group to close until it finds a licensed kitchen in which to serve its meals. The Circle has served 7,500 meals since it began the program in November 1982. (Camera, Dec. 10, Margie McAllister)

Boulder's "hidden poor" make up 16.3 percent of the city's population, or 11,353 people living at or below the federal poverty level. The figure does not include 8,000 students living in dormitories. (Camera, Dec. 14, Daryl Gibson)

The city council agreed to donate \$11,500 as start-up money to a non-profit corporation that has been formed to operate a day-care center at the Crossroads Mall.

The Boulder Urban Renewal Authority has agreed to put \$130,000 into the project for construction of the center in space donated by the MaceRich Co. which owns Crossroads. (Camera, June 8, Daryl Gibson)

Boulder's city-owned night club for teens opened as an experiment to give young adults a social alternative to alcohol and drugs. The club has been named "Up the Creek" and is in the remodeled Botanica flower shop at 1301 Arapahoe Ave.

Randy Gunnoe, director of Youth Services, used federal and state grants and private donations to complete the project. This year the club will get its salary money from the Youth Services division and the Boulder County Private Industry Partnership. (Camera, July 14, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Model Lease is not being used as much as the Boulder Tenants' Union would like. If voluntary usage does not increase soon, the group may pressure the city to make it mandatory. (Camera, March 14, Sharon Gillen)

Two controversial pro-tenant laws gained the approval of the Human Relations Commission. They favored an ordinance requiring landlords to enter into written leases if units are rented more than 30 days. Another proposal would permit landlords to enter their rental units only after giving tenants 24-hour notice. (Colorado Daily, Aug. 17-18, Kathleen Smith)

A law passed by the Boulder City Council two weeks ago giving tenants token protection against unwanted visits from their landlords, was tossed out by the council at their next session.

The council considered a law suggested by the Boulder Tenants' Union requiring landlords to give tenants 24-hour notice before entering a rental for a non-emergency. They approved a watered-down version which said landlords had to give "reasonable" notice. But "reasonable" was not defined.

City Attorney de Raismes brought the law back to the council because, as passed, it had no mechanism to prosecute landlords but only provided for

prosecution of tenants if they failed to let their landlords enter.

The new language led to a shift in the vote of a councilperson and to the defeat of the law. (Camera, Nov. 2, Daryl Gibson)

1984

## HOUSING

The San Juan del Centro low-income housing complex has been sold by St. John's Episcopal Church for \$4 million to the National Corporation for Housing Partnership, a group which buys hundreds of low-income housing projects for investors to use as tax shelters.

San Juan was built in 1971 under the sponsorship of St. John's with a loan from the federal Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD). It was the first low-income housing project in Boulder.

HUD does not allow sponsors of federally-funded housing projects to profit from a sale, but it does allow them to give the money to charities. A St. John's Foundation has been established with the money from the sale of San Juan. Its grants will go to Boulder County charities and HUD will have veto power over the allocation of the grants. (Camera, Jan. 8, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Housing Authority has set income limits and price ranges under Boulder's moderate-income housing program for 1984.

This year, for the first time, the range of prices set by the city varies with the interest rate charged by the builder. Local builders asked for flexibility to charge higher prices this year if they could offer lower interest rates. The price/income formula is geared to stay within the

mortgage limit of 28 percent of the buyer's income. (Camera, Jan. 28, Daryl Gibson)

A Task Force on Mobile Home Options was formed by the Boulder County Consortium of Cities. Committee members met for the first time and agreed there is plenty of demand for mobile home parks and subdivisions, and there are developers who would be anxious to create them. However, there is no land within the county's municipalities zoned for mobile homes. (Times-Call, Feb. 22, Steve McCoy)

City Councilmember Homer Page is president of the county's Mobile Home Task Force. He presented recommendations to the county commissioners asking that steps be taken to encourage the use of mobile homes as a low-income housing source. (Camera, June 20, Sally McGrath)

Members of the Boulder Housing Authority and Wonderland Hill Development Co. signed a contract to jointly build a \$7 million low- and middle-income housing project.

The 125-unit apartment project will be built on 12 acres owned by the authority in the Tantra subdivision south of Table Mesa Drive and west of the Boulder-Denver turnpike.

Each partner will put a maximum of \$800,000 into the project. The rest of the money will come from development revenue bonds issued using the tax-exempt interest status of county government.

The city's share will include the \$350,000 it paid for the land in 1980 and the rest from federal Community Development Block Grants.

Rents from the units are expected to pay back the bonds, pay for maintenance and operation of the complex and return a little profit to both partners.

The project will be operated as rental apartments for at least 10

years. After that the apartments can be sold as condominiums. City Housing Planner John Martinez said the city would use its share of the sale profits to buy the low-income apartments in the project and perpetually rent them as city-subsidized housing. (Camera, Feb. 2, Daryl Gibson)

The Housing Authority formed a limited partnership with private developers who agreed to build 20 low-income apartments and their own office building on land owned by the authority. (Camera, March 29, Brigid Kelly)

The partnership won Boulder a merit award from the U.S. Dept. of Housing and Urban Development. (Camera, July 5)

The Housing Authority agreed to buy 14 acres near Sixth Street and Locust Avenue for low- and medium-income housing. The land will be purchased from the city's Parks and Recreation Department. The \$260,000 purchase price will be paid from Community Development funds.

The council had approved buying 57 acres, part of which is designated as a park site, and 14 acres for housing. (Camera, March 30, Brigid Kelly)

John Hooyer is leaving his 15-year position as city housing coordinator and as executive secretary for the Housing Authority. He will work for the Christian Reform World Relief Committee and will serve as planning and development advisor to the refugee resettlement program of the Anglican Church of Uganda.

Since 1969, Hooyer has served as Boulder's first and only housing director. Back then no public housing existed in Boulder. Now 660 residential units for low-income and elderly residents have been built. Housing Authority assets, which also started at zero, now total \$12 million in land and buildings. Hooyer said the emphasis has been on scattering the

low-income residences throughout the city thus avoiding a high concentration of one economic level in one part of town. (Camera, March 27, Brigid Kelly)

John Martinez, who has served as acting head of the Boulder Housing Authority since April, has been nominated to the permanent position. (Camera, Oct. 20, Linda Cornett)

The mean sale price for the traditional single-family home in Boulder in 1981 was \$100,862, based on figures from the County Assessor's office. In 1983, between January and October, that figure increased almost 10 percent to \$110,848.

"Attached" homes, such as condominiums and townhouses, had a 1981 mean sale price in Boulder of \$71,052. This year, the figure dropped about 4.5 percent to \$67,896. (Camera, Business Plus, Jan. 3.)

The Boulder County Housing Monitor started publication this week. The quarterly newsletter's first issue lists data on existing single family homes sold in the county in 1983.

The Boulder Housing Authority will underwrite the cost of the publication for three years. (Camera, Nov. 8, Linda Cornett)

### HUMAN RELATIONS

Karen Thoreson, director of Boulder's Child Care Support Center, reported that the day-care operation at Crossroads may be paying its own way soon.

Thoreson went to the council in January asking for \$27,000 to make up the difference in revenues and expenses at the center. The council agreed to take the subsidy out of the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority budget.

(Camera, March 23, Daryl Gibson)

The San Juan Children's Learning Center has served Boulder's low-income, minority and non-English-speaking children for four years. It helps prepare them for success in public schools.

The city council voted to assist the center with \$23,400. (Denver Post, July 11, Barbralu C. Manning)

Last year's homeless shelter, the old RTD bus depot, was recently razed to make way for an office complex. The task force had searched without success all fall for another building. Owners of the Pleasure Chest, a massage parlor and adult bookstore, agreed to donate their building. Sponsored by the Boulder Council of Churches and Synagogues and helped by \$2,500 from the city, volunteers helped ready the building to meet city code requirements.

The shelter is open from 7 p.m. to 8:30 a.m. to both individuals and families. Twenty-six beds are available. (Colorado Daily, Jan. 16, Kathleen Smith)

The Boulder Emergency Center opened its doors again in November. Its lease expires at the end of March and the task force is trying to find a new location for the building which it owns. However zoning restrictions, lack of capital and inherent problems with potential neighbors all are obstacles. (Camera, Nov. 23, Bruce Langer)

For the past three weeks, the county's day labor office has been operated from the Emergency Shelter at 507 Canyon Blvd. Normally the jobs are handled through the county-run Private Industry Partnership, but that state-funded program is offered only through the summer. When this year's funding ran out, it seemed logical to make the connection between the labor

program and the emergency shelter. (Camera, Dec. 28, Linda Cornett)

A six-week study of the homeless conducted for the city's Community Development program and the Boulder County United Way resulted in a report presented to the city council. It gives options and suggestions for dealing with the homeless.

Non-profit groups and church organizations have spearheaded local shelter efforts. Echo House, run by the Emergency Family Assistance Association, offers 20 temporary beds for families, the elderly, the disabled, and pregnant women. When Echo House is full, Boulder County's Department of Social Services provides extra room at motels, campgrounds and hostels.

Boulder County's Safehouse for battered women, its Alcohol Recovery Center and Mental Health Center programs can handle a total of 80 distressed people.

What remains are those who have "fallen through the safety net" that Boulder offers the needy, Susan Purdy, the city's community development director, said.

The Council of Churches and Synagogues has asked the city for money to keep the emergency shelter open and to explore the idea of a year-round shelter. (Camera, March 14, Brighid Kelly)

Boulder mobile home parks cannot discriminate against prospective tenants who have children according to the Human Relations Commission. Although the ordinance passed by the city does not specifically mention mobile home parks, it was designed to ban discrimination in all Boulder housing. (Camera, March 6, Brighid Kelly)

An interim report issued on the city's Landlord/Tenant Mediation Project shows the first eight months of the operation to be worthwhile and

indicates a high success rate when mediation is used to resolve landlord/tenant disputes.

During the eight months between October 31, 1983 and June 22, 1984, 478 persons contacted the office of Human Rights with questions pertaining to rental property. Of those, 97 individuals requested mediation to help resolve a dispute. Mediation occurred in 37 percent of the 97 cases. (Camera, Sept. 6)

Since 1981, Boulder's Office of Human Rights has received about 2,000 inquiries about possible discrimination in employment, housing and public facilities.

Since the office was established in 1972, only 249 cases have been filed and formally investigated. The office, staffed by Kon Damas, is funded by the city's Department of Human Resources. It is charged with enforcement of the city's civil rights laws.

From 1981 to date, the office handled 30 cases of employment-related sexual discrimination. Of those, 12 cases were dropped as invalid complaints. The others were conciliated - a mediation process that takes about two months.

Damas believes that many cases go unreported because of a lack of awareness of the city's ordinance against discrimination. Efforts to promote public awareness are evident in a brochure published by the city in three languages - English, Spanish and Hmong.

"Ladies' nights" were outlawed in Boulder when the Human Rights Office declared that special discounts for women are illegal because they discriminate against men. (Camera, Jan. 20, Valerie Finholm)

A restaurant owner appealed the decision, and a public hearing was

held by the Human Relations Commission. The commission reaffirmed that discounts based on gender are outlawed in Boulder. (Camera, March 1, Valerie Finholm)

C.A.R.E.S (Continuing Assistance in Rehabilitation and Enrichment for Seniors) is an adult day-care program sponsored by the city. The program operates five days a week at the Boulder Senior Center.

Its purpose is to prevent or delay premature or unnecessary institutionalization, to provide respite for families caring for the dependent older person, and to improve the quality of life of frail, isolated or handicapped older persons. (Camera, Feb. 19)

Cay Graca, director of the housing/employment information service at the Senior Citizens' Center says that Boulder lacks many of the affordable housing alternatives such as shared and cooperative housing, adult foster care, group homes and mobile homes.

Boulder owns four public housing complexes for the elderly and the city's family complexes contain units for them. Waiting lists are one to two years.

Five private organizations in Boulder offer elderly housing. But those that are moderately priced also have one- to two-year waiting lists.

One alternative to help the aging maintain their independence is the "accessory" apartment allowed by city ordinance. However, in the ordinance's first year, only 38 applications were submitted. (Colorado Daily, March 7, J. E. Smith)

# CHAPTER VII

## PUBLIC SAFETY

1980

### OFFENSES

The Colorado State Supreme Court has ruled that the state's anti-obscenity statute is unconstitutional. (Camera, Aug. 19, Chance Connor)

When the News Stand opened for business in June 1977 only two blocks from Boulder High School, it was the worst thing that could have happened, according to some alarmed parents.

Boulder County District Attorney Alex Hunter said that, in light of the recent court ruling, until there is a new obscenity law on the books, no further action can be taken against the bookstore. Both Hunter and the principal of Boulder High School noted that the controversy seems to have died down. (Camera, Sept. 29, Jan McCoy)

### FIRE AND POLICE

A fire that did extensive damage to a Heatherwood home has again raised the issue of adequate fire protection in the Gunbarrel-Heatherwood area.

The 7,000 residents are served by Boulder Rural Fire Department, a volunteer organization near 28th Street and the Longmont Diagonal. Boulder Rural can call for help from the city of Boulder if they feel they are not able to control or if lives or other structures are in danger. The city fire station on Spine Road in Gunbarrel opened a year ago, but serves only the commercial and industrial areas which have been annexed to Boulder. (Camera, Jan. 23, Sue Deans)

A fire started by two people playing with fireworks destroyed a home and scorched 150 acres in the Pine Brook Hills subdivision. Since it started on greenbelt land, there was a "debriefing" meeting held among all firefighting units to determine the effectiveness of their communications and the responsibility for the costs of fighting the fire. (Camera, Oct. 10, Chance Connor)

On October 26, an arson fire at the historic Academy Center did an estimated \$1 million damage. (Camera, Nov. 8, Mike Stone)

City of Boulder firefighters accepted a 1981 contract with the city which promises them a seven percent raise in January and another two percent in July. (Camera, Aug. 27)

In a meeting between members of the University Hill League and Boulder police officials, complaints were aired on the burglaries and sexual assaults in the area, and on the parking habits of Hill dwellers. Chief Propst said that a plan was under way to revitalize the Neighborhood Watch program and stressed the importance of Operation ID for marking and tracing valuables. (Camera, Feb. 7, Michael Ross)

In the year since his arrival in Boulder, Police Chief Jay Propst has worked to integrate several ideas on crime prevention with the day-to-day operations of his force.

The number of officers working full time with prevention programs has been increased, more neighborhood "self-help" prevention projects are being encouraged and police "beats" have been restructured to foster greater contact between patrol officers and city residents.

One effort is the newly launched Neighborhood Crime Watch, a cooperative venture of the police department, the Boulder County District Attorney's Office and the Boulder Insurors Association. (Colorado Daily,

Feb. 25, Patrick Summers)

Chance Connor wrote in the Camera dated June 9 that:

The Boulder Police Department's newly formed Special Crime Attack Team was begun in mid-May to focus on heavy crime areas and help cover calls that department beat officers are not equipped to handle.

The unit, made up of four two-man teams, ... are highly skilled and highly motivated ... (and) highly mobilized.

Connor reported in the November 9 Camera:

Although (Boulder District Attorney Alex Hunter) feels Boulder's reputation as a midwestern drug clearinghouse is somewhat exaggerated because of wanton peddling and consumption in the late 1960s and early 1970s, he insists that the area does have a serious marijuana and cocaine problem. The most pragmatic way of chipping away at that sizeable problem is through education, he says. Stepping up police activity isn't the answer.

Still, the formation of the Boulder Drug Unit (and their) commitment to aggressively pursue drug dealers ... are encouraged by Hunter.

Lt. Bruce Goodman of the Boulder County Sheriff's Department (said) "The dealers we see here are brokers, in a sense. Many of them never even see the drugs. They simply invest and move it around ... . Narcotics in Boulder County is a business, not a pastime."

Boulder police arrested 52 juveniles they believe are responsible for serious crimes dating from 1978, including car thefts, burglaries and grand larceny. Most of the crimes were drug-related. (Camera, March 23, Jan McCoy and Mike Stone)

The Boulder Police Benefit Association ratified a new two-year contract with the city. It will provide city police with wage improvements totalling 21.5 percent over the next two years. (Camera, July 8)

Complaints from citizens and adverse publicity have led to a second local towing company getting a share of towing jobs for the police department.

For years, one wrecker service has done all the towing of cars for the city, the county, and the university law enforcement agencies. (Camera,

Oct. 15, Mike Stone)

Serious crime in Boulder increased by nine percent in 1980. The newly formed Boulder Drug Unit investigated 83 cases and 143 suspects. A total of 84 arrests were made for selling drugs to juveniles. Drugs with a street value at more than \$471,000 were confiscated. (Camera, Jan. 18, 1981, Chance Connor)

#### CORRECTIONS

Jane Cracraft reported in the Denver Post of Sept. 1:

Even if municipal offenders arrested for walking a dog on the mall or sleeping in a park weren't taken to the Boulder County Jail, the jail still would be crowded, according to Sheriff Brad Leach.

(He) noted that the Boulder County Long Range Planning Commission has recommended that the (county) commissioners spend \$40,000 to study ways of adding a second story onto the 100-bed jail.

Jail consultant Dave Voorhis noted that in 1971 planners estimated that Boulder County would need a 300-bed facility in 1980 and 600 beds in (the year) 2000. Voorhis said the commissioners decided then that it would be better to develop new programs, rather than constructing expensive jail space.

In the Times-Call dated November 17, Barry Noreen reported that Boulder County will spend \$15,000 in consulting fees to update the 9-year-old HOK (Hellmuth, Obata and Kassabaum) report which had recommended that the jail facilities include housing for alcoholics and the mentally ill. " ... budgetary considerations precluded those additions and part of the overcrowding problem now is caused by the very inmates the HOK report said should be incarcerated separately."

### FLOODS

In the April 2 issue of the Camera, Daryl Gibson reported:

An engineering firm that once told the City Council it could keep the Crossroads redevelopment area out of a major flood plain for \$3 million now claims the cost is closer to \$5.5 million.

Public Works Director Andy Hollar ... said he has dismissed the URS (United Research Systems) Company from the Crossroads project and is looking for another engineer to do the work.

The Boulder Urban Renewal Authority ... set aside \$1.6 million for the projects (the original cost estimate). The money was to come from the authority budget and the Urban Drainage District.

The cost was boosted to \$3 million ... when the city was forced to add channel improvements to protect the Sherrelwood condominium project from flooding.

Gingery and Associates was hired to replace URS and was told it had \$3 million to work with.

Still at issue is whether the ... (flood control) improvements will wipe out homes on Cordry Court across the creek from the Sherrelwood development. (Camera, May 22, Daryl Gibson)

Design problems and a lack of money from the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority have delayed flood control improvements for the Crossroads Mall by two months. The delay may make it unrealistic to start construction this season. (Camera, June 3, Daryl Gibson)

Gingery turned in a preliminary cost estimate in July and a final price tag of \$3,734,000 (not including right-of-way purchase) in September. "Although the work has been delayed because of a switch in engineers and the need to redesign, city officials are confident the flood channel will be ready by 1982 when Crossroads mall construction reaches the point that it must be protected from creek flooding." (Camera, Sept. 30, Daryl Gibson)

In November, the city briefed citizens on the plan for saving Crossroads Mall and other property from Boulder Creek floods.

Although the price of the plan is up to \$5 million - and nearly \$2 million over what was budgeted - the citizens let the city know they would be willing to pay more money for a better plan.

Specifically, many attending ... demanded "softer" measures than the channeling, concrete walls and berms proposed ... . (Camera, Nov. 14, Daryl Gibson and Jeff Schier)

Daryl Gibson reported in the Camera of November 23:

The Boulder Creek flood plain may not be as wide as city officials have assumed since 1977.

The U.S. Army Corps of Engineers drew a flood plain map for Boulder Creek in 1977. It is the map on which the Federal Insurance Administration bases requirements for flood insurance on property.

... the city hired Muller Engineering ... to check the (Cor

figures.

What the company discovered ... was that the Corps study was technically correct, but not sophisticated enough to account for several sharp rises in the topography.

The new map ... analyzed only the creek from Folsom Avenue to 38th Street. It draws the flood line just north of Arapahoe Avenue ... . (Camera, Nov. 23, Daryl Gibson)

Large local businesses pay more than residents for flood control costs, but reap a large share of the benefits. Crossroads Shopping Center pays over \$1,000 a year in flood control fees. The Harvest House Hotel also pays over \$1,000 a year. The University of Colorado pays \$31,952 a year.

The flood control utility will operate on a \$900,000 budget this year. About \$400,000 comes from outside sources, with the rest from the flood control assessment paid by all city utility customers. (Camera, Jan. 21, Sue Deans)

### WEATHER

January was the snowiest since 1948. The temperature for four days at the end of the month did not set records but had not stayed below 10 degrees for so long for at least 50 years. (Camera, Jan. 30, Todd Malmsbury)

In October, a tornado ripped off part of the roof at the Vocational Technical Center and a funnel cloud was seen southwest of Boulder.

Meteorologists say that tornadoes are rare near the Front Range and usually move in a northeasterly direction. The one on Wednesday the 15th was not only unusually close to the foothills but moved northwest. (Camera, Oct. 19, Todd Malmsbury)

### CIVIL DEFENSE

Mary Belcher reported in the March 23 Camera:

Defense Department planners have tagged Boulder a "high risk" area. That means that if the United States were attacked in a nuclear war, chances are a bomb would be dropped close enough to the city that residents would have to be protected from radioactive fallout.

Eighty percent of Boulder's fallout shelters are located on the CU (Colorado University) campus, with room for nearly 100,000 people.

Boulder residents would be alerted to head for the nearest shelters by an "attack warning signal" emanating from a siren atop the Colorado Building at 14th and Walnut streets.

The Boulder Emergency Preparedness Office operates on a yearly budget of approximately \$60,000 with the federal government footing half the bill, the city and the county the remainder.

There are plenty of plans, but according to Lon Callen, coordinator of the Boulder Emergency Preparedness Office, "the whole shelter program is in pretty bad shape."

All that remains of edible food stock in the CU shelters are lemon drops . . . .

If there were ample warning, people would be encouraged to bring their own food and supplies to the shelters . . . .

People who live or work within a ten-mile radius of the Rocky Flats nuclear weapons plant will receive a one-page brochure explaining the steps to be taken should an accident at the facility release deadly amounts of radiation into the atmosphere.

Should an accident occur, the public would be notified through the media, public safety vehicles with loudspeakers and a steady tone on Civil Defense sirens. (Colorado Daily, Dec. 2, Patrick Summers)

### EMERGENCY MEDICAL SERVICE

In the fall of 1980, the city ambulance contract was up for bids for the first time in ten years. Under the current agreement, A-1 Ambulance Company is allowed to use a city-owned building at 1720 13th Street and is paid \$14,700 per year to subsidize its dry runs, calls in which no patient is transported. The city legally cannot hand out an exclusive franchise to one company and ban all others, but it can contract for exclusive rights to

respond to police and fire calls. (Camera, Oct. 17, Daryl Gibson)

A-1 Ambulance was the only company to bid on a new five-year contract offering a maximum subsidy of \$15,000. Company owner, Bob Wood, refused to sign the contract saying he could not survive financially under the terms proposed. His failure to agree to the pact may mean the city will de-regulate its ambulance services. (Camera, Dec. 2 and Dec. 15, Daryl Gibson)

---

1981

#### OFFENSES

The Boulder Police Department has formed a specialized traffic unit and from January to April the number of traffic tickets issued has increased by 84 percent over the same period in 1980. (Camera, June 7)

### FIRE AND POLICE

An arson fire destroyed Grandma's Bar on the east side of 30th Street. Police and fire officers had complained to the owner that the building was a fire and health hazard. The building had been vacant for several years and had been used frequently as a "crash pad" by Boulder transients. (Camera, Jan. 5, Chance Connor)

The Eco-Cycle center at 5030 Pearl Street was fire-bombed. The damage forced a two-day shut-down of the recycling center and cost the company approximately \$6,000 in lost time and damaged equipment. (Camera, Feb. 16)

Boulder firefighters accepted a two-year contract giving them an 8.5 percent salary increase in 1982 and an 8.25 raise in 1983.

In a separate issue, the pension committee has sued the city for failure to grant regular increases in payments to retired firefighters.

(Camera, Sept. 4, Daryl Gibson)

The city manager and the police chief took issue with a Chicago Tribune reporter's column that stated that Boulder's "entire culture revolves around cocaine". Chief Propst acknowledged that cocaine is the prevailing drug here but said that Boulder is not the drug capital of the world. (Camera, Oct. 31, Chance Connor)

The city council rejected an appeal by Councilmember George Boland to increase the number of police officers in the city. They agreed to restore three officers that the city manager had recommended be cut because of budget constraints. (Camera, Oct. 5, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Police Department has six volunteers working at jobs that otherwise might not be done. One, for example, follows up with burglary-theft victims to advise them about the case, suggests where they might look for stolen goods, and gives them ideas about tightening up their home security. (Denver Post, Oct. 14, Barbralu C. Manning)

Serious crimes declined in Boulder by six percent during 1981. Chief Propst said that 21 arrests and the recovery of more than \$15,000 in property came as a result of citizen participation in the Neighborhood Watch Program. (Camera, Jan. 21, 1982, Chance Connor)

#### CORRECTIONS

County Sheriff Brad Leach and the Criminal Justice Advisory Committee proposed to the county commissioners that a \$45 fee be imposed for municipal offenders. The proposal is aimed at reducing the number of offenders Boulder and other city's police bring to the jail following arrests for municipal violations - open containers, disorderly conduct,

etc. Many of the offenders are "street people".

While expansion of the jail continues to be considered as a long-term solution to the overcrowding problem, Police Chief Propst offered several short-term options. (Camera, June 1, Chance Connor)

### FLOODS

The Federal Insurance Administration has accepted a new map of the Boulder Creek flood plain which substantially reduces the acreage that would be threatened by a 100-year flood.

The new map will allow the city to replan flood control measures to protect the Crossroads Mall. The Boulder Urban Renewal Authority and the city originally budgeted \$3 million for the project, but the cost escalated to about \$5 million. The cost may be reduced with the new flood map, but it could be increased if the city council decides to add amenities to "soften" a flood channel or turn the whole creek into a linear park. (Camera, March 31, Daryl Gibson)

At an informal council meeting, City Manager Bob Westdyke proposed that a major change be made to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. He suggested the "non-structural" flood control approach in light of public opposition to enlarging the capacity of the creek

The council agreed to plan a "scenic corridor" along the creek from the mouth of Boulder Canyon to 55th Street. (Camera, June 10, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder Urban Renewal Authority agreed to allocate up to \$10,000 to develop preliminary designs for an on-site flood control plan for the Crossroads mall. The new plan would include landscaping, berms and special grading and could cost \$1 million rather than the \$3 to \$5 million that was first estimated when the original flood plan included extensive

improvements along Boulder Creek. (Camera, Sept. 24, Jan McCoy)

### WEATHER

Last winter, an all-time snowfall record of 145 inches was set. This winter, snowfall totaled only one-third of that. (Camera, May 2)

### EMERGENCY MEDICAL SERVICE

The A-1 Ambulance Company moved out of the city-owned building it occupied for four years and into new quarters at 1730 14th Street.

The move follows the failure of the city council and A-1 to sign a new contract. The city wanted to be able to review A-1's service rates and allow them to be raised only once each year.

A-1 will now operate without a contract or the subsidy formerly collected from the city for "911" calls. (Camera, Feb. 10, Daryl Gibson)

1982

### FIRE AND POLICE

Two Boulder firefighters were killed on January 26 in a fire which exploded out of control during training exercises in an abandoned north Boulder garage. The deaths marked the first on-the-job fatalities for the Boulder Fire Department in nearly 50 years. (Camera, Jan. 27, Daryl Gibson)

A fire expert hired by the city to investigate the tragedy placed the blame on the low-density fiberboard ceiling which burned eight times faster than ordinary wood. The heat buildup from tires and oil burning for a smoke drill apparently ignited the ceiling. (Camera, Feb. 9, Daryl Gibson)

An assistant fire chief and a battalion chief were placed on administrative leave pending the outcome of an investigation of the

training fire. Assistant City Manager James Piper and Fire Chief Charles Boyes will conduct interviews with fire department personnel.

Members of the Boulder firefighters union voted no confidence in the fire chief and the fire department administration claiming a lack of leadership or direction for the adequate and safe operation of the department. (Camera, March 15, Rusty Pierce)

Three firefighters were dismissed by Fire Chief Chuck Boyes for their part in the January 26 training fire which killed two firemen. (Camera, March 27, Daryl Gibson)

One dismissed firefighter filed a grievance with the city asking that Personnel Director Mike Patton overturn the decision to fire him. (Camera, April 2, Daryl Gibson)

The Boulder firefighters union voted not to accept the dismissed assistant chief back into its ranks to finish the three years he needs to retire from the department. The fire chief wanted to demote the man to lieutenant, but the contract with the union forbids demotions of more than one grade. The issue on which union members voted was whether to waive that section of the contract. (Camera, May 4, Daryl Gibson)

After long hours of negotiation, a settlement was reached by the firefighters union, the city and two firemen who lost their jobs because of their supervisory duties in the accident that killed the two firemen.

In the settlement, they were rehired at what amounts to a one-step demotion for each. The settlements included clauses absolving each man of blame for the accident. (Camera, May 19, Daryl Gibson)

In a short memorial service, Boulder Fire Station No. 3 in Scott Carpenter Park was dedicated to the memory of the two firefighters who were the casualties of the training fire. (Camera, Nov. 7, Daryl Gibson)

The first preventable civilian fire deaths in 18 years occurred. The two victims could have escaped had a smoke detector been working. (Camera, May 5, Rusty Pierce)

An arsonist burned the concession stand at Boulder Reservoir causing an estimated \$10,000 damage. (Camera, Sept.15)

Fire Station Number 6 at 5145 N. 63rd Street has turned off its solar system and is using conventional methods to heat the building and its water. The \$35,000 system, built with an Economic Development Administration grant, has never worked properly and has not been cost-efficient. (Camera, April 29, Jerrold White)

George Blackmer retired in August after 30 years of service with the Boulder Fire Department. (The Blaze, Vol. 1, No. 3, Dec. 16, 1982)

The mother of a man shot and killed on June 23 at Bennigan's Tavern in Boulder came to town to help promote a countywide Boulder Valley Crime Stoppers Program. The informant program maintains a fund to pay citizens who call the police anonymously and provide information on crimes. (Camera, Oct. 1, Betsy Howard)

A 3-year-old Boulder boy is missing and his 31-year-old mother is in Boulder County Jail without bond, defying a court order to reveal the location of the child. Michael Manning was last seen on the day after Christmas. (Camera, Dec. 31, Martin Connolly)

A Boulder police officer was awarded \$5,000 in a racial discrimination suit he filed against the city in 1977. Mack Thomas claimed that the Police Department would not promote him and treated him unfairly. In addition to the cash settlement, Thomas was awarded \$25,000 to help pay for

his legal expenses. As part of the settlement, the city will conduct a study of its affirmative action policies and its police officers will undergo sensitivity training. (Camera, Nov. 23, Betsy Howard)

The police department is organizing a Crisis Management Team to handle hostage situations and other crises. All its members work in other jobs in the department and are called only when needed. (Camera, Dec. 29, Betsy Howard)

### FLOODS

When the city council set up a flood control utility in 1973, the plan was to charge city residents monthly fees for 12 years, raise \$22 million and save the city from catastrophic flooding.

However, in eight years, the utility has raised a little more than \$3 million and there are no plans to cancel the program in 1986.

Part of the failure to generate more money was the drying up of federal aid for flood control projects. The council has been reluctant to go into debt for flood channel work.

Deputy City Manager Dave Knapp compiled a summary of flood control budgets for the past eight years. It shows an average of \$400,000 in fees collected annually, and five years in which the city had to kick in extra tax money from its general fund.

In late 1981, the council raised flood control fees 40 percent so that no more general fund money would be used as a supplement.

A hefty share has been spent on channel right-of-way, on day-to-day maintenance of channels by a five-member crew, and on an early warning system to alert people in the Boulder Creek floodway. (Camera, March 11, Daryl Gibson)

### WEATHER

On January 17, one of the worst windstorms in Boulder's history, with gusts to 137 mph caused damage estimated at \$17 million. (Camera, April 4, Rusty Pierce)

The following Sunday, winds gusting to more than 100 mph did further damage. (Camera, Jan. 25, Todd Malmsbury, Rusty Pierce and Michael Ross)

The County Human Resources building on the corner of 28th and Spruce streets, heavily damaged in the winds of January 17, will probably not be rebuilt. (Colorado Daily, Jan. 26, Marty Durlin)

The Uniform Building Code is a system of rules used nationally to prescribe minimal standards for construction. In Boulder, this code has been especially amended so that local houses will be able to withstand the winds along the foothills. (Camera, Feb. 27)

1983

### OFFENSES

A new fee schedule for police response to burglar false alarms will go into effect July 7. The new ordinance is not intended to penalize victims of actual crimes, but rather to make alarm users manage their systems more effectively. (Camera, June 19)

Businesses will be allowed only two false alarms and will face fines ranging from \$15 for the third alarm to \$150 for the tenth. According to statistics, false alarms accounted for almost one-third of all the city's emergency calls in 1982 and have been growing by 16 percent a year since

1980. (Camera, June 30, Betsy Howard)

### FIRE AND POLICE

A fire of unknown origin caused more than \$100,000 damage to a furniture store in Arapahoe Village shopping center. (Camera, Jan. 27, Martin Connolly)

Arson is suspected as the cause of a fire at the Eco-Cycle facility. There was an estimated \$10,000 damage to two buses and a flatbed truck. (Camera, Feb. 14, Michael Ross)

Arson is being investigated as a possible cause of a fire at an adult bookstore. The fire caused an estimated \$100,000 in damage. (Camera, Feb. 19, Betsy Howard)

A fire severely damaged a sporting goods store at 1622 Broadway. In addition to about \$30,000 damage to the building, the business lost over \$350,000 worth of merchandise. (Camera, Nov. 8, Kevin O'Brien and Lou Chapman)

Four women in the Boulder Fire Department have been promoted to the rank of engineer. In 1982, Boulder had five percent of the paid women firefighters in the country in its ranks. June 1978 was the first time the city hired women firefighters. (Camera, Feb 18)

A training officer has been hired to be Boulder's first fire drillmaster - a position added to the fire department after the fatal January 1982 training fire. The drillmaster's first job will be to put together an academy for new firefighters. He will also develop a certification program for engineers and lieutenants to train them for supervisory responsibilities. (Camera, July 29)

The Boulder Fire Department opened the doors to its new Fire Academy

on December 5. Five new recruits will receive the first formal training program offered to new firefighters in Boulder. (The Blaze, Vol. 2, No. 12, Dec. 15)

The council voted to give firefighters a 6.5 percent pay raise next year.
